THE RED PAGE
THE POETRY Of MATTER
IHAYE been reading some of the new
poets —the strange new poets of to-day.
Their names may possibly prove un-
familiar to the literary scholar ; for
C. W. Saleeby, Carl Snyder, R. K. Duncan,
Sir Oliver Lodge, John B. Burke, Felix Le
Dantec, you will search literary text-books
in vain.*
Poetry ? you query. Yes, the poetry of
that new world of wonder upon whose thresh-
hold f the twentieth century stands. For,
within the last seven years, the human' race
has Secured a foothold in its evolution com-
parable only to the ground gained when the
first ape stood erect, or when the first man
became self-conscious and knew that he
existed. For, in the phrase of one of our
new poets, “the universe has become self-
conscious in the mind of man, and has '
acquired the awful power of influencing its
own destinies.” With the quick filtration
of this new knowledge into the human brain
we may expect an expansion of human
activities, a quickening of the imagination’s
pulse, before which the awakening of Europe
by the discovery of America becomes a feeble
memory. This decade has seen the dis-
covery, not of a continent, but of a universe.
The human playground, which has been
shrinking, shrinking at each onset of science,
until man found himself confined to the
limited surface of a bullet flying through
space, has been suddenly enlarged in every
direction toward infinity. It is as if we
were emerging into a fourth dimension in
space.
These doubtless seem big words. Read
the new poets. Yet these writers, these
tellers of the story of man’s advance toward
the conquest of the unknowable, are but
pedestrian poets who blaze the track that
the inevitable great poet of the near future
will follow. The sudden exaltation felt by
the Elizabethians under the swift onrush of
new possibilities produced Shakespeare, the
crest of that stupendous wave of belief and
hope. This discovery of a new universe
should produce a new and more wonderful
Shakespeare who, stirred and uplifted by
the infinitely richer future upon whose thresh-
hold we now stand, will synthesise and -em-
body the newly divined splendor of the
human heritage.
For the world into which these new poets
lead us is a world of dreams that seem on the
point of coming true. For the first time in
his evolution, man is consciously stepping
out of the dark, no longer merely guessing
at the riddle of existence, but provided with
instruments to probe, and measure, and
weigh. The importance of this new develop-
ment cannot be under-estimated ; for it is a
foothold that cannot be lost. Civilisations as
great as ours have risen and decayed, and
history has been but the ebbing and flowing
of a tide upon the shores of Time ; but now
consciously, surely, man has made one abid-
ing place secure upon the firm earth of
science. Future historians will mark 1900,
a. i)., as the beginning of science—-a date as
momentous as that when the giant sea-tilings
from the steaming ocean dragged their un-
wieldiy bulks up to the marshy shores, and
made man possible through the reptile and
the ape.
And what is the reason for this absurd con-
fidence ? These new poets, termed by the liter-
ary critic scientific writers, sum it up in one
phrase—the invention and use of mechanical
appliances. These mechanical aids, such as
the galvanometer, the lens, the dynamo, the
spectroscope, the voltaic pile, are the pillars
of modern science. As Carl Snyder, who is
a mere scientific journalist, less journalistic
than C. W. Saleeby, less scientific than R,
K. Duncan, observes
The whole extension of our knowledge beyond
that of the savage depends upon the means of
supplementing artificially our primitive senses.
. . . Our native senses give us but a slight notion
of the’real world about us ; they are crude, coarse,
inaccurate, unreliable, prone to delude. For the
upbuilding of the vast body of verified and unified
experience and experiment which we’call science,
there was required the invention of tools. ... It
is the acquirement and possession of these that
alone differentiates us from the old days. With-
out them . . .we should be speculating, dreaming
still. . .. Mechanics is the foundation upon which
the whole superstructure of science rests.
That is, man has not only improved his
coarse senses, but has actually added to his
primitive endowment of seven senses a whole
series of new senses. For the world that
science is now beginning to explore is essen-
tially a world beyond our senses, a world
unseen, unheard, unfelt. A flash from that
supra-sensual world came to us with the dis-
covery of the Rontgen rays. Our actual
glimpse of the visible universe is through a
narrow peephole or slit, on both sides of
which extends an impenetrable spectrum-
band stretching infinitely. Now science is
swiftly widening our window, and a new and
blinding light streams in. By means of
our new weapons we have added to our
senses an electric and a magnetic sense that
we were born without. In the camera we
have- developed an artificial eye that never
tires, that recognises things beyond our
world of vision. We have built ourselves
new fingers, new retinas, new ears. We
have succeeded in detecting, locating, map-
ping, measuring and weighing vast objects
that affect no human sense. The whole
trend of this new knowledge is in the direc-
tion of unity. The universe, while being a
marvellously complex thing, is beginning to
be recognised as inherently simple. The
evidence of the spectroscope seems con-
clusive that the material of the universe is
the same throughout, and that this material
is dominated by the same forces and the
same laws. The universe is possibly finite.
“There is no decisive reason to believe,”
remarks Carl Snyder, “that the number of
the stars is without end. ” Of course, there
remains the old query. If it is finite, some-
where out in space, you would come to the
end of mattter . . . and beyond that, what ?
A scientist once humorously suggested that
there would probably be a big brick wall;
and, further questioned as to what would
be found if one looked over the brick wall,
he logically opined that there wou|d be only
a collection of broken bottles.
J*'
And if space be finite, towards which
conclusion science seems to be reaching, it
can be measured and weighed . . . and domi-
nated ? For conceive the enormous store of
energy that within the last decade has been
proved to be imprisoned within the atom ;
compared with the energy that binds to-
gether the molecules, or in action when a
pinch of salt is dissolved in a glass of water,
gravity—the weakest of known forces—is
almost inconceivably feeble.
And if once we can unlock from the stable
atoms the stored energy which we see to-day
leaking from the atom of radium, what
changes would be wrought in this world !
Professor Thomson, as a result of his calcu-
lations, concludes that a gram of hydrogen
has within it energy sufficient to lift a million
tons ope hundred yards. The trouble is
that, while we know of the existence of this
energy, we can at present only observe, not
unlock it.
We have no real warranty (remarks R. K.
Duncan) that this infinity of energy will be tapped
by man, except this; That what man earnestly
longs for, lie will obtain. If he knows that every
breath he draws has contained within itself power
enough to drive the workshops of the world, he will
find out some day, some how, some way of tapping
that energy.
The new poets tell us the absorbing story
of that search. It has been playfully sug-
gested by Prof. Rutherford (the brilliant
Maorilander whom Maoriland could not
afford to keep at home) that some day it
might be possible to construct a detonator
which wouid send a wave of atomic disin-
tegration through the earth and decompose
the whole round world into helium, argon,
and other gases, leaving literally not a
wrack behind. And if that detonator were
to work, it would be hard to confine its
destructive power to the earth. What is to
prevent a scientific be-spectacled anarchist
of the future, having dined well and seeing
the futility of further human effort, from
lounging into his laboratory and quietly
turning a small brass key in his new cosmos-
detonator, and wiping out the universe ? The
idea has literary possibilities. It would
cause no little surprise and annoyance in
Paradise.
In addition to the probable finiteness of
the universe, and the assured homogeneity
of the universe, we can now say that it is
probable there is a fixed limit of speed for
all its mechanism. Light, heat, electricity,
X-rays—all the motions attributed to the
ether—clip through space at 184,000 miles
per second—no more, no less. No matter
whether the wave is a thousand feet
or a sixty-thousandth of an inch in length,
its rate of speed is exactly the same.
The speed of thought, it is curious to find, is
very much slower. But then thought is not
an ether-vibration. So the cosmos begins to
take on the similitude of a machine, the
parts qf which cannot work beyond a certain
limit of speed, possibly only with that
speed. In the opposite direction the
new poets tell us that their heroes,
those dim and silent figures lost in
the recekses of their laboratories —one
of the most famous of them all,
M. Berthelot, the founder of synthetic
chemistry, out of whose discoveries millions
have been made by the commercial world,
died the other month, and there were no
headlines in the papers—these heroes have
got near to the limits of the size of matter.
Matter is not infinitely divisible, but granu-
lar in structure. ‘ ‘ Matter is no jelly, but
is made up of grains,” declares R K.
Duncan, in his remarkable work, The New
Knowledge, which in a short space of time
has run into its fifth edition. We have got
down to primal matter—the ultimate units,
the ions or electrons, from which the
universe is made. But the physicists
have weighed the electron, measured its
velocity, and defined its properties. And
all its properties are electric. At first
they said, “It carries a charge of electricity ”
—and, curiously, always the same charge is
carried by a,n atom as by a cathode particle,
though one may be 100,000 times the size of
the other. Then they had to say, “It is a
charge of electricity.” But electricity is not
a material thing, but a form of energy. So
an electron, the brick out of which you and
the nebula; are built, is merely an atom of
energy. Electricity is evidently an invariable
property of matter ... or, if you will,
matter is electricity. As It. K, Duncan
proves:—
Matter has disappeared as.a fundamental exist-
ence ; it is explained as a manifestation of elec-
tricity. Mass has disappeared with matter, and
depends solely upon the velocity with which the
negative electron moves. ... As there is no nerve
action without the evident presence of electrifica-
tion, it is possible that we shall some day find that
nerve action, thought and consciousness, and what
in our present ignorance we call electricity, are
one and the same.
We shall see, when we consider Burke’s
work, to what these conclusions logically
lead.
&
But the main characteristic of the new
knowledge is the unification of all the differ-
ent sciences. The same problem of the con-
stitution of. the atom is being attacked by
the chemist and the biologist. Modern
science consists in closing the gaps be-
tween the sciences, in bridging over the
apparent chasm between the organic
and the inorganic, the living and the
dead. It is on these border-lines that
the scientists are groping—the border-
lines of life and the beginnings of
evolution. For this grand conception
is now established, that from the primal
bricks the universe has been built up
through the inorganic, the “dead,” to the
organic, the “living,” till it has reached
man, the self-conscious. Darwin traced the
evolution of the species ; the new poets tell
us of the evolution of the universe. It is a
world of miracles into which these new
poets lead us. They tell us of an excised
heart that can be kept beating for hours,
stopped, started, quickened, or slowed,
simply by slightly changing the chemical
character of the salt solution in which it is
placed. Professor Loeb and Professor J. B.
Burke are the chief of these modern miracle-
workers who play with life. For life itself is
a subject for analysis and explanation. All
..these works are occupied with the question,
“What is Life?” All are groping toward
a definition of Life—for if science defines a
thing it has made the first stride toward
capturing it. Le Dantec, in his extremely
technical book, tries his hand. As thus :
Life is only a surface accident in the history of
the thermic evolution of the globe. ... The whole
history of life is bound up with that of liquid
water; life is an aquati® phenomenon. .. . Life is
a succession of functions; the living being is a
succession of organs. . . .
Elsewhere life has been definitely labelled
“a series of ferments.” Pasteur thought
that the ferment went on because the
organism was alive, not that the ferment was
itself life. Buchner smashed that theory by
smashing yeast- cd&s by enormous pressure,
and from the sap, or liquor, which flowed
from the pulp he produced exactly the same
fermentive action as that of the yeast-cells.
Obviously, fermentation is due, not to the
cells, but to the chemical substance which
could be extracted from them. So life is a
ferment.
And what hope have we that ultimately
we will understand, and therefore control,
this marvellous universe into which we are
just now being beckoned ? The answer is,
Time. The human race, it has been cal-
culated, has an enormous age-limit before it
—probably three million years. Organised
knowledge is only three hundred years old.
Roughly speaking, man is now only a baby
one year old ; the chances are that he will
live to be a centenarian.
The New Knowledge remains the most re-
markable and explicit of all the new books of
the poetry of matter. It is, indeed, a tine
achievement to construct from the tentative
steps made by man within the last decade a
theory of the universe which seems destined
to stand—until a more satisfactory one is
enunciated—which is just as long as a scien-
tific theory can hope to exist. Here
the evidence for inorganic evolution is
plainly and convincingly stated ; and
the sweep and arrangement of the work
is admirable. It. K. Duncan is easily the
greatest of the new poets. Local booksellers
state that the sale of works such as those
enumerated is increasing to a remarkable
degree ; the man of the future, as Wells
suggested, is a man less interested in litera-
ture than in knowledge. The Australian,
too, means to know. The New Knowledge
gives a full account of the apparently simple
experiments by Prof. J. J. Thomson, with
tiny magnetised needles thrust through
disks of cork 'floating in water, from which
child’s pastime Thomson has furnished a re-
markable explanation of the arrangements
of the corpuscles within the atom—a sugges-
tion that, it is not too much to say, approxi-
mately explains the composition of matter.
In other words, Thomson explains chemical
affinity on electrical and mechanical models.
Chemical affinity is electrical affinity.
Chemical action means simply the power of
an atom to lose or acquire corpuscles, and
that power depends absolutely upon the
arrangement of the corpuscles within it,
which in its turn depends upon the number
of corpuscles the atom contains.
Professor Burke is not only a teller of
miracles ; he is a maker of them. A few
years ago he produced certain infinitesimal
things in an insignificant test-tube, and from
them constructed a - theory of the origin of
life. For in these radiobes, which were
generated in sterilised bouillon by radium—
both “dead” things—he has created a
species of life. He does not claim that he
has made living from absolutely non-living
matter, for in the new definition of life all
matter is living, but that
We have arrived at a method of structural
organic synthesis of artificial cells, which, if it
does not give us natural organic life such as we
see existing around us, gives us at least something
which admits of being placed in the gap, or, as it
might preferably be called, the borderland
between living and dead matter.
Burke proves the continuity of vital pro-
cesses—that the links between the organic and
the inorganic, between “living” and “dead”
matter, form an unbroken and continuous
chain. His radiobes are really a long-vanished
type of living matter, the unstable gropings
of Nature’s first experimental essays at life
—poor, fragile groups of atoms that could
not survive. From some more stable forms
the bacillus has at length been developed.
So we see the principle of the survival of
the fittest applies even to the atoms.
And what is this life, after all ? Burke,
like the other poets, suggests it is merely
mind—that matter is mind-stuff.
Life, in its ultimate form, would appear to be
the consequence of an aggregation of aetherial
units or electrons, the units of electricity, of which
all matter, too, is ultimately composed. . . . Life
is a property of matter as much as radio-activity
or electricity—or life and matter are merely dif-
ferent phenomena of electricity. The ultimate
vital substance corresponds to a state of
matter in the process of being—neither
molecular or atomic—a nebula of electrons
not yet condensed into a regular system. . ...
Matter, or the electrons of which matter is
composed, is mind-stuff. The atoms are nothing
more than ideas. . . . The great universe into
which we shall ultimately, consciously or un-
consciously, be resolved, is mind, and nothing
more or less than mind.
So this patient investigator, in his labora-
tory, weighing, testing, experimenting with
his fragile little radiobes, comes back at last
to the domains of metaphysics. Matter is
no more than a transient expression of a
transient electrical relation. Atoms are
electricity, and electricity is mind.
But having found out all about it, what
use is it anyway to us, poor ephemerides
upon a freezing buck-shot in space ? If the
universe is only a clock wound up
by the Creator, and slowly, but inevit-
ably, running down, there must come
the End. This theory, generally held a
decade ago, is going the way of other
materialistic theories. The new science has
discovered that some of the stars are growing
hotter, and that the earth is self -heating.
Rutherford shows that the radio-activity of
ordinary substances is in the right order of
magnitude to balance the loss of the earth’s
heat into space. But the great question is
yet unsettled, though the trend of scientific
belief is towards a recurrent rhythm of
existence, from nebula to nebula —a clock
that eternally winds itself.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THIS BULLETIN.]
The Meeting.
See yon fair lady ! Mark her queenly tread
As gracefully she moves along the street.
Note with what dignity she lifts her head
And passes on. Not thus we used to meet.
I wonder do her thoughts sometimes depict
The time, when, like a dream, she crossed my
life —
Ere I became a social derelict.
And she became a money-lender’s wife?
Take care, fair lady, lest your silken train
Should touch the skirts of that more humble
she
Who walks beside you ! They may still retain
Some faint aroma from the factory
Or shop, wherein she toils for daily bread.
She’s a mere woman, clothed in hard-earned
rags.
You are a lady, who preferred instead
To deck yourself with tainted money bags.
I knew you both, when each was young and fair,
And I was not so ugly, nor so old.
She gave her life for love—his life to share.
You flouted love, and sold yourself for gold.
Now, widowed, she must work for daily bread.
She dresses shabbily ; her cheeks are pale,
While you can wear a fortune on your head, .
Filched from some wretch who gave a bill-of-
sale.
Yet, when her weary eyelids close in rest,
She lives again the spring-time of her youth.
Her soul seeks his, among the ever-blessed.
And wakes, refreshed, to innocence and truth.
What are your dreams, when tossing restlessly
Beside the Bloated One to whom you’re sold ?
Ak<s 'ithey raf Ibnen whose grinding poverty
Has splashed with blood your foul, ill-gotten
gold
Do shades of greasy, hook-nosed bailiffs seek
To steal your gew-gaws as you lie asleep ?
Do famished wives, and babes with pallid cheek,
In sighing crowds around your curtains creep ?
And do you wonder, when at break of day
You hear the storm, and wake in sudden fright,
Whether the wretches, sold off yesterday.
Have perished in the wintry streets at night ?
N.S.W.
THOS E. SPENCER.
The Vision.
Night comes to me remorsefully,
The draughty stars subside and smur,
Or blaze with horror where the sea
Licks on the edge of Hell’s hot spur.
The tramping millions of the dead
With wailing songs and loamy rags
Push, thumb-tied, past my truckle-bed
Twelve-thick, like devil-driven stags.
There have 1 seen the mitred beast,
(His crozier-staff, crepusculine,
Supported by a recreant priest
With books and candles, bread and wine);
His smoke-white lips hooked up beneath
The shadow of his plough-share nose ;
His eyes turned, fainting, where the wreath
Of Satan’s umber furnace glows.
And all his scented raiment flares
With sparks and spouts of dropping flame,
Despite his gabbled Latin prayers
And cries on God’s neglected name.
Too late ... too late. His greasy flesh
Makes fat and fuel for the stake
Set i’ the thickest toss and thresh
Of Tophet’s red avenging lake.
Perdie ! The dirty lazar-thief,
The cut-purse harlot and her man
Have twice the earnest of relief
Than this high pontiff of his ban.
A curse on all hypocrisy !
A curse on ev’ry covered crime !
The eyes of God but magnify
The sin laid up till Judgment time. . . .
Lo ! Slowly thro’ the haggard night
The floating winds with dust festoon
And dim and blot the shining white
Curved beauty of the half-sword moon.
N.S.W.
HUGH McCRAE.
Ciss.
Oiss is bilious, tint’s her fault,
And on bilious days looks yellow.
But on good days, cry a halt,
She’s bewitching to a fellow ;
Then doth health her cheeks adorn,
Peerless then, she’s supercilious,
Then I’m forced to plead and fawn,
Till she once again is bilious.
Cakes and oranges and stuff
(Ciss, alas, is fond of feeding),
Make her liver groan and puff,
Then I’m haughty, and she’s pleading :
Bless me ! next day health begins.
She grows peerless, contumelious.
I must then confess my sins ;
She confesses—when she’s bilious.
Eyes of blue, how sweet they are,
Breasts divinely round and tumid,
Hair like thread-beams of a star,
Lips all rosy, soft and humid—
Ruddy cheeks enhance her charms.
But they make her grow punctilious,
I prefer her in my arms,
Cissy’s limp when Cissy’s bilious.
Oh, the joy of holding Ciss
In my arms, our lips uniting !
Oh, the rapture of a kiss
All the soul to love inciting !
Pliant then, her eyelids fall,
No more is she supercilious.
Love is monarch over all,
Cissy’s mine when Cissy’s bilious.
N.S.W.
RELWOF.

lloiv to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by talcing some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to- the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and, even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there ; the official
alivays is.

Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor ivill carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. ivill be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, JUNE 13, 1907.
JOSEPH’S NEW IDEA IN NASTINESS.
AcTiNG-Prime Minister Forrest told the State
Premiers that, in the matter of the State debts,
the Commonwealth was about to go ahead and do
the work with which the people entrusted it seven
years ago. Thereupon, having dodged the busi-
ness for so long, Carruthers, of N.S. Wales,'
propounds still another scheme, not merely of
dodging the business a little longer, but of “ dish-
ing ” the Commonwealth altogether. This sort of
policy is the price which N.S. Wales pays for
having for its Premier an attorney with a Police
Court mind —and a suburban Bobby Court at that.
If the magistrate won’t let the accused off because
he was drunk when he did it, then the Police
Court attorney is prepared to change his ground
and demand an acquittal because the accused was
sober and everybody else was drunk, and there-
fore incredible. The Police-Court attorney’s
one business in life is to “dish” the
other side. Carruthers brings that fine,
statesmanlike quality into the service of
N.S. Wales, and seems proud of it. He pro-
poses, in short, to put the debt where the Com-
monwealth can’t touch it—or where he thinks it
can’t; which, by the way, is much the same sort
of offence for which, a few days previously, a couple
of partners were committed for trial. The differ-
ence between the two cases is that, the partners
being practical men, they are alleged to have put
assets where they would be out of the way ; Car-
ruthers, being merely cantankerous, proposes to
put liabilities away where nobody else can reach,
them, and pay them off. His idea is to “partially
hand over the debt to the Railway Commissioners
as a corporate body,” and then hand over some
more to the Water and Sewerage Board—pre-
sumably also as a corporate body—and when he has
got rid of the debt that way he will, he reckons, have
a freer hand to throw mud at the Commonwealth
—that Commonwealth of which Carruthers still
insists that the Ma State is the most important
part. This/seems to show that Carruthers
doesn’t care how much mud gets into the Ma
State’s eye, so long as he can land some into any
other State’s ear.
* * * *
Joseph’s Police-Court attorney’s scheme is very
pretty, no doubt; but it can be looked at from
two or three different points, and Joseph has
apparently missed most of them. lhere is
Cohen’s point of view, for instance, Cohen
didn’t lend the money on the security of the rail-
ways alone. He lent it on the security of the
railways, lands, Customs and Excise, and of the
country generally; and the security of the
country means the power of taxing the country.
And up to now Chief Railway Commissioner Tom
Johnson hasn’t the power to levy so much as a
dog-tax. He can’t even say what the railway
rates shall be. The other day, when Carruthers
was sure that he had accomplished a surplus
(he accomplished it mostly by selling public lands,
and so reducing the real revenue for all time), his
Government instructed the Railway Commis-
sioners to cut large slices out of their rate-book,
and they did it. Seeing, then, that the Com-
missioners can’t even levy a dog-tax, and don’t
control the railway freights, and haven’t any
means of raising the interest apart from the taxes
they can’t levy and the freights they don’t control,
Cohen may have a word or two to say about
Joseph’s little “dishing” scheme. Also, for
much the same reasons, the Commissioners may
have a word or two to say about it. They will
point out that they, or their predecessors, reported
against many of the railways that Carruthers’
£50,000,000 has been spent upon ; also that they
will probably report against a good many more
railways that will be forced upon them ; and
they will require to know why they should be
saddled with a debt for something that they
didn’t want. Then, if Joseph replies that for the
future the railways are to be run as a business
enterprise, and must pay their way, it may relieve
the Commissioners of a little anxiety, but the
Backbone of the Country will start to be
interested. No doubt for the purpose of im-
pressing the country with the genuineness of its
mostly bogus surplus, the Carruthers Govern-
ment lately reduced the freights on the Backbone’s
produce, and the charge for the suburban tra-
veller’s season ticket ; and that sounds like
prosperity. But, though there was a railway
surplus in 1905-6, and will be another this year,
for 14 years before that there had been a net
shortage of £2,301,413. In other words, if the
railways had been worked as “ business enter-
prises,” and were looked to for full interest,
then the Backbone, and the suburban traveller,
and the rest of them, would in those 14 years have
had to pay £2,301,413 more than they did.
And, of course, if the debt is transferred to the
Commissioners, and they are told that they must
earn the interest, the £2,301,413 will have to be
paid; also, there won’t be any starving-stock
rates; and the gentleman who wants a new rail-
way built to develop the country will be asked to
call round again with a marked cheque to guar-
antee the interest and working expenses. From
that gentleman’s point of view, as well as that of
the Backbone of the Country and the other
railway users, Joseph’s scheme will probably
be observed to have several holes in the bottom
of it.
* * * *
And even Joseph himself may discover, as he
goes along, that the scheme is loaded. He says
that the railways represent so much of the
debt, and he proposes to pass on that much
to the Commissioners, and to do the same with
the water and sewerage debt. Then there will be
£22,000,000 left, of which about £2,750,000 repre-
sents borrowed money put into post and tele-
graphs, and defences. The other £19,250,000
represents nothing in particular; the odd thou-
sand that was fooled away on this work, and the
odd hundred that was guzzled and put down to a
punt, or a drain-pipe, or something. After every
million has been passed on that is represented by
anything that is earning anything, there will still
be a sediment of nearly 20 millions of debt; and,
when Cohen remembers the assurances that his
money was all going into permanent and repro-
ductive works, he will look at the sediment and
probably decide that it had better be got rid
of before any further transactions are carried
through.
• * * *
But even if Carruthers’ silly, puerile freak
were possible—if the Railway Commissioners
would accept the responsibility for £50,000,000 of
debt, if Cohen would accept their security, and if
the people would stand the big increase of rates
that would be necessary in bad times to make the
railways earn full interest on their liabilities —
even if all these things were possible, the Car-
ruthers device is still just as useful as a piece of
wet blotting-paper would be when regarded
as a means to obstruct the progress of an
ironclad that was in a hurry. It really makes no
earthly difference whether Joseph transfers
£50,000,000 of debt to the Railway Commissioners,
or the Water and Sewerage Board, or the
Methodist Conference, or the Society for the Con-
version of the Jews, or to the committee in charge
of the Salvarmy doss-house. The absolute
powers of the Commonwealth to take over the
£202,000,000 of State debts which existed at the
beginning of our Federation remain exactly the
same. So do its powers to obtain an amendment
of the Constitution, and, on the strength of that,
to take over any subsequent debts ; and its powers
to convert those debts, and establish sinking
funds for their reduction. As regards the
£202,000,000 of State debts which date back to
1901, all that is necessary is for the Aus-
tralian Parliament to pass the necessary short
Act, to intimate that the interest on cer-
tain loans will henceforth be paid by
the Commonwealth, and to deduct the necessary
amount from the Surpluses' now returned to the
States. If a frantic Joseph likes to grit his teeth
and assert his State dignity by paying the interest
over again, the Commonwealth need not object,
and the bond-holders will be quite pleased. Then
the Commonwealth can invite the bond-holders to
exchange their State securities for Federal ones;
or can simply pay off State loans as they fall due,
and for so much of the loans as the Federal sink-
ing funds will not cover, it can raise the money
by the issue of Federal securities. If Joseph
so far refuses to recognise the transfer of the debts
as to insist on also paying off loans as they fall
due, the bond-holders will gladly accept the two
payments; But Joseph will be politically dead
and gone long before that time. Meanwhile, if
he gets too much in the road, the Australian
Government may be induced to ask the High
Court to order that little State Fright to obey the
law. And if he doesn’t obey it the High Court
may get angry and commit him for contempt.
* * * *
Joseph Carruthers, in fact, has struck an
am'azingly silly Police Court idea of establishing
an alibi, or concealing the identity of the wanted
person under an alias. The Commonwealth has
jiower to take over, a certain State debt, and
Joseph proposes to call the thing a railway debt,
so that it won’t be recognisable when the Com-
monwealth calls for it. The creditors will be the
same, the bonds will be the same, the amount
will be identical, the same Treasury will pay the
interest, and it will be the same interest —in fact,
the accused will have the same features, clothes,
boots, hat, address, and everything ; but, whereas
he used to be Jones, he will now say that
he is Smith, yet all the time the old Jones
door-plate will be on his door. It is
a freak proposal, and hasn’t any possible sort
of hope of ever being adopted ; yet Carruthers
can accidentally do more good than he would
ever be likely to do intentionally by elaborating it,
and showing up the size of that part of the N.S.
Wales debt which is largely represented only by
good intentions and bad and still-remembered
headaches. If he sets that out clearly enough,
he may stir up N.S. Wales to do something
reasonable in the way of clearing this sediment
out. True, that seems to be the last thing that
Caeruthers wants to do. Apart from sheer can-
tankerousness, there is only one reason why any
State insect should try to block the transfer of the
debts to the Commonwealth, and that reason is
the determination of the Commonwealth to get
rid of the debts. As Bigjohn Forrest told the
Brisbane party, the Commonwealth Government
is wedded to a sinking fund ; and in that bit of
projected honesty lies the Commonwealth’s real
offence.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Ineffective Tariff.
The best test of whether the existing duties offer
enough protection to Australian work people, and
those who have put money into Australian
industries, is the movement of imports in lines
that are supposed to be protected. Commonwealth
Statistician Knibbs has just supplied data for
this test, by producing his first trade figures. They
cover the opening three months of the year, and
show that £12,210,306 of foreign goods arrived, as
against £10,773,463 in the first quarter of 1906.
Thus the foreigner supplied, in these three months,
a million and a half more value in goods than he did
in the same part of last year. Therefore, as a full
fourth of that represented labor, Australia paid the
foreign workman over £300,000 more than it did in
1906. Meantime an Arbitration Court is sitting, to
hear that so many Australians are available for
the job of passing on the imported calico to the
public, that adult assistants, some of them married,
can be got for 255. per week. Here are half-a-
dozen or so specific samples of the Australian
suicide that results in married shop assistants at
255. a week :
Jan.-Mar., Jan.-Mar.,
1906. ' 1907.
Apparel £3,155,934 £3,472,638
Cordage and twines .. .. 136,271 142,876
Furniture .. .. .. 47,398 55,518
Hats and caps 119,904 130,785
Agricultural tools .. .. 84,828 115,775
Bar and rod iron .. .. 202,437 235,823
Galvanised iron.. .. .. 282,197 369,818
Machines (not agricultural) .. 436,810 593,962
Metal goods 283,956 326,089
Paints .. .. .. .. 86,896 97,502
On account of the great natural resources that the
papers talk about, almost every one of those lines
represents goods which Australia is in a better
position to produce than all the world besides, if
the industry were but once put on its feet and
protected against foreign cheap labor afterwards.
In fact, a great quantity of the imports must
actually be made by foreign labor out of Austra-
lian material, which has been humped to the
other end of the earth and back again—at the ex-
pense, of course, of the Australian producer in the
first place, and the Australian consumer in the
second place. Wool, for instance, for the manu-
facture of apparel, also lead, zinc, and antimony
for the manufacture of paints, are simply poured
into Europe from Australia, to be shipped
back again after they have furnished the
European laborer with work, the Euro-
pean manufacturer with profits, and the
European capitalist with industries to pro-
fitably finance. The Australian not only
misses all that, but he pays enormous sums for
totally unnecessary cartage, not of those articles
only, but also of the wheat and things that the
man at the other end of the earth consumes while
he is making up the Australian goods, when he
might just as well be consuming them at this end.
So what between not earning the money he ought
to earn, and paying away huge sums that he
needn’t pay away, the Australian worker finds
his wages bitten off at both ends; and when, on
the strength of a testimonial from a Judge or
somebody, he creeps into a job at serving out
tape, he finds the bitten wages are as much as £1
a week. Then, remembering that McMillan, the
typical softgoodsman, is a Eoreign-trader, he
reckons it is up to him to be a Foreign-trader too,
so that he can “get things cheap.” Poor chap,
he doesn’t see the difference between the Fat Man,
and the mite in the cheese that tUe Fat Man gets
fatter on !

The Rev. Stinking Fish.
Fitchett, of the steal magazine, and the indus-
trious scissors, and the enthusiastic paste, and
the strangely bungled historical writings, is
either curiously ignorant of history or he has
awful ill-luck in the way of being misreported.
Consider, for instance, the account of his recent
lecture on “ The Motherland and her Children,”
at Stanmore Wesleyan Church (Sydney). Here
is Jingo Fitchett waving the blanky shirt in his
best form :
The secret of Britain’s wealth was her industrial genius,
and it was due also to her Protestantism and the open
Bible. The only patch of soil in Europe that for 890
years had never felt ihe touch of an invader’s foot was
the Motherland of our race. (Applause.) Those 800
years of peace had helped to make England rich. . . .No
continental country enjoyed the liberty of England;
There was the amplest freedom with the most stable
order. England lost her American colonies through
attempting to tax them, but she made no such attempt
with Australia. Bather, the boot was on the other leg.
Whilst England forebore to tax us, we taxed every bale
of goods that came from the Motherland. Magnanimous
mother, not to complain !
Think of Fitciiett’s historical accuracy ! Maud
of Germany invaded England about 1139, and
knocked the country about for several years'. A
little later her son Henry landed with French
soldiers to argue the point with Stephen,
the howling swordsman of Blois. A French
prince invaded England less than 700 years
ago, and had not a-hot-headed French general
allowed himself to be led into a scrambling
battle in the harrow streets of Lincoln, Louis,
nicknamed the Lion, might have secured the
country. There was a Dutch invasion not much
more than 200 years ago, which changed the
course of history. There were quite a number of
Scotch invasions—the last less'than 200 years ago.
There was an Irish army doing battle in England
within the period that Fitchett mentions,
though it was only a sort of an army and may
be left out of the tally. Also French and
Burgundian soldiers trod the soil of England
at various times in support of various pretenders.
Apparently Fitchett, the historian, has never
heard of these events. As for Fitciiett’s state-
ment that Australia taxes “every bale of goods
that comes from the Motherland,” that is simply
a falsehood —the falsehood of an excited Jingo
preacher who didn’t even t’ake the trouble to read
up the Australian tariff before he held forth. It
would be no disgrace to Australia if the state-
ment were true, for we have as much right to
practise Protection for the purely selfish reason
that we think it will pay us to do so, as
England has to practise Freetrade for the
purely selfish reason that it thinks there
is profit in that policy. But Fitchett evi-
dently regards it as a disgraceful act to tax
every bale of goods that comes from “ the Mother-
land.” He throws the statement at us, regarding
it as mud. Therefore, it is worth while to tell
Fitchett that we don’t do what he alleges, and
that he is throwing what he regards as mud with
a shocking disregard of the truth. Furthermore,
it may astonish Fitchett to know that some of
the things we export to England pay rather heavy
duties there, and that England’s Customs duties
average over 6 per cent, on all the country’s
imports. If Fitchett knows these things, why
does he mangle the truth so disgracefully ? If he
doesn’t know them, why does he set up in business
as an instructor ?

Subsidies and State Rights and Things.
The large capacity of Anti-Sosh for swallowing
Socialistic subsidies is getting it into trouble.
The Victorian millers and big, anti-Sosh wheat
dealers induced Victoria to give a shipping firm a
subsidy of £2OOO a year to run a line to Singapore
and thereabouts; and S. Aus., which had a
Singapore flour business already, screams that
the thing is unfederal and unconstitutional,
because i„ means that now the Vic. flour and
wheat men will be able to undersell the S. Aus-
tralian exporters, who have to pay all their own
freight. So long as there are States, there will be
that sort of subsidy, anyway. The one cure for
it is unification, and the one way to make the
disease spread and get worse is to whittle away
instead of extending the force and functions of
the Federation. The £2OOO is a small matter
compared with the help a State can freely give
through its railways, even if all the present
powers of the Commonwealth are exercised;
N.S. Wales, for instance, the other day subsi-
dised its farmers to the extent of tens of
thousands a year at one pop. And S. Aus-
tralia subsidises its wool and wheat and wine
growers by running an export depot; _ and
N.S. Wales subsidises its exporters to Singa-
pore and thereabouts by paying the large salary
of an expensive commercial traveller and secretary
to buzz for business in those parts. Queensland
used to also relieve its merchants of the necessity
of paying a commercial traveller in Asia by pay-
ing Jones ; but Jones, though he is still the
Queensland Commercial Agent, gets his com-
mission from the merchants — Kidston being
Premier up that way now —and confesses to an
income of £lOOO a year or more. It is just as well
for the crowd that is calling out about State Rights
to remember what State Rights mean. They don’t
simply mean the right of the State to give back-
talk to the Commonwealth ; they mean also the
right of the States to pick one another’s eyes out.
That sort of thing may suit the big State;
but the little State is liable to lose its eyes,
and be shortly in need of a dog and a piece
of string and a stick. Yet it is the little
State like Tasmania which is readiest to follow
Carruthers’ lead in yapping about State Rights;
which only shows what a great fool a little State
may be. There is another aspect also of this subsidy
business. Price, at the Premiers’ Conference in
Brisbane, ran in a motion at the last moment de-
ploring this sort of policy, but withdrew it after
an unreported discussion. Yet he was sure of
quite a lot of sympathy. But if, on behalf of the
Australian producer—not the producer of any one
small patch of the Commonwealth —a Premier
had protested against vast sums being spent to
subsidise foreign-owned mail steamers, so that
the bill for the imported blanket might arrive 36
hours earlier than otherwise, he would most likely
have been laughed at. Even the nominally Pro-
tectionist States have a great deal too much re-
spect for the importing Joss.
“A Link.”
Now that the British Government has laid it
down, for the manyth time, and with an emphasis
which must at last have driven the fact into the
thickest State cranium, that all its dealings with
Australia must go through the Commonwealth
Government, it is surely time that the imported
State Governor was abolished. He was supposed
to be a "link” binding each separate State to the
beloved Ma Land, but since Britain itself has
decided to become officially unaware of the exist-
ence of the States, and refuses to know that there
is anything at the other end of the "link,” what
in thunder is the use of the “ link ” ? Apparently
it is now merely a scrap of broken chain
which Ma drags behind her. When two
things are tied together, and the second thing
isn’t there, and consequently the first thing
is only tied to itself, the natural result is that the
first thing is only encumbered with a lot of use-
less rope. And there are so many better uses to
which rope could be put. If the imported State
Governors were abolished, with all their useless
attendants, hangers-on, uniforms, private secre-
taries, aides-de-camp, residences, travelling ex-
penses, frills and passementerie, and the real work
of the billet, which is mostly a matter of assenting
to Bills and receiving the frequently-far-too-rare
resignation of a State Ministry, were entrusted
to a deputy appointed by the Governor-General,
Australia might save enough in Course of time to
build a battleship. And a good battleship could
communicate with the British Government if war
happened. If it dropped a big shell right on the
head of one of Bull’s enemies it would be some-
thing like a “ link.” Unfortunately the recent
Conference of Local Premiers was too much
occupied with matters of its own dignity to con-
sider the Australian, aspect of the case. It didn’t
exactly curse the British Government for refusing
to communicate with Australia except through
the Commonwealth authorities, but it was very
angry at the Commonwealth for being there to be
communicated through. Not daring to curse the
man at the other end of the tube, it denounced
the tube.

The Fighting Jew.
“ Philo ” makes a few statements
Re The Bulletin’s remark (8/5/’O7) that the Russian
Jew has no fighting record. On the contrary, the Rus-
sian Jew has a more substantial record of military
achievement than the Jew of any otheivcountry. In the
Crimean war he was very conspicuous, and his fighting
proclivities received special recognition. Then one of the
most brilliant feats of heroism in the Russo-Turkish dis-
turbance was performed by a Jew named Moisch Fkioe.n-
baun. He and a large number of other Jews marched
into the bed of a river and flung themselves down in the
water to form a human causeway for the passage of the
o-uns and their names were inscribed on the Russian Roll
of Honor for the deed. Of the exceptionally large
number of American Jewish soldiers who served in the
Spanish-American war, and to whose bravery President
Roosevelt gave special tribute, the majority were
Russians One regiment, of six hundred men, the Man-
hattan Rifles, which did particularly good work, was
composed entirely of Russian Jewish emigrants. In
proportion to their numbers, the Jews sent more soldiers
to South Africa than the English themselves, and
most of them were sons of Russian emigrants.
In the recent Bear-Monkey affair, Russian official
figures show that there were, proportionally, 8 per cent,
more Jews in the war than people of any other Bear race.
In the battle of Lioyang 2000 Jews were killed, and at


Mukden the number was muoh larger. So many Jews
served in the army in Manchuria that the unleavened
bread sent out to them at the Passover (1905) filled a
special train of 24 carriages. A number of Russian
generals—especially the great Kuropatkin —during the
war repeatedly referred to the “ valor and courage of
their Jewish soldiers. Carl Joubert said; “In the Russian
army there is no more useful soldier than the Jew.” Also,
a number of Russian newspapers alluded to tjie extra-
ordinary bravery of the Jewish soldiers (I’ve seen the
translations.) Yet, among many other disabilities, a
Russian Jewish soldier cannot become .an officer, and
the 47 roubles which are given to the widow of a
Russian soldier killed in action are withheld by law
from the widow of the Hebrew soldier. Although the
number of Jews accepted in the Russian medical schools
is restricted by law to between three and ten per cent, of
the students, and although in time of peace no Jewish
doctor receives an appointment for the army or navy, at
least one-half of the medical men sent to the front in the
Bear-Ape war were Jews. Sixty-five of them went from
Warsaw alone. (This information is from a Russian
source.) The Odessa correspondent of the London
Standard, who personally showed very little sympathy
for the Jews, wrote : “ It is admitted that 52 per cent, of
the medical students at the front in Manchuria are
Jews.” Forty-five out of the ninety doctors who went on
active service from the medical school atKarbinwere
Jews. In face of these facts—and they are all positive
facts — The Bulletin’s remarks look like an attempt to
rob Mars of his Jew.
No Money to Spend on Australia.
The State Premier of N.S.W. has supplied a new
and fairly clear definition of his own patriotism.
He is willing that Australia should be saved, if it
doesn’t cost him anything; but if it is going to
cost him anything, Australia, it would seem, may
just go and be lost. Joseph Carruthers is quite
ready to admit that the Northern Territory, as it
stands, is a vast danger to Australia. It
is close to hostile Asia, it is unpeopled
and ungarrisoned ; and if it were occu-
pied by an enemy who had command of
the sea, there is no railway along which assistance
could be sent. Moreover, it is still a British
Crown colony, administered on sufferance by
S.A., but liable at any moment to be resumed and
made a dependency of India or Burmah, or
Singapore, or handed over to a Chartered Co. and
run on purely nigger principles. Britain still
retains the right to assume direct possession at a
moment’s notice, and then the White Australia
law, and every other Australian law, would cease
to have any value so far as one-sixth of this conti-
nent is concerned. Carruthers admits all these
things, and, so long as “ the States ” (which
include all the people of Australia) don’t require
to pay anything, he thinks measures should be
taken to obviate the danger. If there is anything
to be paid, then, apparently, he would rather let
Australia t- Iter on the verge of the precipice, or
fall over t'n.i precipice. Not only does Joseph
not propose to shed his blood to save his country
if need be; lie doesn’t propose to shed even 2d.
Here is his own statement: —
I acUnit that while we have the Northern Territory un-
developed it is a menace to the rest of Australia ; but I
say, if the Commonwealth Government is going to take
it over at a time when the needs of the other States are
unprovided for by the present revenue, that we have a
right to object to the proposal so far as it affects our
finances, Throw the onus on the Federal Parliament—
they have to deal with this matter, if the Commonwealth
members are favorable to it. I do not express an opinion
Whether they will be favorable or not, but, if they are
favorable, throw the onus upon them of making an
arrangement which will not prejudicially affect ■ the
States which, unfortunately, cannot afford to be prejudi-
cially affected. . . Why should the rest of Australia,
which has had to pay the price of its pioneering work
and which has still a great amount of pioneering work to
do—why should the rest of Australia be taxed to develop
this portion of Australia at this particular juncture? . . .
Let US tell the Commonwealth Prime Minister that WE
would prefer to have an arrangement made which would
not involve US at this juncture in a definite loss. If this
railway can be constructed without any sacrifice of OUR
interests, let it be constructed.
It is hard to find words to express the contempt
that every decent Australian ought to feel for the
person who is willing that his country should be
protected if it-doesn’t cost him anything. And the
huge insolence of Joseph almost surpasses even his
readiness to let his country take its chance.
The Australian Parliament, created by the Aus-
tralian people, proposes, with their consent, to
spend a little of their money on their defence, for
the railway to the Northern Territory is as essen-
tial an item of defence as the military forces
themselves. The State Governments will still get
back the three-fourths of the Customs and Excise
revenue to which they are entitled, and beyond
that they have no claims whatever. In giving
advice to the Australian Parliament as to how it
should run a purely national matter, the effront-
ery of this mere local personage is akin to that of
Rockefeller’s office-kid telling Rockefeller
how to run the business. And the “we” and
“us” of the purely local Insect are very much
out of place. In local drain-pipe matters “we”
are the mayor and aldermen, and “us” are the
ratepayers. In matters dealing with State lands,
and a few similar items “we,” in N.S.W., are
Carruthers and his colleagues, and the State
electors are “ us.” In national concerns like
Defence, posts, telegraphs, the settlement of
the Federal capital and the matter of the
Northern Territory, the Australian Government
and Parliament are “ we” and the whole body of
Australian electors are “us.” When Joseph
Carruthers, as a mere local calamity, with no
more voice in Federal affairs than any other Federal
elector, brings forward resolutions about national
affairs at a meeting of officials as purely local as
himself, he is simply a petty and cheeky person
who doesn’t know his own limitations. When the
same Joseph proposes that our motherland should
be left exposed to the worst danger that has ever
befallen it unless itoan be rescued without tberescue
costing him anything—well, when he does that, he
is just Joseph, and nothing much worse can be
said of Carruthers in a political sense than that
he is just himself. That covers everything. And
when he takes up the attitude that N.S.W. can’t
afford to pay a trifle towards the protection of
Australia—the Australia which is alleged by the
Carruthers crowd to be mostly N.S.W.-—at a
time when it has about the biggest surplus in its
history, and proposes to reduce taxes and to
“blew” much public money on the subsidising of
friendly societies (Druids, Buffaloes and the like)
—well, again there is nothing worse to be said of
him than that he is himself. Contempt ends there.
Joseph has no money for Australia, but he has
money for Druids.

Australian Naval Defence.
The Bulletin recommends’this letter to the con-
sideration of all Australians :
Dear Bulletin.—l am weary alike of W. H. Kelly and
Captain Creswell. The problem of Australian defence
contains more things than either of them dream of. The
M.H.R. has been reared on the axioms, and postulates of
Mauan, and could make out a good enough case if there
were anything in the Yankee captain’s theories to meet
the Australian situation. But there is not. Our position
as far as naval defence, or want of defence, is concerned,
is absolutely unique, and no appeal to history will throw
any light on it.
As for Creswell and his toy flotilla, the thing is either
a joke or an imbecility. Any compliance with this scheme
would mean sheer waste of money, and would delude the
easily cajoled Australian public into an utterly unfounded
sense of security.
Let us go over the facts again. We can never do this
too often.
Ever since the days of the great Armada, with the
exception of a period, when the Dutch disputed its
supremacy, the British navy has been the cock of the
seas, and it could truly be said in Mahanic phrase that
the frontiers of England Were the coasts of her enemies
all over the world. Tne enemies’ fleets were penned up
in their ports, and if they got out, the maxim was to fol-
low them up and destroy them.
But this policy has been completely changed during
the last few years. The British War vessels have been
withdrawn from the Seven Seas, and concentrated for
defence in British waters. Two' years ago, there
were four battleships on the China station ; to-day there
is none. The Mediterranean licet has been weakened,
so has the North American.
The objective of the fleet is now, and will remain, not
the coast-lines of the continents, but a narrow strip of
shore on the North Sea.
We in Australia are guaranteed from offence from that
quarter, and if that were the only direction from which
danger could threaten, we could sleep soundly in our
beds o’ nights, and be justified in voting a subsidy of a
million to the Imperial navy.
Because the real menace to the future of Australia has
only become visible during tile last year or two, most
men have failed as yet to grasp ils significance. Three
times nearer our shores than the massed Imperial navy,
exists a fleet, small, but second to none in efficiency, and
about to become infinitely more formidable by the early
addition to it of some of the most powerful battleships
afloat. These ships are held in leash, as far as we are
concerned, for the next eight years, by considerations of
diplomacy that will have lost their potency at the end of
that time.
And at that date the British naval forces will probably
be confined more closely than ever round the English
coasts. However willing the mother country might be,
she would not dare to detach her Dreadnoughts for the
protection of Australia, even though we doubled or
trebled our subsidy.
What Chen is to stand between us and the might of the
Power whose navy will have undisputed control of the
Pacific? Frankly, I do. not know, although I can say
without hesitation . that 'it is riot Captain Creswell’s
mosquito crafts. The most drastic and heroic measures
are called for, and I fear we have no statesman in Aus-
tralia prepared to propose them, nor a people sufficiently
alert and enlightened to give ear to them. But what
cries aloud to be done is to increase enormously the
population, and so the number of taxpayers. Im-
migration on a huge scale is the question of
questions for Australia during the next 10 years.
There should be no. renewal of the Braddon clause, so that
the Commonwealth may have ample funds to carry this
out. And defence should at once take the form of com-
pulsory service either on land or at sea. For those who
chose the latter, plenty of old warships could be had for
the asking from the Imperial authorities, on board of
which an admirable training could he obtained. Ten
years hence, we could make a beginning by investing, at
least £10,000,000 in the very latest naval engines of
destruction, and could man them with our own sailors.
This I admit, would be an intolerable burden for a small,
if wealthy, community like this; but we have got,'
sooner or later, to realise the appalling character of our
geographical position, and to make sacrifices commen-
surate with it. We will have to submit cheerfully to
being taxed to the last penny, to resign ourselves to a
universal service as rigid and oppressive as ever ruled in
any military State in the old world, and, when we have
done all these things, we shall escape—if at all—only by
the skin of our teeth.—Yours, ett:., Richard Arthur.

The Discourteous Caucasian.
About the most ingenious apology so far given
for the Jap’s disinclination to pay what he owes,
and to carry out the terms of contracts he enters
into, was offered at Sydney University the other
night by Professor Jordan, the lecturer imported -
from the Land of the Fried Nigger and the
Potted Man to talk British Imperialism through
his nose. Jordan said that the Monkey was fre-
quently denounced for dishonesty, but he was not
really dishonest. All he wanted was to disown a
contract that didn’t pay him. Jordan ex-
plained that, unlike China, Japan was, until
recently, unacquainted with the contract
system, and, if a mutual agreement didn’t
turn out so profitable for the Monkey as he had
anticipated, he felt that he was entitled to repu-
diate it, while the other party—usually a Euro-
pean, in practice—was expected to be sufficiently
courteous to let things slide. Though Jordan
carefully omitted all reference to the fact that
Jap civil tribunals have a way of invariably up-
holding their countryman against the foreigner
who seeks protection from this peculiar code of
morals, it is possible that the principle of mercan-
tile courtesy pervades Monkey law as well, and
that the foreigners who take action when they
are defrauded through the refusal of Mr. Monkey
to keep his pledged word should deem themselves
lucky that they are not fined for discourtesy,
in addition to being thrown out of court.
In the light of investigation, however, Jordan’s
facts are as erroneous as his conclusions. The
argument that the Jap is unacquainted with the
contract system is ridiculous. Ever since human
beings were able to articulate distinctly, they
have been in the habit of saying to one another
“If you’ll do this. I’ll do that.” The Very spirit
of Bushido, which Jordan grew maudlin over
in the earlier portion of his speech, is based on the
contract system. If the Jap is unable to dis-
tinguish right from wrong, as J ORDAN states, he
is simply an instinctive criminal, and Japan
is the * moral plague spot of the earth. By
Jordan’s showing it stands alone as the single
place where the obligations of one individual to
another—recognised among the savages of Africa
and the maligned aborigines of Australia —are
unknown. In effect, Jordan, in combating the
contention that many Japs are thieves, excused
these persons by saying that all Japs are thieves,
and lack the moral sense to know that they are
committing wrong; that Japan is inhabited by a
nation of criminal lunatics, and that the only way
for the foreigner to prevent himself being robbed
is to refrain from dealing with any Jap
whatsoever. Yet Jordan’s lecture was in-
structive in one aspect. His audience was a
typical University audience, culled from the
fashionable suburbs of Sydney. Much of it was
in evening dress, and it was’just the sort of gather-
ing that might have been expected to exhale a
strong odor of Stinking Fish. Still, it received
Jordan’s pro-Jap remarks with a chilling silence.
When the nasal snuffler paused for the applause
at the end of a telling sentence, the applause was
not forthcoming, and even Willie Kelly, on the
platform, was afraid to put his hands together
for fear he might be applauding alone, like the
pelican giving three cheers in the wilderness.
But, when Jordan, while denying that Japan in-
tended to invade Australia, warned it at the same
time to be prepared for such an invasion, the audi-
ence let loose all the enthusiasm that had been
accumulating during the evening.

Universal Postage.
The relation between a very angry Minister and
a very red postage-stamp may not be at once
apparent, yet even such things have a con-
nection which becomes clear after a little study.
A few weeks ago the Hon. G. Fowlds, Maoriland
Minister of Education, expressed himself deeply
shocked at the attitude taken up by Prime
Minister Deakin and his colleague Lyne, at the
recent Imperial Conference. And, among other
things, Fowlds said these
The tone adopted in their speeches at HOME has been
positively distressing. It is absurd that they should
attempt to dictate a policy to the Mother Land. I have
always believed very strongly in maintaining the unity
of the Empire, but if such a policy as that adopted by
the Australian Premiers is to be continued at future Con-
ferences, a party will grow up at HOME which will want
to sever all connection with the colonies, and the Con-
ferences will cease. Sir William Lyne in particular has
shown execrable taste in forcing himself upon the Con-
ference as an uninvited guest. The idea of two men who
can only command a following of seventeen in a House of
seventy-five going HOME to dictate to the British Govern-
ment is simply preposterous. The real practical results
of the Conference will follow from the suggestions made
by Sir Joseph Ward.
These observations were not very interesting in
themselves, but a dozen or more people were
so excited over the remarks of Fowlds that they
cut out great masses of the newspapers in which
his sayings were reported, and each man pushed
his clippings into an envelope, and stuck on it the
little reddish stamp bearing the enormous legend
“ Universal Postage,” and sent the thing to this
office. It still keeps arriving at this office-
worse luck ! Maoriland is, apparently, the
only white man’s country on earth which
has the giddy assurance to print on its stamp
“ universal postage ” —as if that one badly-
printed stamp would carry a letter to Saghalien
or Jupiter. Some little half-bred American
republics do that sort of thing, but neither
Britain, Russia, France nor the United States
puts on quite as much pomp as this, and
the stamps on the famous “Letters from
Hell” didn’t bear so vast a legend. The
Bulletin paid excess postage on most of these
letters, the “Universal” postage stamp not
having anything like the carrying capacity the
Maorilarider thinks it has. Then it opened the
letters and read the tale of Fowlds. Fowlds is,
like many other Maorilanders, a quaint mixture
of enormous cheek and “loyal” humility. He
hasn’t any home of his own ; he still calls a place
about 12,000 miles away “Home,” with a capital
“ II.” Not being able to get “ Home,” he is a
nomad, or tent-dweller, or a person camped under
a sheet of bark in his own mind. He hasn’t yet
realised either that his country has a name of its
own; he lumps it in with a number of other
places as “the colonies.” He is particular
about the silly little titles conferred by the
authorities at the distant place he calls “ Home”
-—the “Sir” before the name of William
Lyne, and the “Sir” before the designation
of Joseph Ward. He is shocked at the
rugged independence of Alfred De.akin and
the aforesaid William Lyne, and is grieved to
the soul because they were not’umble like Mr.
Heep. Yet, amidst his abasement, the vast con-
ceit of the Homeless creature stands out. He is
positive that, at a conference at which were also
present the representatives of the British Govern-
ment, Prime Minister Laurier (Laurier is a
' tall man of his hands) to speak for Canada
and its 5,000,000 people, the representatives
of Newfoundland, Cape Colony, Natal, the
Transvaal (Louis Botha is a man of size), and of
Australia and its 4,000,000 inhabitants—he is sure
that whatever real practical results may arise
from that gathering will be owing to the man
from little Maoriland, a new country with a popu-
lation about equal to that of Glasgow or Liver-
pool. And his yarn reaches here with an insuf-
ficient penny stamp, on which is printed
“ Universal postage ” —as if it would carry a book
packet to Gabriel, the Archangel.
A Shriek from Chatswood.
The proposal to run the N.S.W. State elections
on a programme of bogus Ma State grievances has
roused a few members of the Sydney importing
push to a fury of enthusiasm, and they have
taken up the pen with energy, and dabbed it into
the ink with speed, and written to the papers.
Almost every week some anti-Australian fool, or
liar, or wholly unaccountable person, stands out
conspicuously above the ruck of fools and liars
and unaccountable persons, in the correspondence
columns of the daily press, and makes mirth for
the community. Last week a high place was
held by one “J.E.C.,” who wrote to Sydney
Telegraph, giving his address as Chatswood.
Some extracts from the ravings of “J.E.C.,” of
Chatswood, follow:
So flagrant have been the breaches of faith that unless
we show fight our partners will soon possess all the assets
in the concern, and our share will consist solely of bitter
experience. .. .
New South Wales is undoubtedly in a position to
require the Imperial Parliament, which drafted that
contract set out in the Constitution Act, to see that
every provision is carried into effect. . . .
She [Victoria] knows that the choice of any site south of
Sydney will cut off a big slice of New South Wales territory
from its proper government, and throw it more completely
than ever into the lap of Melbourne ; hence the desire to
make the Federal territory larger than the 900 square
miles contemplated by the Constitution. . . .
They are not slow to appreciate that if the Common-
wealth becomes surety for the debts, it will blandly ask
for the assets, and, if their request is not acceded to, will
exercise its powers to take over the railways. The mag-
nificent railway system of New South Wales, with its
profit-making capabilities, would make an effective bol-
ster for the miserable wheelbarrow tracks of some of our
collectively dominant partners.
This person ought to be about the record parochial
ass jf the week. He actually alleges, in the
vastness of his parish ignorance, that the Austra-
lian Constitution was drafted by the Imperial
Parliament, that it provides definitely for a Fede-
ral territory of 900 square miles, and that it gives
the Commonwealth power to compulsorily take
over the railways. And “ J.E.C.,” of Chatswood,
raves over these figments of his disordered imagi-
nation. In this respect, “J.E.C.,” of Chatswood, is
like most of his tribe, and in another respect he is
like all of them. Despite his shouts about alleged
breaches of the Constitution effected to the pre-
judice of N.S.W., he doesn’t propose putting into
writing the exact details of even one alleged
breach, and then going to the High Court (com-
posed mostly of Judges from N.S.W. and Queens-
land) for redress. Not one of the anti-Federal
shriekists and woe-mongers ever does. Even the
most virulent anti-Federal liar seems to know
that his case couldn’t bear a moment’s honest
judicial inspection.

High Court and Privy Council.
The Australian High Court has come to the only
possible conclusion in regard to “the income-tax
cases.” The extraordinary thing is that one of the
Justices should have given a dissenting judgment,
for the only point at issue in this latest phase on the
matter was whether the Australian Court should
deliberately abandon the duty with which it was
entrusted by the Australian Constitution of inter-
preting that deed of national partnership. As
the Chief Justice well pointed out, it would be a
recreant and unworthy thing to do that. The
responsibility was deliberately conferred ; it was
as deliberately accepted ; it can be in no way
vitiated or altered by any mistakes or fancied
mistakes, and it cannot, without cowardice, be
abandoned. There is such a maze of legal issues
around these income-tax cases that the main
points may be with advantage briefly sum-
marised :
The Australian Constitution invests in the Australian
High Court final jurisdiction in all matters of the inter-
pretation of that Constitution.
The Australian Parliament, as a matter of convenience,
vested in the State Courts original jurisdiction in Federal
matters. It is now contended that the decision of a State
Court, even though it be on a Federal Constitutional
matter, can be referred on appeal to the Privy Council,
and that any attempt of the Australian Parliament to
limit that appeal is ultra vires.
The Australian High Court held that Federal officers
should not be liable to pay State income-taxes. The de-
cision followed an American case, the idea in America at
the time being that some especially nasty local J. Car-
ruthers might drive the Federal authority out of a State
by taxing oppressively its officers. In The Bulletin’s
opinion, the High Court’s decision was unwise. It would
be quite time enough to guard against differential State
taxation of Federal officers when it was attempted. The
High Court shot at a bear which isn’t there so far. But
that is beside the question.
An appeal was made to the Privy Council on the point,
by the subterfuge made possible by the Federal Judiciary
Act, and the Privy Council reversed the decision of the
High Court.
A fresh case was taken, and the High Court was asked
to say what it thought of the matter now. It decided
that it was of just the same opinion, and that the Privy
Council should mind its own business.
No other answer could honestly have been given.
Now there is curiosity as to the next step. The
Australian Government, we may announce,
intends to remove the very small bone around
which this great contention has arisen, and pro-
vide through special legislation for the payment
by Federal officers of State income-taxes. . That
will leave the arena clear for the issue whether
Australia intends to interpret its own Constitu-
tion or allow outsiders to interfere —and, as
regards this special matter, ignorant outsiders at
that.

“ Clericus,” re the Employers’ Federation and
the sweating of clerks in Melbourne :
The ingenious methods adopted by the Employers’
Federation to investigate (and disprove) the allegations
with regard to the sweating of clerks, have filled the
quill-pushing profession with admiration. At the presi-
dent’s request, the 'secretary proceeded to test the
accuracy of certain statements which were a “disgrace to
the commercial community," and he displayed remark-
able dispatch and diplomacy in the work. He imme-
diately interviewed the managers of one or two selected
offices, and delicately asked them if they sweated their
clerks. Triumphantly he reported to the council that
they said they didn’t, and that, in several instances, he
had actually been shown the wages books, and had found in
them no facts to support the allegations. Of course, had
any such facts existed, the managers would have been de-
lighted to indicate them and supply the inquirer with
detailed and reliable data to place before his council.
From the result of these exhaustive investigations the
diligent secretary could only conclude that the state-
ments were grossly exaggerated. The Employers’ Federa-
tion, however, has decided to continue the investigations,
and hundreds of struggling clerks are still hoping that
the secretary may inadvertently discover something that
he doesn’t want to hear of. ,
The secretary of the Employers’ Federation stated that
in one institution a staff of 40 clerks are receiving
salaries averaging £175 per annum. Were he to carry his
investigations further he would, no doubt, be surprised to
find many large staffs receiving an average of under £IOO
per year. He would be astonished to discover a staff of
over 40 clerks amongst whom four or five head men re-
ceive £2 10s. or £3 per week, whilst the remaining clerks
collect an average wage of 30s. He would further be
shocked to learn that in another large and prosperous
business, the heads of departments are paid £3 per
week ; the chief ledger-keeper, £2 10s.; the sub-ledger-
keepers £2 ; the cashier (handling hundreds of pounds
daily and after ten vears of faithful service), £2; the
senior clerks, 355. and 375. 6d. ; and the other clerks from
15s to 30s. per week. If Mr. Walpole persevered in his
inquiries he would doubtless be quite dumbfounded to
The Old Unifier.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Mr. Carruthers protests,” &c., “Mr. Piiilp objects,”
fee., “ Mr. Evans disagrees,” &c., “Mr. Newton Moore
threatens,” &c.— Any issue of any Daily Paper.
In an age obscure, uncharted,
Came a People grim, stout-hearted,
And they seized the land called Britain with a
firm, emphatic seize;
’Twas an age of grab—of slaughter,
And the Jutes from o’er the water
Smote with large and frequent waddies Britain’s
aborigines !
So the British Combo perished—
And the land that he had cherished
Passed to seven Saxon rulers—angry men in iron
coats.
Swords on bull-hide helmets pounded,
And the Heptarchy resounded
With the strife of warring parties in the Witena-
gemotes.
In that olden, misty Britain,
Where the hapless Celts were smitten,
There were seven Saxon kingdoms occupied by
seven kings.
There were seven Legislatures
Where Ms.P. with insect-natures
Rose and cursed at Federation—rose and howled
untruthful things!
There the strong States cheeked the others,
And an ancient, cheap Carruthers
Gibbered wildly in the distance re the Ma State’s
bitter wrongs ;
While the angry folk of Essex
Heaved the cat of scorn at Wessex,
And the D.T.’s dim ancestors chanted shrill
Secession songs !
But at last there rose a Fighter—
’Twas that Egbert called the Smiter.
He went through Kent and Mercia with his axe
collecting votes;
And the small kings broke asunder,
And the State-Rights mob went under
With their scandalous collection of dim Witena-
gemotes.
On a dusty page ’tis written
How he unified old Britain —
How he filled each shrill Carruthers with a very
solemn fear.
Yea, the boasted English nation
Only found its rightful station
When Egbert served his notice on the old State-
Frights to clear.
Now, in this our newer Britain,
With its history still unwritten,
There are surplus States like England’s when the
Insect-Kings held sway ;
And the Saxon-time Carruthers,
And his uncles and his brothers,
Are alive again and crying for disunion here
to-day.
And we wait for the uprising
Of another man surprising
Like the Unifier mentioned in old Saxon anec-
dotes,
To wreck six Legislatures
Run by men with beetle-natures,
And bury deep for aye our parish Witenagemotes.

If employers are so eager to pay capable men generous
wages, as Mr. Walpole avers, why should so many ad-
vertisements for clerks request applicants to “ state
salary required”? Do commercial men adopt this com-
petitive method as a matter of form, or with a view of
