figure offering ?
It was stated by the secretary of the Employers’ Federa-
tion that there was absolutely no evidence to show that
any married men of ability were receiving only 30s. per
week. That was possibly quite true in the carefully
selected places he visited. With sufficiently careful
selection you can avoid finding out almost anything.
The Regular Anti-Federal Falsehood.
One day last week the usual anti-Federal falsehood
appeared in the correspondence column of the
S.M. Herald thus: —
Sir, —As an example of the operations of our circum-
locutory Postal authorities, surely the following will be
difficult to beat. Inverell is 96 miles from Moree, with a
direct line of railway between the two towns, and trains
backwards and forwards every day. Yet, if a letter be
posted in Inverell, addressed to Moree, our postal wise-acres
start it on its journey by sending it 42 miles by coach in
an opposite direction, viz., to Glen Innes. From there it
travels 423 miles by rail to Sydney, and from Sydney it is
sent back 413 miles by train to its destination. The
letter thus travels nearly 900 miles, part of the way by
coach, to reach a town only 96 miles from where it was
posted, whilst at the same time there is direct railway
communication every day between the two places.—l am,
&c., 0. R. Staples.
Inverell, June 1.
And the statement, as usual, is mostly reckless
invention; the anti-Federal person doesn’t seem
to care much what he invents so long as he holds
up the Government and Parliament of Australia,
and through them Australia itself, to the world’s
derision. Here are the facts, which Mr. Staples,
of Inverell, could have found out for himself before
writing, if he had had about him a vestige of what
is sometimes called “British fair play” :
(1) There are about 30 letters a day from Inverell
to Moree.
(2) If a letter is posted at Inverell for Moree, it goes
to Glen Innes by coach, thence by Werris Greek
to Moree. It doesn’t go near Sydney. This knocks 510
miles off the 900-mile lie. The Moree-Inverell line is
only used for mails between Moree and Warialda.
(3) One reason for the circuit, which is nothing like the
circuit Mr. Staples alleges, is that owing to State exac-
tions it would cost £940 per annum to send about 30
letters daily over the 47 miles between Inverell and
Warialda. By the circuitous coach track they are carried
for £2BO a year. With all manner of parochial liars con-
stantly shouting “ Federal extravagance,” the Australian
Government has to economise. The saving helps Carru-
thers’ surplus.
(4) Furthermore, as regards a considerable proportion
of the letters, the circuitous service is the quicker. The
slow, solitary, thrice-a-week train gets into Inverell
late and leaves early, and the average person often
wouldn’t have his reply to the letter from Moree written
in time. And a letter from Inverell to Sydney, if sent by
coach to Glen Innes, can often beat the slow branch
railway by a full day or more. Also the coach, which
starts at 11.30 a.m. from Inverell, often delivers a letter
even at Moree some hours earlier than it would arrive
there going direct by train, seeing that half the
time there is no train that day. It seems to be a
habit of the State railway authorities to consider postal
arrangements as little as possible in fixing train hours.
A slow train that runs only three times a week (at least
that is all the time-table shows) is a mighty poor mail-
carrier, anyway, no matter when it starts. Inverell prob-
ably wants its mail oftener than thrice a week.
The peculiarity of almost every anti-Federal yarn
is that it proves on inspection to be either
a whole lie or three-fourths of a lie. The
anti-Federalist seems to have a code of his
own, which is to the effect that truth, fair-
play, and the general conduct of a gentleman can
be laid aside when the Australian Government is
in question, and that any slander or misrepresenta-
tion is justifiable, provided it brings the ad-
ministration of Australian affairs into con-
tempt. And he never apologises when he
is bowled out—at least not to this paper’s know-
ledge. Apropos, one F. R. Hodges, of Oarrington-
road, Marrickville, who accused Australia
of breach of faith in not fixing the Federal capital
during the life of the first Parliament as “dis-
tinctly provided for in the Constitution ” (these
were his words), doesn’t seem to have either apolo-
gised or explained. Nothing resembling what
he alleged was ever heard of in the Constitution ;
there was consequently no breach of faith, and
the allegation was a baseless slander. But it was
only a baseless slander against Australia, and
to the anti-Federalist it appears rather a merit
than otherwise to falsely slander Australia.
For an unholy tangle many worse things
couldn’t happen than the last Senate election in
S. Australia. Symon and Russell were safe in
the front; only 14 votes separated Vardon and
Crosbie, and Crosbie had the 14. So Vardon
demanded a recount. He got it, and it gave
Vardon 34 votes more than Crosbie. Meantime
Crosbie died; but Blundell, the other Labor
man, who was only about 30 behind, went on
with a petition for the judicial inquiry which the
Commonwealth law provides, instead of the old
farce of inquiry by a Parliamentary Elections
and Qualifications Committee. Justice Barton
turned up to make the inquiry, called for the bal-
lot papers, and found that 9000 of them had been
accidentally burned, and the 9000 included the
votes polled at Houghton, where 55 per cent, of
them had been thrown out as informal because the
local presiding officer-ass had initialled them on
the face. Justice Barton then went fishing, tie
found that, on the papers that hadn’t gone to
blazes, Vardon would only have a majority of
two, but that if he counted the papers that had
been made informal by officer-asses scrawling their
initials in the wrong place, or by not scrawling
them anywhere at all, then Vardon wouldn’t have
a majority at all; so he concluded that Vardon
got his majority, not because a majority wanted
him, but because of the dunderheadedness of the
important local presiding officer-asses. So he
voided the election on the ground of official neglect,
and Crosbie (who is dead) and Vardon (who will
perhaps wish he was dead before the trouble is over)
have got to pay the costs between them. The pre-
siding officer-asses don’t have to pay anything.
And, anyhow, they were the Commonwealth’s
asses—they weren’t Crosbie and Vardon’s team
of mules; and it seems about a fair thing that
every man should pay for the damage done by his
own mule.
N.S.W. Local Premier Carruthers, arguing
against the taking over by the Commonwealth of
the State debts :
The Commonwealth had no better chance in London
than the State. In 1905, when we [that is the State of
N.S.VV.] borrowed two millions, the London Stock Ex-
change “slated” our loan, and made us suffer to the
tune of over 3 per cent., because the Commonwealth had
interfered in regard to the employment of coolies on the
Rand. The stockbrokers openly retaliated on us because
of the policy of the Commonwealth. What a dressing-
down a Commonwealth loan would have got just after
the Six Hatters business, or the Petriana incident, in a
hostile crowd of stockbrokers. It must not be forgotten
that the States have no “ foreign ” policy, and therefore
are not likely to create an unfavorable sentiment; but
the Commonwealth is always irritating the British in-
vestor and capitalist, and will for a long time find that
he can, and will, retaliate if he gets a chance.
Well, admitting that Cohen did “slate”
N.S. W.’s loan because of some alleged iniquity on
the part of the Australian Parliament, Carru-
THERs’ position is something like this :
(1) The States can get better terms from Cohen than
the Commonwealth, because, having no “foreign”
policy, they can’t offend Cohen.
(2) The Commonwealth, having a “ foreign ” policy,
can, and does, offend Cohen; and Cohen “slates” the
States’ loans in retaliation.
(3) Therefore the States can’t get better terms than the
Commonwealth ; and therefore the States should retain
their debts and save the difference which doesn’t exist.
Also, if a Commonwealth loan would have got a
“ dressing down” just after the Six Hatters busi-
ness, &c., who would have been responsible for
the toilet operation? Carruthers knows well
enough that the Six Hatters business, &c., were
perfectly legitimate and harmless acts that
wouldn’t alarm the shyest Cohen, and only the
hard lying of the political gang to which Carru-
thers belongs, and of the press that barracks for
the gang, made them appear anything else. So
when Carruthers says that the people would
have had to pay more to Cohen had they gone
camping on Cohen’s doorstep just after the Six
Hatters business, &c., Carruthers furnishes
another good reason why the people should be
very tired of Carruthers.
“Jimmie Pannikin” aims the clod back again
(the clod has been thrown back and forward so
often that it is just about worn out ):
I hope that “ William Henry Spank ” does not often shed
the garment of affability at this time-o’-year, because it’s
not good to go naked and unashamed. If “W.H.S.”
would oftener don the garment of ability, leaving the
affability to the amiable, he’d be a better critic. At
present he is only a bitter, illogical, untruthful fault-
maker and inventor of defects.
If I were like some States Frights, I might retaliate that
his article describes the Victorian Educational Depart-
mental defects; but has little or no bearing upon the
weak points of the poor, old, robbed Mother State’s
educational administration. Such pars as those he
quotes from the Victorian Teachers’ Gazette have never
appeared in the official Public Instruction Gazette of
N.S.W.
“W.H.S.” next states that the teacher has to prepare
his day’s work—about three hours’ toil, if properly done —
before school. That statement is a gross exaggeration or
an infernal libel. The teacher who finds it necessary to
spend about 15 hours per week preparing his work before
school must surely be a man of merely microscopic
attainments and infinitesimal general reading, not fit to
be allowed to lead little children astray.
Teachers do not talk more “shop” during holidays
than any other professional men. The teacher who
does not try to widen his outlook and add to his stock of
ideas by talking shop at times with his brethren and
sistern, must be a lazy lout incapable of decent intellec-
tual effort—or, worse, a self-satisfied nincompoop.
Men of all callings talk “ shop ” in moderation, if they
are worth their salt. Shop is better than stagnation,
anyhow !
“ W. H. Spank ” writes of “training the kids ungodly
hours for months” to the detriment of his “health and
temper, their school work, and their parents’ cows,” &c.
A teacher who can’t get children ready for a decent school
concert without traiiiing them ungodly hours for months
must know as much about music as a pig does of poetry.
I had 25 years’ experience as a teacher in the Big Scrub
and in the Big City, and I never knew a case where a
teacher had to train pupils ungodly hours for months in
order to give a creditable children’s concert.
“ The average pedagogue isn’t fit to strip in a bath, let
alone a public hall.” Surely this is not true of Victoria!
Even in the outer-back, the teachers, as a rule, are
amongst the most athletic of the community. Physical
culture is doing wonders in N.S.W., because it is carried
on sensibly and systematically. I have known many
fine teachers (male and female) who established libraries
and gardens ; who supplied their schools with pianos,
pictures and physical culture apparatus long - before the
new syllabus, without the nearest approach to crawling.
Some teachers would like to have absolutely everything
done for them. Are we to show no talent for self-help in
reason, no initiative, no tiny self-sacrifice, no love at all
for the little ones whom we teach, no power of organisa-
tion, no ability outside the school? Are young Aus-
tralians to be trained as helpless, hopeless suckers of the
State Milch Oow ? Good old Cow !

“Backstay” writes:
Bulletin pars on the Easby loss and Marine Court In-
quiry should open the public eye to the way some ship-
owners run things.
At the time of the Merchant Service Guild v. Ship-
owners case, in the Federal Arbitration Court, I pointed
out in the Bulletin the necessity of having a proper
chart-room, in spite of Justice O’Connor’s dictum.
Charts are deprived of most of their value without a
proper place to keep them in—a place open to all officers
at all times. When they are in the skipper’s room, an
officer always feels, at best, that he is only on sufferance
when he goes in. Often he doesn’t go when he most
needs, because the skipper is dressing, or yarning with a
passenger. Justice O’Connor, in his judgment, said
most of the new and larger ships had chart-rooms. That
is very wide of the mark. Many that pose as first-class
passenger ships have none—or at best a table on the
bridge that is useless at night or in wet weather, when
charts are most needed. When there is a proper chart-
room, an officer can go and look up the coast and its
dangers, especially on a new' run, at some off-time. He
hasn’t much off-time, but the man who is keen can find
time. He will then keep his watch w'ith more comfort to
himself and safety to the ship.
The Easby case is so bad that I hope it is unusual. Two
pounds (£2) would buy all the charts absolutely necessary
on its regular run. Not much, seeing that it makes for
the safety of several thousand pounds’ worth of property,
Of course, the lives don’t count. They’re only sailors,
who dre paid to take risks.
The Court of Inquiry had a good chance to bring the
ship in as unseaworthy, being without so necessary a part
of its equipment. There would have been much more
justice in that than in dropping on the unfortunate and
overworked skipper as it did. The idea of the ship-
ow'ner running round, with his hat off, after the man with
a certificate, is no w'ild dream of the future. The shortage
is right here with us. A few months ago a man I know
asked for his holiday. It was granted, and he made all
arrangements, but no one could be found to relieve him,
so he had to go another trip. Not long ago, men in deep-
water ships were all trying to get on this coast. Now,
with wages in China higher than ours, and English and
Indian wages going up, they’re not rushing us.
Carruthers defeated S.A. Premier Price’s
sensible motion, at the Brisbane Premiers’ Con-
ference, that local men should be appointed as
State Governors, with a view to saving some of
the £50,000 or so now wasted on pomp and
frill for the imported Ex. Carruthers nearly
dropped dead with horror at the idea of the
Commonwealth having a real live lord from Eng-
land as Governor, while his blessed Ma State
would have to be satisfied with a plain local
person as official Pump. Opener and Stamp
Affixer.
Dear Bulletin, —Father has had a busy week amongst
the shipping’ fraternity. The old man does not take long
to learn a new business. He had not been on board a ship
10 minutes before he knew how to “ splice the main brace ”
an i he can tell “ when the sun lies over the fore-yard
arm ”to a minute. He explained to a meeting of ship-
masters that disease was carried round the world in ships.
We should have no plague if it were not for the ships,
therefore, it is important that the ships should be
thoroughly disinfected. The present system was obsolete.
It is not in use in any hospital or any place where they
know anything about disease. The proper way to dis-
infect a ship is with Tyree Spray and Misicible carbolic.
He also pointed out the advantage of keeping the holds
whitewashed, as it meant a saving of labor in handling
the cargo—the reflected light allowed the men to see what
they were doing, besides which it kept the ship clean and
wholesome looking. The machine could also be used for
enamelling, painting or spraying tar—this would preserve
the iron from rust. Father advises every ship owner or
ship master to write at once to Tyree, 314 George-street,
for full particulars. Yours faithfully, Bill Williams.
N.B.—Or write to the agents— Tyree Automatic Spray
Offices, Block Place, Melbourne ; Tyree Automatic Spray
Offices, 26 Adelaide-street Brisbane; Tyree Automatic
Spray Offices, Waymouth-street, Adelaide.*

Perhaps Wagner, too, can be put into plain
Australian. Well, Tannhauser was a fair-class
fighting man, but his real gift was singing. When
he rolled his eyes around and bleated “Queen of
My Heart,” there wasn’t a feminine pulse that
did not flutter. Tannhauser was in the swell
class, and his special fancy was Elizabeth, niece
of the Governor, whom he had met at Govern-
ment House; and who was keen on him.
She used to accompany his songs, and it
looked like a fashionable wedding. But Tann-
hauser had a wild strain in him. There was a
barmaid with yellow hair and 15 different
pneumonia blouses, each one wickeder than
the other, who had taken rather a liking
for his top-note and his fine romantic eye.
The end was that he took the barmaid away to
Heligoland (which is German for Tasmania), and
they had a high old time together until he’d got
tired of her stock of slang and knew all the
15 pneumonia blouses by heart. Tannhauser
dropped her then, and the lady threw some fine
language at him as he left. He came back to his
own crowd, and found that the music fever was so
strong that there was to be a song and recitation
competition at Government House with the hand
of Elizabeth as the prize—infant phenomena
barred from the competition out of consideration
for the prize’s feelings. Everybody had to sing
impromptu on love. Tannhauser chummed up
again with Elizabeth (who hadn’t heard of the
trip to Heligoland), and entered for the competi-
tion. She reckoned he was a dead bird, because his
top-note could beat anything behind pace in the
country, and he was to be a trier. The night of
the competition, one singer started off with a
milk-and-water lyric on love, which was all pappy-
goose and green melons. Tannhauser followed in
a warmer tone, and Elizabeth looked pleased.
But the other competitors were offended, and
there was a musical melee, and they said that
Tannhauser was “ off-side,” and knew more about
love than he ought to. Tannhauser at this fired
up and threw off his singing chest a few real
throbs. The temperature went up to 102° Fahren-
heit. They ragged him as to how he knew so
much, and'he let out all about the Heligoland
trip, and the barmaid with the 15 pneumonia
clothes. Then there was a rumpus. They were
going to disqualify Tannhauser for life, but Eliza-
beth, who didn’t seem nearly as much shocked as
she should have been, interfered, and he was given
another chance. Things got miserable after that.
Tannhauser tried to forget the baimaid and
couldn’t, and Elizabeth died of grief at his gene-
ral unsatisfactoriness; and then he died too, out
of sympathy. That’s the Tannhauser story.
* * * *
As presented at Sydney Royal on Satur-
day, ‘ ‘ Tannhauser ’ ’ marked the sum mit of achieve-
ment of Mr. Musgrove’s Grand Opera Co., so far.
True, one of the principals, Mohwinkel, was
suddenly afflicted by illness, and the important
part of the Landgrave of Thuringia had to be
taken at very short notice by Emil Greder ; but
it is difficult to imagine that this was a handicap,
so fine was Greder s acting and singing. Indeed
he, with Miss Sara Anderson, the chorus, and the
orchestra, supplied the most artistic bit of the
evening—the reception of nobles in the Hall of
the Muses to the music of the famous march.
The lady not alone sings—she acts with grace, and
it was only when these two held the stage together
that the opera had a fully harmonious present-
ment. The first act suggested unfavorable com-
parisons with a previous production in Sydney.
Miss Yallerie Waller was not equal to the music
of Venus. The tenor, Karl Studemuna, has a
sweet resonant voice, which, at times, reaches the
point of perfection ; but he cannot satisfactorily
present “Tannhauser.” His appearance is an
obstacle. It is not merely that he is so well
nourished, but his gait and acting are ungainly.
To close the eyes and listen to his voice is a
pleasure; to watch his acting is a penance.
And these heroic figures of Wagnerian opera
in their carpet-design clothes need to be “in
the picture” in every detail. With the second
act there was a markedly higher level in the
production. Miss Sara Anderson has given
way a little to that remorseless enemy of the
great singer, and has no girlish figure; but
she is a consummate artist in dressing, make-up,
and acting, and her firm, strong fresh voice was
subjected to no handicap by her appearance.
Greder as the Landgrave, as before remarked,
was excellent. Fitz Rupp cut a fine figure as
Wolfram, and the voice of Tannhauser was always
melodious and buoyant. The work of the chorus
and orchestra was generally good, and conductor
Slapoffski was heartily applauded at the conclu-
sion of the overture.
* * * *
The Royal Grand Opera Co. dropped from
Grandeur to Humor when it put forth “The
Barber of Seville ” to shave the serious expression
off Sydney’s face. Whether the jump was up or
down is a matter of individual opinion. Some
persons would as soon part with their seriousness as
with their whiskers. Rossini’s jolly swash-
buckling opera was made in Italy 100 years
ago, and it has not been done in Sydney
for a long, long time. The Germans were
a little heavy over the mercurial music
and acting. Raking over the ashes of mode-
rately ancient musical history, the audience
at “The Barber” came on memories of
Rossini that convinced it that he ripened
in a fit environment for “The Barber.”
His father was town trumpeter and inspector of
slaughter-houses. The combination is stagger-
ingly humorous. His mother was a baker’s
daughter (Ophelia said the same of the owl).
The jolly trumpeter got into gaol for political
reasons, and his wife went forth and
made her crust as a prima donna, while
her child remained with a pork butcher.
The youngster clearly inherited his love of music,
and a musician who taught the harp, and had
also a license to sell liquor (almost as curious a
combination as the trumpeter and meat inspector),
used to give him lessons. The harpist-liquorist
used to fall asleep standing up ; and when little
Rossini had got his fill of fun and music out of his
teacher, he was apprenticed to a smith. But music
will out, and from banging the anvil he took to
banging the piano—such as it was then. Out of
his comic environment the grown Rossini pro-

duced “The Barber of Seville.” It has a large
number of thefts embedded in its orchestration,
but if the original owners didn’t complain, no one
in Sydney is going to take up the club in
defence of such very dead composers.
* * * *
Figaro, the Barber, shaves the quality of
Seville, does its hair, and is also surgeon to it and
its beasts. He is a merry scallywag, and when
he finds a patron, Count Almaviva, to be in love
with a certain Rosina, whose acquaintance he
cannot make because her guardian himself intends
to marry her, the humorous vet-razor-wielder
puts him up to tricks to outwit the guardian.
The Count is a drunken soldier, pretending
to be billeted on guardian Bartolo ; then he is a
music master, and gives the fair Rosina lessons in
love. Presently Rosina becomes jealous, and
agrees to marry her old guardian. He brings in
a notary to celebrate the union, but the irre-
pressible Figaro and the Count contrive to be
there, and while Bartolo goes for the police
Rosina and the Count are married ; and all is
happiness and sitting on one chair. Mr. Massimi,
a light, pleasant-voiced tenor, who took the r6le
of Count Almaviva, sang in Italian, but he
swashbuckled and made love like a German.
Excepting a chorus (and the Massimi’s songs) the
opera was done in German. Miss Stoller sang
the highly-flourished, trilled, and ring-tailed love
song, with which she opened the second act, in her
best style—which is somewhat tliin. Fritz Rupp
was the jolly surgeon-vet-barber ; Hans Mohwin-
kel the music master ; Emil Greder the easily-de-
ceived guardian. Next week the management
will probably produce “Faust” and “Rigoletto”
—old-young favorites that the public knows,
and of which it does not weary.
* ■* m •
“ Hansel and Gretel,” the matinee opera,
was seen, for the first time in Sydney, last
Wednesday. It is a jewel —say a fine cat’s-eye—
among the Grand collection of the co. It
was sung in English, but the track of the
German tongue is so well blazed that
the principals might as well have used
their own Deutsch. The opera has some
delightful airs—as, for instance, the cuckoo
duet, and the “ Evening Prayer” of the ragged
children. The witch, who eats little children
after baking them into ginger-bread, the
broom-maker and his wife, their tattered
kiddies Hansel and Gretel, the Hew Fairy,
the Sleep Fairy, and the march down from
Heaven of the shining angel band—all these
make up an attractive series of pictures. The
poor broom-maker’s hut, and the witch’s ginger-
bread villa, and the oven in which she bakes the
babes, are splendidly staged.

Saturday will see the production of Boucicault’s
“Colleen Bawn.” Miss Beatrice Holloway will
be the Eily O’Connor of old Hibernian legend.
«* * *
The Philistine sees a good deal of likeness
between Wagner and “The Spring Chicken.”
There are times when you almost suspect that
Wagner is going to break out into a tune; and m
“The Spring Chicken” there are moments when
you can detect symptoms of a plot. Then, how-
ever, the immature bird seems to recoil from the
thought of anything so serious, and dashes off
after the first worm dropped in its path by any
of a dozen or so Providences —from the French-
man who supplied the original naughtiness which
so shocked St. Stead in London, down (or up) to
G. Lauri, who jokes about things no further away
than Market-street. Considering the number of
Providences who assist, the chicken gets amighty
poor breakfast. Possibly the naughty worm
that horrified Stead was the best worm; but
it was evidently dropped overboard in the
tropics, and only the faintest odor of it now hangs
about the chicken’s plumage. (Not very gay
plumage either, by the way—the Williamson
management certainly cannot be accused of rush-
ing into extravagance lately.) The story, so far
as there is one, relates to a young French advo-
cate who, on the arrival of Spring, cuts off his
beard and, to the pain of his little wife, proceeds
to shake a loose leg. For eight months of the
year the days in his almanac are marked off in
sober family colors; for the other four months
they are all blue. Then, of course, there is the
mother-in-law, who arrives on the unexpected
visit, and who, out of the fulness of her ex-
periences with her own Girdle, who is Lauri,
knows how to treat such Spring friskiness—
with a sleeping draught. But, unfortunately
for the good name of the mother in-law,
she innocently swallows some cf the
“elixir of youth” that Girdle bought when he
bought also the railway ticket to Paris—and then
the curtain falls, with an amorous mother-in-law
pursuing the amazed Girdle, who has had too
many sleeping draughts forced upon him to be
equal to the outburst.

In the production at Her Majesty’s, Syd-
ney, most of the old “ Royal Comics ” are in the
places that knew them in Melbourne before
the pantomime came to interrupt them ;
but Olive Morrell having gone out of the
Williamson casts, one or two changes are con-
sequent. Miss Alma Barber takes the imported
lady’s part of the little wife who has eight
months of happiness and four months of Spring;
and Miss Irby Marshall comes over from the
Julius Knight co. to play the giddy princess who
pays with a Spring kiss for the legal advice she
gets in her suit for divorce. Miss Barber’s sweet
little voice and clear enunciation suits So
Gently,” the most “catching” of all the lyrics.
\s in Miss Clara Clifton’s principal number
(Miss Clifton is of necessity the mother-in-law),
the words count for more than the music. Nothing
except the Rotterdam children’s ballet is encored
so often as Miss Barber’s expressive song ; which
gives Miss Barber much walking back and forth,
and she can do almost anything on the stage
better than she can walk. Miss Florence Young,
vigorous as usual, struggles hard to overcome the
banality of the words she has to sing ; Lauri, be-
sides his frivolling, has rather more than usual of
singing and, with Arthur Crane, makes one of
the successes of the piece in a ditty which dis-
cusses the relative merits of being under 40 and
over • Reg Roberts, in the chief tenor part—he is
the lawyer of the two seasons —sings the most
solid numbers of the extravaganza in excellent
voice • and the chorus and the ballet, as usual,
deserve to have each a line to itself very near the
ton of the programme, along with an artistically
made-up Leslie, who is a better Leslie with the
arrival of each new season.


Modern Saw Mill Machinery.
WILLIAM FLEMING, Equitable Building, Sydney.
“The Squatter’s Daughter, at Sydney
Cri., demonstrates with each succeeding week
what a mighty demand there is for the home-
made dramatic article hereabouts. .Just as the
“resting” actor amuses himself going to see
plays, so the way back visitor to Sydney hankers,
while on his holiday, to gaze once more upon the
primary industries he has left behind him. “The
Squatter’s Daughter ” fills that want; and hence
the sheep-shearing and wood-chopping scenes
continue to draw crowds nightly. If William
could only manage to introduce in the drama
some dummy selectors, half-a-dozen poison carts,
and a crate or so of dead rabbits, its run would be as
interminable as a Yankee murder trial when the
defendant has much money.
*# # #
Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society, with
Hazon at the helm, had a brilliant night last
Friday at the second concert of its season. The
Funeral March from Hamlet” was played in
memory of the late musicians, Vollmar and
Delaney. The piece of the programme was the
Beethoven Concerto achievement, known as “The
Emperor,” with Mrs. Carreno at the Steinway.
Excerpts from the “Queen of Sheba” and a
Lisztian Fantasy made rivers of melody. Mr.
Bryce Carter gave a violincello solo. In fact, the
programme was heavily jewelled, and so was the
audience, beginning with Lady Northcote.
#* * *
Genius Marie Hall and her marvellous
fiddle will be seen and heard in Sydney at the
Town Hall next Saturday night. She is acclaimed
as one of the first violinists of the day, and as
there are only four or five first- class fiddlers
scraping around in Europe, little Marie must be a
genius to have arrived in the van with her imple-
ment in her small white hand while still in the
flower of early youth.
** * *
The performing cats make Sydney Tivoli
matinees popular these days, and the Castlereagh-
street house has, more or less, taken the place of
the pantomime as the reward of the boy who
cleans up the yard nicely on Saturday morning.
The only feature of the bill that wasn’t also a
feature last week is her Wonderland Majesty
Gracie Graham, who is back in N.S. Wales from
Victoria and other such-like poor, foreign parts,
where the sun of Carruthers does not shine.
Ernest Fitts is also there, warbling in the old,
vigorous way ; but Fitts is too big and brawny
nowadays to be a feature—he is almost a whole
landscape.
“ Mother Goose ” was due again in Mel-
bourne yesterday, and, if the advertisement told
truly, the Williamsonian pantomime is now flap-
ping its “pinions of popularity” (a managerial
figure of speech) at the Princess’s. The cast of
the Goose characters has been considerably
changed, and the construction of its “ plot ” is said
to be different from that of the Christmas Goose,
which Melbourne remembers. It will put in nine
performances, including two matinees. On the
22nd “The Blue Moon ” is to shine upon a newly-
organised comic opera co.
#* * *
“The Virginian,” in its dramatised form,
is a riddle to most people who have never read the
book ; therefore Melbourne is about to give it up.
The one thing in the Yankee drama which every-
body can understand is the awful wickedness of
Trampas. He makes a crafty attempt to suggest
Robinson Crusoe in his hairy lower garments, but
from the waist upwards he looks like a cross
between the Guy Fawkes of Fifth o’ November
fiction and the Assistant Torturer in “ The Yeo-
man of the Guard.” Also he is not unremindful
of Jonathan Wilde. Although humanity at large
has a sneaking fondness for a whole-souled villain
who frankly declares himself in his countenance
and his costume, common sense demands a brief
explanation of his antipathy to the Hero. The
authors of “The Virginian” forgot to explain
what’s the matter with Trampas. He isn’t pro-
perly “ interdooced,” as the Hero would say. His
murderous intentions are sticking out a mile, but
their origin remains wrapped in mystery. An
incidental reference to “frogs” is equally un-
satisfactory. The “ frogs ” story is puerile in the
novel and vaguely nonsensical in the play. Other
things, notably the clucking hen Emily, are
likewise ridiculous, and so, notwithstanding its
Wild Western atmosphere, “The Virginian”
will mix those babies up for the last time on
Friday. On Saturday a revival of “ The Squaw
Man ” will supply a want at Her Majesty’s.
Melbourne has not exhausted its liking for “ The
Squaw Man,” or certain parts of it, and when the
feathered Chieftain comes back, with his weird
language and his savage grunt, a crowded house
will give him a cordial greeting.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal a revival of “In
London Town” is to oust “The Great Mil-
lionaire ”on Saturday. All citizens desirous of
feeding their loyalty with the spectacle of King
E l ward and his Queen in their golden crowns
are herewith exhorted to seize the present oppor-
tunity to-night or to-morrow. The Royal Pair
defy contradiction. King Edward has a con-
vincing grey beard ; the Queen doesn’t look a day
more than five and twenty. And their haughty
manner of accepting homage indicates that Bland
Holt is paying them an enormous salary.
** * *
At the instance of General Request, who
has a finer ear for music than even his brother-in-
arms, General Desire, two extra recitals were
supplied by Marie Hall at Melbourne
Town Hall, making six in all. It may be
said of the interesting violiniste that her
only fault is the one she will inevit-
ably grow out of. She is a very young
woman, and although her skill commands the re-
verence of respectable old men fiddlers, her method
seems not quite ripe. The performance is charm-
ing, but not emotional in a marked degree. When
her violin is overcome by the pathos of the musical
situation there are tears in its notes —but it weeps
rather too quietly. Its tender passions are ex-
pressed with the same reserve. A Town Hall
performance needs to be a trifle melodramatic.
* * *
Albani, supported by a company of five, is
giving three concerts at the Town Hall this week,
and the last of them happens to-morrow. The city,
knowing what to expect of Albani’s voice, was in
no danger of being disappointed with the ruins of
her renowned warble. On the other hand, a good
deal was expected from the company, and this
notice, written before the first concert, predicts a
pleasing success for the strangers whose names
are not typed large on the programme.

The long, lean Carlton, who talks and
frivols with a pack of cards, is leaving the Opera
House this week. Granville, the ranting char-
acter impersonator, came to hand at the same
time as Carlton, and he could better have been
spared—but the passing of Granville is un-
accountably delayed. All this time the two
Svengalis on the stage are receiving instructions
from the Svengali whom they can’t hear or see.
He formerly confined his rambles to the ground
floor, but he now goes to the d.c. and amphi-
theatre for orders, which are promptly forwarded
to the stage by magic or tick-tackograph. It
seems that the firm’s capacity fgr executing
orders is limited to the tunes and “ make-ups ”
that are kept in stock. The Svengalis are but
mortal. If they haven’t got what you want, they
give you something different.

“ 0.K.” writes :
In Melbourne’s highah musical circles the name of
Town Olerk Clayton is Mud these times, because he
persists in reserving the Town Hall on Saturday nights
for recitals by the municipal organist. The prevailing
soreness on this point has a humorous aspect. Firstly,
there was a mild protest against the re-engagement of
the organist, Dr. Price, from Ireland, on the ground that
the billet could be as capably filled by a local man. Now,
there is an objection raised to the profitable Saturday
night organ recitals, as being unfair to other kinds of im-
ported musicians—instrumentalists or singers—whose
managers want the Town Hall on the same night for
“oof’’-making purposes. From the City Fathers’point
of view, it is better for them to run their own show on
any night if they can make more profit than the £2O they
would get in Hall rental from an oujside speculator
From the general public point of view, it is better to
enrich the municipality, if the profit tends to city im-
provements, than to load the coffers of professional show
men and their foreign “stars.” As for those musical
people who are said to miss the chance of bearing fine
artists because they cannot hear them on the one night
when Price plays the organ—their wail seems hardly
worth considering. Other chances are open to them,
anyhow. And if the musical education of the “ masses ”
is a thing to be muchly desired, nobody can deny that
the masses derive more benefit from sixpenny organ re-
citals, which they do attend in pretty large numbers,
than they would obtain from comparatively expensive
performances of sonatas and concertos, whereat they are
not present. Seeing that the Town Clerk’s policy happens
to be socialistic as well as business-like, it is highly com-
mendable, and if it should result in the building of a
special Melbourne concert hall for special entertainments,
the public all round will be the gainers. For in that case
the supporters of expensive entertainments will get
decent accommodation in a suitable building, whilst the
Town Hall will become a recognised shilling show place,
comfortable only when it is well filled. Under present
conditions, it is mostly a cheerless building, where musi-
cal enthusiasts catch cold.
** # #
It is hardly possible to make a more complete
change than the Tittell Brune Co. has made at
Adelaide Royal. On Friday evening it was pre-
senting the magnificently-staged “ Parsifal, ” with
its vague chronology, its high-flown sentiment,
and its triumph of Virtue after a long, careful
look at what Vice could offer in the meantime.
On Saturday it shed its armor and cloaks,
beards and long locks, and declined on to the
gifted Hebrew’s “Merely Mary Ann,” which
is as modern as it can be made. This
writer sees no reason to alter a previous
conviction that Miss Brune is a better
comedian than tragedian. The booking at the
Royal has been enormous, but Birthday Cup
week has kept a number of other attractions
going, and well patronised too. Ada Ward
faded quietly off the local horizon. Adelaide
may be a Holy City, but its piety is very
practical, and it is little given to losing its head
over sensational preachers and preacheresses.

“ The Oof Bird ” is a term that once
Came into vogue because
Its meaning tempted every dunce
To guess at what it was.
Fools nursed the term in former years
Ere letting it run loose ;
“ The Oof Bird ” nowadays appears
As good old “ Mother Goose.”
The creature lays big golden eggs
Of pantomimic breed ;
It travels on a lot of legs,
Just like a centipede.
The house wherein its songs are heard
Is filled from floor to roof,
And whilst the public cheer the Bird
The showman bags the Oof.

“ Peacock ”: Went into a high-class restaurant t’other
day. I wanted only food and rest. In the middle of a
trout, my repose was disturbed by a buzz of excitement
all over the room, and looking up I saw three red feathers
coming round the corner. It was Tittell Brune. and
train. She gave a ravishing bow to a young fellow in the
corner. He immediately spilt his coffee and puffed
violently at the lighted end of his cigarette. Things
hummed. A bevy of girls at the next table blushed and
beamed. Fancy sitting next to Miss Brune ! The pro-
prietor rubbed his hands gleefully, but the waiter mopped
a perspiring brow. The responsibility was too much for
him. Tittell Boom was gracious to her train. Her face
was a study of emotions, and her arms floated here and
there expressively. Mummer Kingston put in some good
work, sidestepping Tittell’s left hand. He appeared to
be used to it. Then the band played a Hungarian waltz,
which brought me to the conclusion that high-class
restaurants are mainly intended for stage people to act in
Some two months ago the London music-hall world was
deeply moved. An American Trust had been formed to
control variety business in the States, and unheard-
of salaries were offered. Many- performers accepted
engagements which promised in a short time to
place them on a pinnacle of wealth. Harry Lauder,
a Scotch comedian very popular in London, was
engaged at a salary three times as great as he could have
hoped for in the United Kingdom. Everything was lovely
until there arose an iconoclast, a well-known New York
theatrical agent, who cabled to London, and his cable was
published: “Keep my firm out of the newspapers. You
know nothing. All salaries mentioned are smoke. People
dreaming fairy tales and tremendous salaries may wake up
soon with tremendous headaches, and those who have
accepted extraordinary salaries are not over their troubles
yet.” Somehow, the gentleman who wanted his name
kept out of the papers got into the papers, name and all, so
there is likely to be an exciting time ahead of him. He
doesn’t say whereabouts lies the hole in the magnificent
Yankee agreements, but some worried comedians would
much like to know.
“ Adjong ” : I’m not puzzled as to whether an actress can
be a Christian ; what I am puzzled about is how the lady
who married Hugh Fisher, nearly 30 years ago, can still
be the Miss Ada Ward she says she is. I know an actress
can’t be a married woman. Can’t an evangelist either ?
“D.”: Can any one tell me why, under the stress of
acute emotion, our only Tittell loses all her whilom
gracefulness? In both the tearful “farewells” which I
have seen her make of late, she developed a curious
action like unto nothing on earth so much as the awk-
ward forelock-pulling bowings of the honest old mariner
of melodrama —he of the simple faith and the great
bullock-like Heart. Why should Tittell farewell in this
fashion? Artistically speaking, her antics constitute a
misdemeanor within the signification of the Act.
Manager Anderson is already sole lessee of Perth’s only
two theatres —Her Majesty’s and the Royal—and will add
the scalp of His Majesty’s, Fremantle, to his belt, with a
long lease, in a few weeks. And Anderson’s star hasn’t,
apparently, reached its zenith even yet.
Stated as a fact that Eugenie Duggan will take an
innings as a “star” actress in “Camille,” and other
Bernhardtian plays, at Melbourne Princess’s next year—
six plays in six weeks, starting from Easter Saturday.
The thing is to be done “ regardless of expense ” with a
picked Australian company. Which sounds more probable
than a previous story, to the effect that Miss Duggan was
going to burst forth as a tragedienne in London.
Marie Narelle and co. make ready for a'joyous Indian
jaunt. The blue-eyed tenor Fentress, whose platform
simper cruelly belies him, is chummy with a maharajah
of immense tonnage, and the potentate has invited them
to come and kill tigers and time, and have a period of
great joy in his warm-colored country. The concerts are
to be merely incidental.
“Blister”: The most inartistic thing I have seen this
year is the “ Rescue of Varischetti ” act by one Howard
Edie, a long-haired mummer with a genius for advertise-
ment. I call it absurd to make Varischetti rage
like a madman underground, even in the dream
scene, where allowances can be made for the bellow-
dramatic instinct. At present the “ Rescue ” is a one-
man play, but Edie threatens to build a drama on a
grand scale round it, and storm Australia with the dread-
ful outfit.
The funniest thing on at Melbourne Opera House is the
impersonation of Bill Squires by the Svengali, a youthful
German who reproduces great men of the present age and
the near past at the request of the audience, said request
being conveyed to him by the alleged will power of an
elderly Teuton. The impersonator is slim and pale, and
has the general appearance of a curate who lives on
lentils and celery soup. Consequently his William
Squires is a cross between W. H. Judkins and a pre-
Raphaelite female. It is the wildest absurdity, and
resembles anything else on earth, from a picket fence to
the landing of William of Orange, but never carries the
faintest hint of William Squires.
A performer at a little Melbourne variety hall brands
himself “ the melancholy comedian.” It is about time
some of the others owned up to it.
“ Hassan ”: I saw the Druids’ procession. It was
bosker. First there came a cart drawn by several dis-
gusted-looking horses, with ribbons fluttering into their
eyes and tickling' their ears. In the cart were
foolish persons in nightdresses and long property
whiskers. I asked the man next to me whether it was an
advertisement for hair-tonic, and he said “ No, these
are Druids.” Then came a lot of harmless lunatics with
red-and-white w'aistcoats, worn outside their coats, and
a couple of policemen seeing they didn’t escape. Next
came drags full of the Druids’ missuses and tarts and
daughters—some of them not bad-looking, which is
more than can be said for their pas. More Druids fol-
lowed, holding up their nighties from the mud, and
dodging the trams, and cussing the rude little boys who
poked borak at them. More female perks of Druidism—
I think I’ll join the religion if the whiskers are not
obligatory. The quaintest item was a large cardboard
cromlech, which wobbled so that two Druids in vermi-
lion beards had to hold it on the cart. I expected to see
Boadicea coming along any minute, bleeding from the
Roman rods, but she evidently hadn’t recovered suffici-
ently to take part in the tomfoolery. It’s a strange
desire, that of dressing one’s self in a property beard and
a nightdress, or a scarlet waistcoat worn outside one’s
coat.
“ J.E.M.”: There is a glaring mistake in Tittell Brune's
version of “La Tosca.” In the torture scene Miss Brune
wears a gold and blue evening dress. The act concludes
with Tosca and her lover both fainting 1 . Tosca walks
over to the only mat in the room, and faints exactly in
the middle of it, as if she were a geometrical problem.
Scarpia has them taken to the castle of St. Angelo, which
is shown in the next scene. It is only supposed to be an
hour or two later, and Tosca has not even had time to
find out where she is. She didn’t faint with a carpet-
bag full of clothes in her hand. There is no mention of
Scarpia getting her measured for new clothes while she
was insensible, nor does it appear that he sent his minions
to her boarding-house for a heap of duds. And in any case
the girl is too distracted to worry about getting into a
new skirt and blouse. Yet, when Tittell appears, as the
anguished captive of St. Angelo, she has changed her
clothes and jewellery, and wears the floppy white dress
which convention demands at the tail-end and caudal ap-
pendage of a tragedy. How much more effective would have
been the glittering ball dress contrasting with the haggard
faceand dishevelled hair, and theroseson the breast droop-
ing and faded ! Miss Brune invariably wears the wrong
clothes, and gets tangled up in them every time she
moves. She always gives me the idea that she went
around in a state of nature before she went on the stage,
and lived in a tree, like the hero of Bill Liverpool’s book,
“ Before Adam,” which, by the way, is a darn poor book,
despite the monkey footprints on the cover.
San Francisco Argonaut relates that when the Ameri-
can actor, Richard Mansfield, was going to the theatre
one night, a small bull-terrier ran in front of his automo-
bile and was demolished. The machine was stopped, and
an irate old gentleman appeared, to whom Mansfield
made his apologies. “Your dog ran in front of the car
so suddenly that it was impossible to avoid the accident,”
he said. “ That dog,” said the angry ancient, “ was
worth 500dols.” “Well,” replied the actor, “the dog
evidently didn’t know it.”
Singing comedian Alexander, Torrey’s associate, is not
likely to visit Australia again. He has become a bloated
capitalist of very great bloat. The Alexanders went
from Sydney to San Francisco, and there awaited Mrs.
Alexander’s mother, widow of Richard Cadbury, the rich
cocoa man, of Birmingham. Mrs. Cadbury had been on
a visit to another daughter, wife of a missionary in China.
She was an aged lady, and she fell down the gangway of
the steamer on the return voyage, and died of her in-
juries. A great share in the cocoa business thus devolves
upon Mrs. Alexander, and doubtless her husband, the
one-time singing missioner, will find himself under the
painful necessity of remaining in Birmingham to super-
vise it, and gather in the profits.
A visiting American writer tells the Yankee papers that
Australia wants only the coarsest kind of theatrical stingo,
and suggests : —“ There is a lot of money down here for
some enterprising American manager who will bring a
repertoire of the worst plays to be found in America with
a company selected from the Rialto, composed of third-
rate actors. Not many months ago Dion Boucicault and
Lionel Brough brought from England a first-class com-
pany of London players, and a repertoire consisting of
such high-grade plays as ‘ The Second Mrs. Tanqueray,’
‘ Mrs. Gorringe’s Necklace,’ ‘ The Profligate,’ and other
Pinero successes, and, during their eight weeks’ stay,
they did not take enough to pay for the rental of the
theatre. Society plays are not wanted here, because
there is practically no society in Australia.” The gentle-
man is probably as much “ out ” in his forecast as in his
history ; so that American manager had better not bring
his third-raters. Yankee first-raters even don’t always
shine here.
“ Lil.” : Albani has lately declared that she did not
take her name from Albany (New York), where her sing-
ing first attracted notice. Her Italian tutor, looking
round for something in his own lingo to replace her
original name, Lajeunesse, picked on Albani by mere
chance. When the Albany newspapers accepted the
christening as a compliment to their town, the notion
seemed too good an advertisement to be contradicted.
So this base confidence trick was played on the gentle
folk of Albany for many years. The lady’s peculiar matri-
monial “ monniker,” Gye, is officially ordered to be pro-
nounced “Ji.” Anyone speaking it as if it were spelled
Gay Eye is liable to be treated with the utmost rigor at
the box-office.
The ordinary English actor nowadays is a person who
lacks the equipment or the audacity (which is a necessary
item of even the best equipment) to carry him through a
successful music-hall “ turn.” The halls pay better
salaries than the theatres pay, and the work of a “turn”
is much easier, and the job more stiddy than that of a
part actor in plays. Consider the sketch artists imported
by Rickards. How short a time they are on the stage,
how long a time they stick at the same songs, dances, or
what not—especially what not—and how handsomely
they are paid in comparison with the stock actor! But
the variety person has to go on and face the raging public
lion single-handed; whereas even the star, standing in
the limelight, has somebody on the stage with him to
prop up his loneliness, and restrain the sinking sensation
that might otherwise make him imagine his stomach was
going swiftly down a lift on its lonesome.
“ Gebo ” : I was at Rickards’, in Melbourne, the other
Saturday night (up in the gallery), and they were showing
a series of illustrated pictures. One was of Phil May ;it
represented him as sitting back in a folding chair. When
it was shown a general shout of applause happened, and
one of the crowd greatly enlightened those around, in-
cluding myself. He said that May was “ the lazy beggar
who stayed in bed for 29 years, and did no work.” I
began questioning. “Yes, Phil May, I remember the
name well,” said the wild galleryite. “Never stirred out
of his bed for 29 years ! ” “ How did he live ? ” I asked.
“ Oh, I don’t know! He had money or something, and
made his mother keep him ! The police had to come and
pull him out of bed ! I don’t know why they’re shoving
him on here ! ” I have heard a good many reminiscences
of Phil May, and had an idea I knew his pedigree, but I’d
never heard that before. It struck me as humorous.
Sara Bernhardt is evidently getting thinner in the
skin, else she wouldn’t worry about a little thing like an
attack on the originality of her pen-work. Seventeen
years ago Archibald Forbes wrote venomously about her
as a drunken profligate, who was fast breaking up (the
S. Aus. Advertiser, among others, printed this par-
ticular yarn of Forbes’s) and all Sara did was to talk back
for half-an-hour —and live and work for 17 years to scoff
at the story.
PAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Soothing sounds that throb and thrill
Through the murk of my distress ;
Gall and wormwood, grief and ill,
Where is now thy bitterness ?
Rancor, railing, loud disputes,
Venomed smiles satirical,
Fade before the rippling lute’s
Laughing notes and lyrical.
Chords triumphant thunder out,
Pealing, rhythmic, belled, sublime ;
Warrior angels seem to shout,
Marching in the van of Time
Like the rage the billow brings
Roaring from a stormy sea,
Like the rush of mighty wings
Rolls the Jovian melody.
Wailing voices, tense and thin,
Full of woe and full of fears,
Quivering, grieving violin,
Sobbing out its soul in tears ;
Moaning to the heavens above,
Mourning to the aeons grey,
Weeping for the death of love ;
Who shall wipe our tears away?
Happy, careless, dancing notes,
Simple music free and wild,
Glad and pure it soars and floats
Like the laughter of a child ;
As the flowers that deck our path,
All is innocence and truth ;
No o’ershadowed aftermath
Waits for sunny halcyon youth.
Mystic music witch-like broods—
See green branches dip and sway ;
Fawns and Satyrs in the woods
Make their pagan holiday ;
Classic orgies wake the glades,
Pulsing warm to Love’s refrain ;
Wizard Pan, from out the shade,
Pipes, and all is changed again.

“T.S.” : Apropos of Ada Ward and her
famous query, has anybody ever raised the ques-
tion “Can the Devil be a Christian?” When I
write my famous essay on “How to be Happy
though Buried ” I shall deal with this matter as a
side issue.
The other morning seven Melbourne de-
tectives rushed down to the Mint in a hurry, to
clear up a mystery and arrest some one. The
Mint allows gold dealers to deposit their boodle
at all hours, but after the establishment is closed
for the day they plant it at their own risk in a
safe in the guard’s lodge at the gate, where a con-
stable is always on duty. At 10 p.m. on June
3rd, Pascoe, a Bendigo gold-buyetj stepped in
with three bars of gold, worth some £l7OO. These
were put in the safe by constable Barclay, who
locked the apparatus, stowed the key in his Vest
pocket, and shortly afterwards went to bed.
During the night the lodge office was occu-
pied by a constable, who only left it twice,
for 10 minutes each time, in order to go
round the Mint with a visiting sergeant, and
he locked the door When lie went out.
Between the lodge and the street is a high
raiimy and an iron gate, also locked, Barclay
came on duty next morning at nine, took
the key from his pocket, and opened the safe—
and got a shock, from which he hasn’t recovered
yet. The gold was gone ! The safe was just as he
had left it; there were no marks on either door or
window ; and, though the window was unlocked
and unguarded, it is Bft. above the footpath, with
a sloping sill, and if a thief entered that way he left
no trace of his presence. There are, so it is alleged,
only two keys of the safe in existence —one being
in Barclay’s care, the other in the strong room in-
side the Mint. The latter was found in its usual
place in the morning. Barclay’s key remained in
his pocket all night, unless a burglar got into his
bedroom, took it out and opened the safe, and
then carefully put the key back again—all during
the ten minutes the constable was away going his
rounds. The burglar who would be ass enough to
put the key back again after having got the gold
is so impossible as to be not worth looking for. So
the only feasible solution to the problem is that
either the gold wasn’t put into the safe, or
somebody had a duplicate key ; yet Chubb’s local
representative defies any locksmith to make a
duplicate without having one of the two proper
keys as a pattern. Altogether, it is a large size
in mysteries.
* * * *
A feature of the search for the lost bars of
gold, stolen from the guard’s lodge at the gate of
the Melbourne Mint, was the sifting of a heap of
loose earth, lying in the Mint yard. Some de-
tective genius, who had been studying Gaboriau,
suggested that the most improbable place for a
plant was the most likely, so laborers carefully
turned over the mound while a couple of smart
’tecs stood alongside in the attitude of Ajax
expecting the limelight. But the claim proved a
duffer.

An ex-miner writes :
The gold stolen from Melbourne Mint would weigh about
a quarter of a cwt., and might have been carried in a stout
hand-bag. The police make a song of the idea that if the
thief is not acquainted with a big dealer, he will experi-
ence difficulty in disposing of the gold, which is sheer
nonsense. It would probably be worth ihe robber’s while
to take a little trip abroad with his £l7OO in bullion.
In any case, gold is easily broken up, and if the
cool burglar is content to lie low for a time, it
will soon be quite safe for him to figure at country
banks as a lucky digger with a parcel of small slugs for
sale. Meanwhile, should he go short, a few ounces filed
off the bar with a rasp and subjected to an hour’s sweat-
ing in a chamois bag with a handful of sand, would pass
for gold-dust with experienced dealers, and provoke no
suspicion. If the D’s get their man through his efforts to
dispose of his lump of raw gold, he must be a greater fool
than his success in annexing the bullion would lead one
to suppose.

“S.” writes:
Few Australian gold-stealings have been quite as neat
as this most recent robbery from the Melbourne Mint.
It is as clean as a new pin, and leaves no garbage, no
scraps of material, thumb prints, grease stains, jemmy
marks, no impressions of hob-nailed boots—nothing for
Sherlock Holmes to seize.upon. But it may be assumed
that some person possessed a key of the door and a key
of the safe ; and that he walked in swiftly, took the gold
hurriedly, and walked out again rapidly. This shock-
ingly commonplace explanation is supported by a tradi-
tion to the effect that certain keys went missing years
ago. When the keys were lost somebody thought it
would be advisable to change the locks, but apparently
the good intention was forgotten, and went to
help a paving contract in Gehenna. Evidently the
man who found the keys has an excellent memory.
But on top of this comes the mystery of the
murder of diamond merchant Bauer, killed in his
office in Modern Chambers, in busy Collins-street,
at a time when the locality was humming like a
blue-bottle, and the deed was done without even
the man in the next office having the faintest idea that
skull-fracture and robbery were going on just at the
other side of the partition. And, again, there seems to
be no trace, no boot-mark, no finger-print, and nobody
remembers that he saw anyone in particular go in or
anyone unusual go out. Even if all the keys of the Mint
were lost and the front door as well, that throws no
light on the deadness of Bauer, the Collins-street diamond
merchant. He doesn’t seem to have been struck with
a key.
The Sydney D.T. missed a great oppor-
tunity over the murder of the diamond merchant
Bauer, in Melbourne. The headings were com-

Modern Chambers, Collins-street, Mel-
bourne, the scene of the Blandersonian murder of
diamond merchant Bauer, is one of the landmarks
of the boom. A huge slab-sided stone structure
of the wedding cake order, seven storeys high, it
stands opposite the Equitable office, and right
in the centre of commercial life. The building
was erected in’BB for the Modern Permanent Build-
ing Society, but when that concern’s greatness de-
parted it passed to other owners, and the original
proprietors hang out in a chastened spirit in more
modest quarters further up the street. For
many years the upper floors of the Modern
Chambers have consisted mostly of empty
offices, and only one of the two lifts runs
nowadays; but there was a time when pos-
session of offices in the Modern was enough
to get a man an overdraft anywhere.
Among notable Melbourneites who had their
headquarters there were Billy Bruce, solicitor and
celebrated Australian Eleven batsman ; Hughie
McLean, captain of the Geelong football team at
the zenith of its success ; and Runting and
Wright, a land-dealing partnership that was
reputed to hold millions in the late ’eighties.
When the boom collapsed the money owed by
Modern tenants who compromised with their
creditors would have filled three pantechnicons.
One of tho quaintest incidents of Yankee
Professor Jordan’s lectures occurred when
the Prof, was holding forth at Sydney
University. He was extolling the virtues of the
Jap, and stating how superior the Monkey is to
the poor, white European, and generally throwing
slush at his own race: The lecture was well under
way when a small Japanese male person, accom-
panied by a white woman, came down the aisle
and took a front seat. Then, while Jordan was
in the middle of another wild whoop about the
wonderful courtesy of the Japanese character, the
small, brown man spied a vacant seat on the
platform, and strolled across to it. There
he remained for the rest of the evening,
leaving the white woman to look after herself in
the body of the hall. Even the push has better
manners than that.
* * ♦ *
A strange old fiction was knocked out in a
casual way by the Westralian Parliament a while
ago, and the Courts have only just discovered it.
In cases of seduction, it has always been the angry
parent who has come along with demands for
large sums, nominally on account of “ loss of ser-
vices.” The State Parliament knocked that out,
so that the lady herself may now make the de-
mand, and frankly claim damages in the bill for
the injury to her innocence. One father, who
didn’t know what Parliament had been doing,
went to the Supreme Court in Perth the other
day with the usual claim ; but the Judge decided
that all that a father could claim for (except out-
of pocket expenses) was for shock to his feelings ;
and the imported Judge Rooth evidently reckoned
that a father in the beastly colonies shouldn’t be
shocked much by a little thing like that. Any-
way, he fixed the solatium at 40s.
The evidence given before the N.S.W.
Arbitration Court, regarding the cost of living in
Svdney, indicates that there should be an award
t..v boarding-house keepers. In many instances,
it is manifest that the average boarding-house
keeper, through inability to properly dissect the
items of her expenditure, actually loses money
over her boarder. It is impossible that the des-
perate woman who replied to the advt., offering
lodging and three meals daily within walking dis-
tance of the city for 6s. 6d. a week, could have
made a correct estimate of cost. She must
simply have cut down her price, day after day,
in desperation, until she reached the bedrock
of 6s. 6d. The secretary of the Y.W.C.A.
dispelled all doubts about the question when
she stated that in her institution—not a
profit-making concern, be it remembered, but
a heavily-endowed philanthropic one—it costs
14s. weekly to maintain each inmate. Many of
the cheaper boarding-houses are mere death-traps,
in which no medical man would permit a patient
to live for five minutes. Ventilation is unknown,
so is sanitation, and cleanliness is unheard of. The
boarders are thrust into something like the space
that the nigger-run mail boats provide for their
Lascars, and the bedrooms are little more than
stud-farms for consumption germs. The lot of
the boarding-housekeeper is one of the hardest in
the community, and, like most people in despe-
rate straits, she has to make her living as best
she can. Very often, her “best” means the sacrifice
of the lives of others, the dissemination of disease,
the non-payment, as far as possible, of the butcher
and baker, an almost lifelong scrimmage with the
time-payment furniture man, and then failure, the
bailiff, and disappearance. And where the old,
faded boarding-housekeepers go when they dis-
appear, Heaven only knows.
REV. CAMPBELL, OF THE CITY TEMPLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There rose a man in London town,
Among the modern preachers
(Men used to call them teachers),
Who started running Satan down.
Said he : “ I’ll bet you half-a-crown
That I’ll destroy his features.
I’ll analyse his sulphur pits,
Knock horns and tail to little bits,
Crack Hades through the middle ;
And, to confound my brethren wise,
Will rend the ancient, worn disguise,
And prove the Father of all Lies
Himself a tarradiddle.”
And so he clove the cloven feet,
Revealed the tale a fiction
In cultured modern diction.
He sho.ved the lurid fires a cheat,
That seem to glow in Brimstone-street,
Down vales of malediction ;
And Hell’s an old wife’s vulgar yarn—
The Bogey of the Haunted Barn—
Made for the simple urchin
When men were only babes and fools,
The church’s prey, the clergy’s tools—
The naughty boys on dunce’s stools,
Who needed threat of birchin’.
Thus said this modern clergyman,
This Reverend Mr. Campbell,
With more or less preamble ;
And instantly a shudder ran
Through all the godly cleric clan
That one of them should damn hell.
Through all the din was heard above,
“ How dare he say the God of Love
Forgot to make the devil ?
How dare he doubt that Adam fell ?
How dare the bliss of saints dispel
By robbing them of sight of Hell,
Its scenes of pain and revel ? ”
And so this man of London church,
A-shouting from the steeple,
Appealed unto the people
From his imagined lofty perch :
“ I will destroy the ancient birch
And ostracise the Bogey,
The Spectre of the Mouldering Barn.”
The people merely murmured “ Garn,
You most amusing fogey ;
You Campbell antiquated !
We burnt that mediaeval switch
(How sadly you’re belated)
Tied to the last surviving witch,
Beside a nineteenth century ditch
(Oh, Reverend Mr. Campbell,
How sadly slow you amble),
And there the Devil dangled
Upon the gibbet strangled,
And the deed of execution
Was well and truly done
(Oh slow and laggard one)
By Hangman Evolution.”
VIC.
ALPENSTOCK.
CALVES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Sydney physiologist asserts that big calves are a sign
of intellectuality, and nations with stout legs are the
most advanced.
The bishop’s calves are neat and slim,
The footman more than rivals him.
The lady who’s a Tory Leaguer
Has understandings rather meagre
Compared with Flossies of our time
Who caper in the pantomime.
Bent’s calves the connoisseur admires,
And eke the solid legs of Squires,
While Jud’s supports are hardly lawful,
And Northcote’s calves are something awful.
And in our Parliaments there be
A lot who’re brainy—to the knee.
We’re puzzled, but at least we know
Why girls their silken stockings show
At crossings ; ’tis that all may see
Their intellectuali-TEE.
vie.

TheJoneHand
The July Lone Hand is now going through the
press, and, fulfilling promises, it will surpass the
two previous numbers. The magazine is now
“getting into its stride.” Contributors and
artists are recognising what is required of them,
and the result is a steady progress in its appear-
ance and general interest.
Opening with “A Bush Symphony,” a prose
poem of the Australian forest, by Frank Myers,
The Lone Hand for July follows with a humorous
set of verses by the late Victor Daley, illustrated
by “ Hop,” and continues with a robust story of
sentiment, “Riches of Exile,” by Howard Ash-
ton, with pictures by Norman Lindsay. These
three items represent well the eloquence, the
humor, the romance and the art of Australia.
A larger instalment of J. F. Archibald’s memoirs
is given in this July number, including some most
interesting yarns of early-day journalism in
Australia.
As a frontispiece, a fine portrait by Norman
Carter, in red chalk, of J. C. Watson will be pub-
lished.
There is not room to speak further this week of
the July number. But it will mark the highest
point yet reached in Australian magazine work.
The “ For the Public Good ” section will be par-
ticularly strong.
Subscription 12s. per year (post free).
Advertising Rates on Application.
214 George Street, Sydney.

. 309-311 George Street* SYDNEY*
An Adelaide burglar has unconsciously
earned a first-class testimonial for the local police.
J. R. Edwards, a lawyer practising in Broken
Hill, sent down to the S. A. Police Commissioner
a notice to be served on a witness. The man had
left the State, the notice was not served in time,
and Edwards wrote to the papers to say that the
Crow-eater police were a lot of fossils. But he
has a house in Adelaide himself, and last week
burglars went through it. Within four days every
article was recovered from different pawnshops,
and the thief—a stranger both to Edwards and
the police—was in custody. The solicitor has now
publicly withdrawn his growl. Adelaide is no
place for a burglar, anyway. There is nothing to
do with the plunder, and the few suspicious
characters are so well rounded up that arrest and
restitution are commonly effected in 48 hours.
* * * *
Some of the clerics who ran the sensational
Methodist mission meeting in Sydney last week,
when £2353 was raised in one day for the purpose
of teaching the Fijian to pound the loud hymn in
short metre, played it very low down on some of
the people in the audience. When the stream of
contributions had apparently run out, and there
was no sign of anybody else offering, one black-
coated buttoner arose and remarked: “There is
an avenue of subscriptions that has not yet been
touched. Many of those present have lost dear
relatives and friends through death, and, no
doubt, would like to contribute something in their
memory.” One sad-faced, grey-haired oid lady in
the front almost immediately burst into tears, and
offered two guineas in the name of a dead son, and
fully £3OO was raised through this cruel and savage
harrowing of the feelings of the bereaved. It was
a triumph of ingenuity—this idea of collecting
from the dead —and when that grey-haired mother
sat weeping in the front seat it is to be hoped the
clerical hawk felt ashamed of himself.
* * * *
The Carruthers Government, which has a
mania for selling something, has sold the old
Observer Tavern, in George-street, where the
ancient Sydney inquests used to be held. It got
£3500 for the 30ft. frontage. Another mouldy
pile in the same quarter, the New York Hotel, is
being torn down. It was part of a block that
went up in the ’twenties ; but it wasn’t licensed
as a pub till 50 years later. Then, probably as a
delicate compliment to its age, they called it the
Nil Desperandum, but renamed it some. years
afterwards when it got so old that despair was
written all over it.
When the N.S.W. Public Service Board
took over the supervision of the State’s unfortu-
nate public servants, it created an arbitrary valu-
ation of emoluments to nurses, thus : Salary, £65;
quarters, £10; rations, £l5; fuel and light, £5;
total £95. Then an understudy of Joe’s made the
brilliant discovery that rations cost more than £l5
per annum. He said nothing about fuel and
light, possibly because he didn’t see how the fuel
and light provided for the patients could be cut
off from the attendant nurses. He was also silent
on the subject of quarters, because even his intellect
was equal to realising the convenience of having a
nurse within call instead of her living in a boarding
house at the other end of the town. But he sug-
gested that the nurses should be handed £l5 per
annum and told to feed themselves, and Premier
Joe is'said to have approved the great idea. The
scheme came to nothing, for presently the genius
discovered that if the nurses had to runout to buy
their chops and potatoes, and then leave the
patients while they personally fried, that pro-
vender, the hospitals would go to pieces. But
probably Joseph and his friend will think out
another bright idea by-and-bye.

DEATH COMETH UNA WARES.
“Yes, I’m thinking of trying a good hair dye.”
“ Going to dye it! Why , man, you're not even
in time for the funeral.”
Adelaide business people are wrath with
the Tramway Trust for refusing to run a double
line of tram rails down the principal commercial
streets. Old Colonel Light laid out Adelaide with
a compass and a rule —first a wide street and then
a narrow street —and it happens that the big
retail shopkeepers have nearly all gone into the
narrow streets, and left the wide streets to offices,
warehouses, boarding-houses, and the little general
store that has the fly-paper and the sticks of
lolly in the window. The Trust’s proposal is to
take the cars along the wide streets. Wherefore
the traders of Rundle and Hindley streets, the
principal narrow ways, cry out, as did the
Ephesians, that their craft is in danger, and
they are making a great fuss on the subject.
* * * *
N.S. Wales Rifle Association is getting to
holts with Colonel Foster, the Sydney ’Varsity’s
military lecturer, who says good rifle shooting is
of no particular use in battle, and it points out
that the local panjandrum is at loggerheads with
Roberts, Buller, and the modern text-books. As
Foster may be regarded as a sort of animated
text-book, and as Roberts and Buller are at log-
gerheads with one another, it looks as though
military authorities have a fine way of disagree-
ing over the best method of kil ling perf ectstrangers.
Possibly there is no best method—all depends on
the sort of perfect stranger you set out to kill.
That is generally the way. There isn’t even a
best method of eating eggs. If you are in close
and constant communication with the hen, you can
boil them. If she is a stranger in a far land, you
try them scrambled.
* * * *
Just now Fitzroy (Melbourne) is having a
violent time among the record-breakers. Persons
with nothing better to do are still making pop
eyes in the vicinity of the piano that a misguided
fellow-creature vainly tried to keep on thumping
for 48 hours. And while they are gazing, a rival
record-breaker presents them with documents
ornamented with an elegant portrait of a duck,
and reading thus: —
RECORDS !! !
The goose that laid the golden egg is not’to be compared
with this duck. This duck laid 326 eggs in 354 days, only
missing 28 days in the above time. On view in our
window. Don’t miss seeing this record-breaker.
Mr. Healy played the piano for 46J hours unceasingly.
Another good record !
Yet some ignoramuses assert that Australia is
hopelessly severed from the great centres of art !
* * * *
An enterprising individual who sought to
make a living by trading on the bargain-hunting
proclivities of women was lately dealt with at
Sydney Quarter Sessions. His offence was the
selling of an alleged pound of tea to a Neutral
Bay resident for Is. 6d., and throwing in an order
on an alleged George-street firm for six cups and
saucers. Now, 3s. a dozen will buy only
the most ordinary cups and saucers, so
that a purchaser, under the circumstances,
couldn’t expect much in the way of tea.
In this instance the lady weighed the tea and
noted that it wasn’t and on inquiry in George-
street found that the cup and saucer firm was a
myth. Also the tea was vile. So she got
precious little for her eighteen pence, while the
vendor received eighteen months. It came out in
evidence that quite a competence can be earned
this way, as the duped female can be usually
depended on not to prosecute. .Also it was elicited
that certain “tea” is sold wholesale in Sydney
for under 3d. a lb.
