There are woods of high mercantile value,
Rare woods of great beauty and price,
And woods of such close grain and hardness
As resist all white ants, rats, and mice.
But the Woods of all woods to be lauded,
(You all will agree, I am sure),
Is the Woods to whom mankind’s indebted,
For the only Great Peppermint Cure.*
At one Melbourne skating carnival a fan-
tastically-garbed youth floated round the floor,
upsetting everybody. When remonstrated with,
he upset them more, and, finally, an indignant
stranger, whose fiancee had just been made to
turn a succession of four somersaults, dragged
him outside and punched his
head gloriously. After a turn
at the tap, the youth re-
) turned gamely to the floor,
upset several dozen more
couples, and, when the
time came for the prize
distribution, announced
that he was sustaining the
character of a whirlwind.
The committee had no
option but to hand him
over a butter-dish, valued
at 3s. Gd., and he glode
off with the iron-mqn-
gery, a flush of pride
suffusing his battered
features. The com-
mittee of the Literary
Pageant ball scowled
with a dark and hor-
rible scowl at a
frolicsome youth
who proposed that
he and a number
of familiar spirits,
male and female,
should contribute a
Rabelais set, in which
he proposed to appear as
Panurge and, he re-
marked, “ have a shot
for the prize for the best-
sustained character, of
course.” A high-toned
affair like the L.P. ball
didn’t have any prize for the
best-sustained character, and
he knew it. In fact, it would
be rather an advantage if the
management of every ball shut
down on this form of tom.
foolery. Whenever visitors
find some individual making
himself rather more of an ass
and a nuisance than usual, he
is found -to be going strong
for the best-sustained char-
acter prize. Some years ago,
the committee of that great
Parisian fixture, the Students’
Ball of the Four Arts, used to
give such prizes ; but finally a
competitor elected to go as a
Stench, and sustained the
character so well that, inside
five minutes, an official with
his nose in his hand rushed
at him in the middle of the empty ball-room,
and laying the prize at his feet, motioned
for him to leave before be was assassinated.
That was the last occasion on which such a prize
was given. Since then the officials have shuddered
at the very mention of the best-sustained character.
* * * *
“Sailor” on the vexed question of the
Australian seaman :—
Very good of “Belaying Pin” (B. 16/5/’07) to agree
with ' me re Australian sailors. I know about
the Maoriland sailing ships he mentions, and think
he’ll find most of his “six out of every eight
engaged” are Maorilanders. They and Tasmanians
go to sea more than Australians. I still hold
that my Australian ships’ average of two Australians in
eight is well up to the mark. Granted that many
foreign rs make good Australians and fine sailors, that
isn’t the point. The best all-round sailor-man I’ve met in
these waters is a Dago, but, for all that, I’d rather have
British-Australians. Illogical, perhaps ! Have met a few
called Limburger or Gorgonzola who are as Australian as
The Bulletin ; but they are of the second generation.
The Australian shipowner is mostly English, and doesn’t
give more than he can help. The better pay and food is
the result of Trades Unionism. There is still room for a
lot of improvement in accommodation. Few of our older
ships would pass present requirements of the British Board
of Trade as to fo’c’sles, and those requirements are not
exacting. One of the Howard Smith Co. said lately : “ The
Australian coastal trade was better catered for and at low'er
prices than that of any other country.” 'that was the
Fat owner talking of his Private Enterprise ships. He
didn’t mention that the profits are from three to five
times larger than those of English coastal cos. And the
way they treat the employees is evidenced in the Easby
case. The present combine is sufficiently near to a com-
plete monopoly as it is, and should not be subsidised.
Much better for the Commonwealth Government to start
a line of its own, using it as a naval reserve.
* * * *
Chief Justice Madden spoke strenuously in
the Banco Court, Melbourne, one recent afternoon
“It is the same thing over and over. Every day
I have to try cases in which the pleadings are all
over the place—no cohesion, no logic, no any-
thing. It is all talk, talk, talk.” At the very
moment that his Honor railed thus, four expen-
sive barristers were wasting needless days trying
to help a costly Judge ascertain whether certain
somewhat-skilled laborers ought to get as much as
£2 per week. The questions asked by those
allegedly learned gentleman-by-Act-of-Parliament
were puerile when they weren’t insulting—what
the witness did with an odd fourpenee that
hadn’t been accounted for in the weekly balance-
sheet ; whether he smoked ; if he had an occa-
sional drink, etc., etc. On the one side, men
doing Useful work, and struggling for £2 a week ;
on the other, men—“all talk, talk, talk”—
patronising or bullying or insulting the useful
people, and getting for it as much a day as the
useful people were pleading to be paid a month.
Truly, it is a cranky world !
The new Adelaide Tramway Trust is
making progress. It hasn’t yet done much to
get rid of the filthy old horse-cars that rumble
through the streets, it is true, but it has fixed up
this bylaw, and Le Hunte has confirmed it: —
No person ’■hall, whilst upon a tram, without the per-
mission of the secretary of the Trust, blow or play upon
any musical instrument, nor shall any person whilst upon
a tram blow or play upon any horn, mouth organ, whistle,
Jew’s harp, or other similar instrument.
Note the keen ear and intellect of the Trust.
John Downer, for instance, may play a piano on
the tram as he rides out to North Adelaide, pro-
vided he gets a note from the secretary ; but not
even the secretary may authorise John Downer to
play a Jew’s harp on the tram.
Gentlemen who wish to look smart, should
pay a visit to Wild’s hairdressing saloon, where six
first-class artists are engaged. The establish-
ment is under the Patronage of Lord Northcote.
Address, 30 Sydney Arcade (upstairs), entrance
from George or King-streets.*

TRAFFIG
GONSTABLE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A T a crossing where the ever-rushing tram
Bumpeth hard across the intersecting line,
Where the hasty man articulateth “ damn ,”
And the loungers ’ gainst the corner-post recline —
There he standeth! See ! He raiseth up a hand.
And the high and mighty Jehu giveth way ;
Noio the hand across the heaven waveth grand,
Now it oscillateth slightly, seeming gay.
He condescendcth answer to a friend,
He reproveth now the driver of a cart,
And he searcheth for his book and pencil-end,
And he writeth now a little while apart —
What he writeth in the booklet none can tell,
Nor the mighty thoughts that crowd his mighty
brain.
Ha ! The weapon ’neath his jumper bulgeth well
Ashe scowleth on the carter once again.
Though I know there be inspectors more than he,
Though his better half may call him plainly
“Bill,”
Yet he seems a mighty Monarch unto me —
He’s the Overlord of Crossing Places still!

EUNGELLA.
THE OLD, OLD MEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The honorary bench holds veterans of surprising endur-
ance. In one suburban court it has happened more than
once that the four presiding justices have averaged 80
years of age ! — Melbourne Argus.
The courthouse flies buzz in and out, and they hopscotch
up and down ;
They chevy the men who puff and pout on the bench—
from nape to crown.
They chase and skip ; and the angry, red
says, “ Five pound,
Or”—here he slaps his bald, old head —“ Six months /”
and a grin goes round [
As the stricken fowl-fiend murmurs “’Ell!” and grinds
his teeth, clean wild
At the plunge he’s had in the bubbling well of justice
undefiled.
And the old, old men, each brain a-drowse beneath its
scanty thatch,
They make old, snuffling sounds like cows that scent a
lucerne-patch.
The small, round, purple shinypate whose chin hangs
down three deep,
And the gaunt, grey, blighted heavyweight with a face
like a wearied sheep,
The stooped and slim, and the short and stout, they’re
all old men and know
The way of the saint, and the sinful lout, and the school-
girl white as snow ;
They know some tricks of the lawyer’s trade—and shady
ones at that,
The way to sit back, solemn and staid, and wink at the
sins of Fat,
While the plaintive bloke with the stubbly chin and a
record of petty crime,
Who’s perished along the track of sin, goes in to do his
time.
The old, old men, they droop and drone, in a horrible,
sad decay;
They’re gone in brain and blood and bone —but they act
their parts all day ;
They say “That’s right!” and it is wrong—and right,
and they bellow “No ! ”
And the Three-months-hard, he groans “ How long ! ”
(he’d snapped a dollar or so).
And the freed wife-beater seeks his wife and grabs her
lank back hair
And gives her bean 3 with a carving-knife, or the leg of a
broken chair,
The old, old men, they’re old as sin, and their stock of
sense is small,
But they stick to the courts through thick and thin, like
flies to a sunny wall.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
With only one more month to go, the sort of
statements the State Treasurers will be able to make at
the end of their financial year is now pretty evident.
Except Westralia, they will all get on the top rail, and
crow—some of them with some justification, some of
them with very little. Here are the figures, as far as the
incomplete Treasury reports allow figures to be got
together

The principal features of the foregoing may be
put into these bundles: —
N.S. Wales. —This time last year the surplus was
£511,182 ; it is now £989,226, or £478,044 more. Last year
the surplus (for the full year) was £896,124 ; conse-
quently, if nothing abnormal happens this current
month, and only as much income tax comes in as came
in last June, this year’s surplus should be £1,374,168.
That, however, will include something over £1,000,000
derived from the sale of public estate. Out of this sur-
plus and expected surplus £918,051 has been transferred
already to the Public Works and Closer Settlement
Funds. Yet, notwithstanding that transfer, just short
of a million and a quarter of money has been borrowed
and spent. And notwithstanding that numerous items
of expenditure formerly paid out of revenue are no doubt
now paid out of Works Fund, the ordinary expenditure
has moved up from £9,273,704 as at May 31, 1900, to
£11,214,424 as at May 31, 1907, an increase in expenditure
of £1,940,720 for eleven months. Yet, by talking about
remission of taxation and reduction of -railway rates,
Treasurer Carruthers has made quite a lot of people be-
lieve that he is the financial reformer he isn’t.
Victoria. —Nothing is known about Victoria’s expendi-
ture. But presuming that T. Bent’s estimate has been
kept handy, there will be a surplus of three-quarters of a
million. T. Bent estimated that he would get in
£7,554,568 of revenue in the 12 months. In 11 months he
has collected £7,539,756, or only £14,812 short of the full
year’s estimate ; so that practically June revenue will be
all surplus. And last June’s revenue was £735,422.
Nothing is said either about the loan expenditure, but
that doesn’t amount to much. In 1906 the total, exclu-
sive of money spent to repurchase estates (£754,983),
Was £177,983. The year before that it was £372,437.
Ih the matter of recent borrowings N.S. Wales, Wes-
tralia and S. Australia have been the chief sinners ;
though something can be said for Westralia that can’t be
said for the others—it had to promptly meet and provide
for a great inrush of population. Of £34,697,353 of new
debt added in the Commonwealth between 1900 and
June, 1905, N.S. Wales was responsible for £19,221,992 ;
and of a loan expenditure of £3,547,703 in 1906 N.S.
Wales was responsible for £1,357,771, and the figures will
be about the same this year. Therefore of about
£41,500,000 borrowed since the birth of the Common-
wealth, N.S. Wales’ share is £22,000,000, or more than
that of all the other States put together. In the face of
a revenue which has grown from £10,203,931 in 1900 to,
say, £13,320,000 in 1907, and despite the fact that over
£1,000,000 of the expenditure the State had to meet in
1900 is now carried by the Commonwealth, which ought
to have also helped to make borrowing unnecessary, N.S.
Wales has gone to the pawnshop more than all the others
put together. It has added £22,000,000 to its debts while
Victoria has added £4,050,000 (taking the Victorian loan
expenditure for land resumptions, &c., in the past year at
about £750,000).
Queensland.— Last year at the end of May there was
£712,397 in the stocking, out of which the half-year’s in-
terest had to come ; and the year finished the following
month with a surplus of £127,811. There is now £928,979
in the stocking, or £216,582 more ; so this year’s surplus
looks as though it will be about £330,000. Meantime
public-wo.ks repairs and retrenched public-service
salaries have been seen to, which with other things have
added £192,360 to the expenditure : so the surplus is even
better than it looks. The worst feature of the Queens-
land case is the growth of the once almost-abolished
borrowing.
Westralia.—At the start of the year the Westralian
Treasurer said that there was going to be a
deficit, but what was a little thing like a
deficit to worry over ! Other people might put
on an extra tax here, or cut off a bit of expen-
diture there; for its part, Westralia would just
wait for something to turn up. And the thing that has
turned up is a rather larger deficit than was bargained
for. The Treasurer brought forward a debit of £119,900
from last year, and it has now grown to £213,501. It is
likely to go on growing. Meantime the State cheerfully
scents money in the Eastern States, and goes in for a
“vigorous works policy.” A sample is just reported.
Merton’s Reward Co. didn’t have a water supply, it said ;
so the Government, a month or two ago, provided one to
keep the mill going. Now the co. finds that the ore has
got so poor that work will have to knock off. But Cohen
won’t knock the water-supply debt off.
* * * *
The Colonial Sugar Refining Co. is very daring.
At a time when there is a good deal of solid opinion that
it is an incubus and a danger—that the vast profits which
it makes are one of the reasons why it is admittedly
difficult for the Commonwealth to pursue its White Aus-
tralia policy in the sugar-growing districts—the co. an-
nounces what amounts to a watering of capital. The
present capital is £2,200,000, in 110,000 shares, on which
10 per cent, is paid, or £220,000 a year. It is now pro-
posed to increase the capital to £2,500,000, but of the
£3OO 000 only £225,000 is to be paid up by the share-
holders; the other £75,000 will be paid for them
out of undivided profits. In other words, each 22
shares get for a total of £45 three shares which will
be worth something over £125. They will be worth
£133 6s. Bd. on a 4i per cent, basis, assuming that the
10 per cent, dividend is continued. At even £125 this
means that the co. is going to make a present of £BO to
each holder of 22 shares, or very little short of £4 per
share. That, practically, is the amount of the present
“ water.”
What are C.S.R. shares worth, with the new
issue in prospect ? Present price of shares is about £44.
Twenty-two shares at £44 means £968. They carry three
shares = £45 ; total, £lOl3. The 25 shares, assuming the
10 per cent. div. is continued, will bring in £5O a year ;
and that is equal to £4 JBs. Bd. per cent.—which is the
return an investor would get if he paid £44 per share. A
man who wants a 5 per cent, investment cannot pay
more than £43 Bs. 2d. per share (disregarding the fact
that a small amount of interest is already accrued—the
co’s last balance was at the end of March). A man who
is satisfied with a ih per cent, investment, and is pre-
pared to take the very small risk of the present scheme
going through, and the larger risk of the Commonwealth
doing something emphatic in the sugar industry in order
to help it to keep “ white’’—that man could afford to
pay £4B 9s. for the shares.
The Melbourne brewery amalgamation has been
completed ; and the businesses of the Carlton, McCracken,
Castlemaine, Victoria, Shamrock and Poster cos. have
been taken over by the Carlton and United Breweries
Proprietary, Ltd. This co. does not take over all the assets,
however ; the balances not taken by the new brewery
co. either go to assets realisation cos. or are retained
by the existing cos. The new brewery co. has the right
to take the breweries on rental from the present cos. ; in
the case of the Castlemaine brewery it is announced that
the rent will be £BOO a year. The new co. will start with
cash and goods to the value of £200,000, and without any
liabilities. It will not issue scrip, but a sort of trans-
ferable inscribed stock —without liability, of course.
Whether, however, there will be a free market
for the stock remains to be seen, inasmuch as
the directors are expressly placed under no obliga-
tion to issue a balance sheet. In the case of
McCracken’s, the shareholders get 41,625 shares the
debenture holders get 143,375. Of these 41,625 shares
the preference shareholders take 19,000; that means that
pref. shareholders get 10s. in the £ in new’ shares, and
ordinary shareholders Is. lOd. in the £. The Castlemaine
Co. gets 102,500 shares, in return for w'hich it turns over,
as well as its business, goods and cash to the extent of
about £20,000. No clear statement of the whole position
has, however, been given ; and it looks as though it is
intended to carry on the Melbourne brewery business for
some time to come as much undent cloak as possible.
* * * *
Mostly the directors of Australasian industrial
cos., which have to struggle against insufficient Protec-
tion, try to put a good face on the matter by not
writing off enough for depreciation of plant and stock, or
by writing off nothing at all. That is to say, they show
profits at the expense of their capital, in the hope that
the politicians will give them more Protection.
But the politicians are slow’, and that sort of make-
believe must end some time ; and it generallj’ends in a
horrible awakening for the shareholders, who haven’t
been behind the scenes. A typical case comes from
Maoriland. For many years past Donaghy’s Rope and
Twine Co., Ltd., has looked a fairly prosperous
concern, which has paid 7 per cent, on its £47,000
capital. But a while ago a practically new Board was
appointed, which proceeded to make a valuation of
the assets, and then it was discovered that, instead
of these assets being worth £77,055 as the books showed,
they are now really worth only £36,342. The reserves of
all kinds on March 31 were £13,584 ; so there remains a
deficit of £27,l2B—more than half the capital. Appar-
ently, however, the new’ Board hasn’t the heart to face
the situation at once and write off that much capital as
lost ; sp the deficit is carried to profit and loss suspense
account. Fortunately the revaluation does not affect the
profits ; and, as the new Board has stopped dividends, a
recovery may be made. But it w'ill be a long and weary
process.
* « * *
The Melbourne broker’s view is that he isn’t
losing anything by the rule prohibiting members of the
Exchange from sharing commission with outsiders. The
outsider who used to get the commission—one-third it was
till the rule was altered —was in nearly all cases the legal
manager of the co. in whose stock a dabbler wanted
shares ; and the Melbourne broker’s view is that a legal
manager isn’t in the same position as the “runner”
whom the London broker puts up with —chiefly because
London is a big place, and it is harder to run against a
broker when you want one ; or, when you don’t want
one, it is easier to avoid him, and it isn’t the broker’s
business co be avoided. Adelaide and Sydney are claimed
to be solidly with the Melbourne Exchange in the matter ;
and a large section of the legal managers, especially of
the old school, reckon that the less legal managers are
encouraged to mix themselves up with share-dealing- the
better.

So far as its relations with clients are concerned Golds-
brough, Mort and Co., Ltd., probably stands as high in
estimation now as ever it did ; but in other matters its
administration has succeeded in getting itself most
thoroughly distrusted. Even the great prosperity which
it is now able to report—and prosperity generally silences
criticism—has simply the effect of intensifying the
suspicion with which the administration of the concern is
viewed. Now, various things have conspired to create
this suspicion, and the administration may possibly have
a perfect defence. The history of the wormy twistings of
its share and debenture capital is something like this :
1893. —First reconstruction. The paid capital was
£450,000, in 45,000 £lO shares. Reserves (£260,000) were
written off ; 900,000 sharps of £4 15s. were issued, ss.
paid, instead of the old shares. That meant that £225,000
of capital was written off. Debenture-holders and de-
positors, who had about 10-times the capital in the busi-
ness that shareholders had, got 5 per cent, debentures,
partly at long dates.
1895.—Second reconstruction. Debenture stock halved
into A and B stock, carrying 4 per cent., the B stock in-
terest dependent on there being sufficient profit available
after payment of A interest. Shareholders given the
right to pay up to £2 155., and then receive £2 ss. prefer-
ence shares.
1900. —Statement at this date shows that of the 900,000
shares 738,162 had been allotted ; apparently the holders
of 80,919 old shares (161,838 new ones) declined to accept
the £4 10s. liability which the new shares involved. The
738,162 were divided as follows
Paid-up Preference 353,773
Ordinary 206,361
Forfeited Ordinary .. .. -• .. 178,028
Total 738,162
It will be observed that, though a great many shares
were not applied for, or were forfeited, possibly because of
the liability up to £4155., this reconstruction made shares
paid up and preferential when only £2 15s. had been
paid.
1901. —Third reconstruction. All ordinary share capital
cancelled—ordinary shareholders were those who had not
paid up to £2 15s. under the 1895 scheme. (In 1900 the
live ordinary shares stood credited with £178,614.) Pre-
ference shares were now further reduced from £2 ss. (£2
15s. actually paid) to £1 15s. At the same time it was
provided that profits should be distributed—(l) to create
a primary reserve fund of £75,000 to pay in teres on A and
B stock ; (2) after that, 25 per cent, to go to a secondary
reserve of £75,000, and 75 per cent, to pay first 3 per
cent, on capital and then an extra 1 per cent, on B stock ;
(3) when those things have been provided for, share-
holders can do as they like with the profits. (Both
reserves have now been made up to £75,000.)
From all of which it is obvious that of the
original shareholders a very large proportion have
dropped their interest. A great many dropped their in-
terest because of the liability of £4 10s. ; after they had
gone, the liability was reduced to £2 15s. Then a
great many more were dropped because, so recently as
1901, it was presumably held that the co. had irretriev-
ably lost the share capital on all but the preference
shares (and it will have been noted how those shares be-
came preferential). Now a profit is shown, after paying
the debenture stock and writing’ £33,500 off premises,
&c., equal to 15 per cent, on the capital before the ordi-
nary share capital was cancelled in 1901 ; and in 1905-6
the return was equal to over 10 per cent, all round. Yet
these ordinary shares were ruled out in 1901. It is quite
true that Australia is a land of marvellously rapid re-
coveries, yet probably the directors were not justified in
assuming that there would be any rapid recovery after
1901. It is quite certain, on the other hand,
that, unless all were given the most ample and minute
information regarding the company’s doings and position,
people who had that information would be placed at an
enormous advantage over the shareholder or the bond-
holder who had no inside knowledge. And, unfor-
tunately, the published information has not been
remarkable for its amplitude or minuteness. Moreover,
that reduction of the liability on preference shades, after
a great many had dropped out because of the liability,
leaves a nasty taste in the mouth ; and the further
dropping of the ordinary shares in 1901 doesn’t make the
taste any better. If in 1895 anybody knew what was
going to be done in these two cases he would have been
sure of making a very fine haul. Whether anybody did
know, The Bulletin can’t tell. And unfortunately those
ex-shareholders who have been dropped are not now in a
position to force an investigation and a search for any
internal evidence on the subject. The fact remains that
nearly two-thirds of the original share's have disap-
peared ; one-third now hold the interest of the lot.
Whether the co.’s position was ever as bad as
it was represented to be on one or two occasions, is only
a matter of opinion. The debenture-holders acted in a
peculiar way in not getting more power than they did—
and there may probably be an explanation of that.
Apparently, they allowed the shareholders to keep their
interest in order to extract some calls out of them. That
may have been a prudent thing ; but still, considering
what they had at stake, they acted in a strange fashion
in allowing other people to absolutely control the con-
cern. If they had known more about it—more about
Australian conditions and the workings of this co.—B
stock might not have been sold in large parcels down to
37 so recently as 1904. Altogether it has been a bad
business for the majority. Whether the minority
who stand where they do to-day stand there because
they had better information, which only the few
got (as members of the majority protest), or because they
knew the marvellous recoveries that pastoral Australia
makes, it is impossible to say. One thing may, however,
be profitably remembered. In a pastoral way, Australia
is always see-sawing ; and it is probably at the top, or
thereabouts, just now. All seasons are not good ; and
money may be perhaps just as easily lost by grabbing
apparent chances that offer now as it was lost by neglect-
ing to grab chances that did offer in the back years of
depression.

This bank, like most of the others, did not realise how
bad the smash was when it was forced to a reconstruction
in 1893. It therefore undertook to pay 4J per cent, on
its locked-up deposits; and, although in 1887, when
things looked very gloomy, it was forced to get by
English Act a modification of these terms, so that per
cent, of the 4J per cent, should only be paid when profits
admitted, as a matter of fact it has paid the ih per cent,
all through. Indeed, as a r. cord had to be kept of the un-
paid 1| per cent., and all arrears had to be paid before any
dividend was declared, the relief was rather illusory. The
bank got authority, if necessary, to postpone payment of
one-third of the interest on its reconstructed deposits,
but unless it decided never to pay any dividend whatever,
the arrangement made no diminution in its liabilities.
At the same time, the priority dividend on the preference
shares was fixed as high as 5J per cent. ; so that it comes
to this : in order to give the hungering ordinary share-
holders a taste, it was necessary to pay a full 4J- per
cent, on the old deposits and 5J per cent, on the pre-
ference capital. The one consolation, almost, of the
ordinary shareholders is that, when they had the option,
so few of the old depositors accepted preference shares.
Though the severity of the terms has made
rapid progress impossible, the bank has nevertheless got
rid of £3,902,929 of the old debt. As at Dec. 31, 1893,
the total of old deposits not represented by pref. shares
stood at £4,845,156 ; the amount is now £942,227. The
following shows recent progress in repayment

To a great extent, of course, this debt has been merely
transferred. Thus at the end of 1893 the new debts to
the public (notes, bills, and deposits) totalled £1,645,570 ;
the total at December 31 last was £4,174,684/ VVhat pro-
gress has been made with the old securitie- cannot unfor-
tunately be put down in a list like this. Still, if the old
securities, with some help from the new ones, were able
to earn the 4£ per cent, on the old deposits, they
can do a good deal more than earn interest on
the new 3 or per cent, deposits for which the old
deposits have been exchanged. Hence, apart from the
improvement in general Australian conditions, an in-
crease in the rate of earnings might naturally be looked
for. It is no use going back beyond 1903, because the
capital was written down in that year ; since then, how-
ever, profits have gone like this (on the basis of capital
and reserves)

The liquid assets represent under 38 per cent, of the debts
to the public apart from the old deposits, so that the bank
is working to about what may be reckoned the limit of
safety. It certainly has no resources in hand and wast-
ing. Assuming that it has been in a position to make
very satisfactory realisations of old securities during the
past couple of years—at prices which it probably never
hoped for for years to come —it has still also made
large recent advances. In other words, it appears to
have adopted a bold, launching-out policy; and so
far Australian conditions have entirely favored that
policy. The future may also possibly do so. That has to
be proved. The bank certainly faces that future with
bigger risks ; for example, the bulk of its deposits are
not a fixture as they were ; but whether that is of much,
or little, consequence depends upon the character of the
securities, old and new.

Though this office suffered less than some in the Ameri-
can insurance upheaval, it still shows the effects of that
trouble. And they are by no means all bad effects. It
is true that the upward march of the new business and
total income figures has been stopped. (The table
above shows the figures relating to cash income
and expenditure.) But only those misguided people
who mistake bigness for greatness will be sorry.
It was that mad rush for bigness which finally forced
the New York Legislature to limit the amount of
new business the office might take to £30,000,000 a year.
That of itself would automatically reduce the expense
rate ; but the rate is expressly limited. Look at the
result of that State interference that many insurance men
are fond of saying is so utterly unnecessary in their
case

In 1905 the new premiums were £2,670,000. Last year
they were £1,570,087. That shrinkage accounts for the
remarkable fall in the expense rate ; for it was the costly
pursuit of an ever-increasing volume of new business
which previously made the expense figures what they were.
Certainly it cannot be said that so far the new business
has cost any less than it used to ; and it ought to cost less,
for now naturally only the most easily and cheaply got
business will be pursued. As a matter of fact, applying
the system usually adopted here, new business last
year cost about 117 per cent, of the first year’s
premiums, as against about 110 per cent, in the
previous year. Still, it has to be remembered
that the co. has had to fight vast prejudices
during the year, and that would make business abnormally
expensive to get. For the same reason, surrenders have
been very heavy. But these things will right themselves.
There have been scandals, but every expert investigation
made of the co.’s affairs has concluded with a certificate
of complete solvency. Moreover, the American insurance
law, as a result of the scandals, has become very
stringent—far more stringent than it is in countries
where no inquiries have been made, and where it is quite
possible that materials for scandal would be found if
inquiries were made.

in 1902 the. pledges and stock totalled £19,283 ; in these
prosperous times they are down to £17,961. It’s a good
wind that doesn’t blow ill to somebody. Still, the in-
creased dividend absorbs less than two-thirds of the net
profit, assuming that the provision for depreciation is
enough and that the stated profit is, therefore, really- net.

Ivanhoe’s revenue for 1906 showed a decrease
compared with 1905 and 1904, but the net profit was suf-
ficient to permit of the usual dividend distribution of :4
per cent. On the whole, the position of this Westralian
co. is very solid. The general reserve fund has been in-
creased to £60,000 ; the bullion reserve stands at £37,000;
the cash balance at £60,000. And the ore reserves,
accepting the official estimate, represent a profit of nearly
£6 per share.

* •* . * *
Word is being passed round that the big tellu-
ride ore shoots in the Kalgurli (W.A.) show signs of con-
traction in the bottom workings. The first two levels
yieldedalotof rich ore ; for the next few hundred feet the
property was only- a shadow of its former self; after
that, immense bulges of good stone were struck and held
down to 1150 ft. It is below this depth that the shoots
are alleged to be shrinking. In July, last year, the ore
reserves stood at 650,000 tons of a recoverable value of
605., equal to five y-ears’ supplies at the present rate of
output. The Kalgurli is a fine mine, but it lies within a
zone noted for utter unreliability of the ore shoots, so
that one never knows at what level it may go wrong.
Boulder Perseverance (W. A.) is fading day by
day. Four years ago, when shareholders received eight
shares for every one previously- held, the dividend dis-
tributions were equal to 25 per cent, on the split shares.
Each succeeding year has seen a drop of 5 per *ent. in the
rate of dividends, which for 1906 only amounted to 5
per cent. The serious falling off in ore value at depth
explains this heavy shrinkage, and future years promise
to be leaner still. As usual, a big song is made in the
annual report as to lower working costs and higher ex-
traction. Working expenditure is set down at 20s. lOd.
per ton. Total costs, however, really work out at a
shade under 295. per ton, so that the co.’s method of
putting facts and figures before shareholders is mis-
leading, and savors too much of the old Kalgoorlie game
of putting the limelight on some costs and keeping others
in the dark. No figures are given in the report as to the
assay value of ore treated or percentage of extrac-
tion obtained for the past three years, so it is
impossible to say without going through the books
whether the loud whoop about the great improvement
effected in extraction is justified or not. Taking last
year’s figures as a basis, and allowing 3s. 3d. per ton for
loss in residues, the actual profit in sight in relation to
capital is so small that it requires a keen eyesight to see
it at all. This, coupled with the continued deterioration
of values at depth, makes the future outlook a bit blue.

Great Ophir, Davyhurst (W.A.), has made a
fresh start, and it is again stated that sufticuent work-
ing capital ” has been provided. It would require
£SOOO to £lO,OOO to get the mine going, and whether
this amount has been put up it is IU IP°. S ® I ~ .
This is one of the best mines outside of Kalgoorlie,has a
large low-grade lode opened up to an underlay depth oi
3Joft., and has about 50,000 tons of ore practically in
sight. Two prospecting shafts prove that the lode con-

main workings. Want of working capital has, however,

Mararoa, Norseman (W.A.), shows continued
improvement in the bottom level, both the drive and the
rise running up to 80s. a ton for 4 s ft. wide. The co.
till next February to pay £< 000, the balance o* the
purchase price, which Manager Henderson reckoned he
could make up out of profits. But as Henderson s pre-
dictions about the fourth level were not realised, holders
have lost some of their faith in the rest of ins predictions.
The future working of the mine will be greatly aided by
the Norseman railway, the first section of which to
Higginsville, the half-way house, has been started. When
completed, this will reduce cartage by 50 miles.
Lancefield (W.A.) is still under a cloud and the
furnaces are not proving the success anticipated, lpsis
attributed by the management to the dampness of the
ore, but the real fact is that the large percentage of
arsenical pyrites, and, incidentally, of graphite, spoils the
roast. It is said that a fresh expert is coming out from
England to try his hand. # 4
The Ida H., Laverton (W. A.), looks like having
a new lease of life. In the winze below 500 ft. the reef
cut clean out ; but, on crosscutting- 25ft. east, a new
make of stone 3ft. wide, worth 3oz„ has been met. The
Ida has been a good little mine, and original holders have
more than got back their money.

Hansen and Evans, Kalgoorlie, who are work-
ing a prospecting show on the fringe of the belt beyond
the North End, have had three small trial lots, angre-
,,-ating 25 tons, treated for a return of 170 oz over the
plates. This show is on the same run of country as the
Hidden Secret, and further east than where most of the
operations of the early days were carried out. Too little
work has been done to allow of any decided opinion as to
the value of the find, hut the owners have priced it at
£5OOO. Prospectors, however, generally open then
mouths wide.

South German, Maldon (Vic.), has got the
western reef at 2000 ft, to the accompaniment o as nice a
little market jig as has been seen since the flutter over
the cutting of this make of stone in the upper level.
The hand that dealt was the hand of Esau, though
the voice was that of Jacob. Scrip was garnered
in for a week before in the neatest way conceivable ,
then support was apparently withdrawn, jobbers oouldn t
resist “chancing it,” and 48 hours later had to wondei
whether averaging at a 10s. advance was the right game
to play. Maldon certainly was having its revenge for the
hacking of the past ; but still shares had to again go
down, on a very poor amalgam return An end
must come to this gambling, even with South Ler
man The good name of the past won t avail it
the stock is to be, as it looks to-day, a market counter.
So far, the western reef has lived up to its reputation
when worked in the German Extended grou ndandthat
wasn’t much. The Block 2 stone milled from the leef by
the South German has not promised anything in the way
of divs What has to be realised, therefore, is that until
bulk lots are treated from the 2000 ft. level, buying shares
in the South German Go. is a rank gamble, with Maldon
on top most of the time.
Ajax, Daylesford (Vic.), after a long spell of
ballooning is slowly settling down towards Mother
Earth. At present it is fluttering over the place where
“ the bears ” rage, and some of the fripperies are being
torn off by their clawing. Go into the market place, and
forty different opinions are vouchsafed to account
decline. One is that there was a gold reserve on the
mine With the recollection of the dust that was raised
over the North Main Leads reserve, it is hardly P° s&lbl ®
that anything of the kind has been attempted At
all events the balance-sheet never hinted, at
such a reserve, and that ought to be pnma
facie evidence. The next story is that the reef is
narrowino- as if it were the legs of a saddle. The thna
is that some shooting has been done at the stock from
certSn quarters. Such solemn visages have been worn
hv one or two of the crowd that this is accepted as the
truth. Grumbling all along has been rife over the early
flittino- of ducks from the little Daylesford share pools.
But at the bottom of all is the fact that the spurry coun
trv isn’t panning out up to boom expectations, and the
shoots of 3 gold in the bottom level are not so rich as over-
head. There never would have been such a rise if some
of the bears had not given the chance for a squeeze.
Apart from that, when did the public do well by follow-
ing Ballarat? * * *

The glowing speeches made by Alec. Peacock
and Co. over the Welsh and Slater, Rocky Lead (Vic.),
have been succeeded by a dead silence brokem only by
calls The co. was floated in February in 32,000 shares of
±1 each, 8000 fully paid ; and preceding this the syndicate
crushed 111 tons for 414J0Z. Shares were applied for
hastily on the syndicate’s last return of 105 oz. from 30
tons, and it was prophesied that the mine s wealth would
astonish the fortunate shareholders. (Loud applause.)
A winding plant, 10 head battery, etc., were landed on the
mine, and from the 100 ft. shaft a drive was put in to cut
the lode. The golden stone is supposed to be only 30tt.
away, but all work has been stopped by the directors,
who have become liable for about £l4OO on account of
machinery, etc., and who won’t go on till the June call
(Is.) is paid. The directors seem to have mighty little
confidence now either in the reef or in the shareholders.

After a long struggle the little Magnet, Waratah
district (Tas.), has got on to a good wicket temporarily.
In the past 12 months, on its paid capital of £15,000, it
has made £11,910 net profit, of which ±6300 has been paid
in divs and div. duty—£42oo in the first, and £2lOO in the
second half the ptant has been added to lately, which
accounts for the smaller distribution in the second half.
There are 40,000 shares, which are worth about 30s. , on
the market value the year’s return has therefore been 10
ner cent.—none too much, considering where the metal
markets have been. The last half-year the output has
been 3330 tons of crude gossan and sulphide ore and con-
centrates. But the output ought not to be lumped
in this wav, for apparently it takes about 14
tons of sulphides to make one ton of concen-
trates Apparently the profit is really about 14s.
per ton of ore ; while the gossan of which there is only
about 12 months’ output in sight, and which last half-
year supplied nearly half the metal, ought to bewortlq
L £3 10s. per ton. That brings the profit from
the sulphides'* down to very small figures. The
manager says that two years’ dividends are practically
assured ” —ls. a quarter seems to be meant, or Bs. for the
two years. The lower-level developments are good, but,
after the gossan is gone, it is hard to see much profit
ahead should the lead market move £3 downward, as it is
fust about certain to do. How the 80s. per share value
s arrived at by the market it is still harder to see.

Mt Bischoff (Tas.) treats 6000 tons of tin stone per
month or 18,000 tons to provide a quarterly ss. on 12,000
shares ’ so this modest distribution on shares nominally
worth £2O is equal to 3s. 4d. per ton, which is perilously
little considering the price of tin at the moment. Lewis,
of the Anchor mine, has made a report suggesting
economies in the handling and treatment of the ore. The
milling Recovery is fairly good ; the tailings, which are
subsequently re-treated, contain 3£lb. of tin oxide pel ton
of ore, equivalent to 16 per cent, of the original metal
contents 4 Some of the best plants in England have re-
duced their losses to 21b. of black tin per ton, and Mt.
Bischoff tailings, under re-treatment, show a loss of only
2Mb. per ton of ore. The Anchor itself is treating low-
grade material containing only 311 b. of black tin pel ton ,
hut there is a great difference between saving tin from
crude me and saving it out of tailings. It is Bischoff’s
luck to have a financial nest-egg, either to get improved
machinery or to secure another mine ; for the latter
purpose the directors are to get additional power. With
high prices the old mine should be a good stand-by, and,
to make the outlook in that way better, an improvement
in one part of it occurred quite recently. The ore is still
as rich as that of many Cornish tin mines.

British Broken Hill Co., at its meeting held in
London early last month, only had the accounts to the
end of December before it, and they showed a liquid
balance of £77,000 ; but the chairman announced that at
dateoif meeting the total had got up to £115,000. Out of
this balance, as at December 31, the 3s. div. was paid (in
L.A making ss. 6d., or per cent., for the year, as
against 22J per cent, for 1905: The net profits (£84,878)
were arrived at after charging working account with
-Cao 000 spent on development and machineiy. The ore
feserves were estimated at 570,000 tons, which was 5000
tons less than 12 months previously ; and, of course, ore
tanned by drill has not been reckoned in. But the
British ore bodies are irregular and hard to gauge, and
too much reliance may easily be put on figures like these.
The Hogan-Palmer party follows up its voting
success at the Broken Hill Junction North meeting by
putting in a requisition for a special meeting to pass a
few things that won’t he good for the health of the pre-
w directors. The first little cut at them is that no
director shall hold office who doesn’t also hold 500 shares ;
and that, on a recent showing of the register, would
make it very awkward for at least four directors. In case
the present Board should get over that the special meet-
in-is also asked to say that any director may be kicked
nut when the co. feels that way ; and the recent ballot
showed that this co. feels that way pretty badly.

Queensland Copper Freeholds Ltd. now holds
more I properties ; all those of Archibald Coats and of the
Australian Copper Syndicate, of Glasgow, having been

bow Chinaman, Diagonal, Yamamilla, Crusada, and
Dobbins containing an area of about 378 acres ; we also
hi ve a third of the shares of the Mountain Home Copper

tioTof 40 aeresat Cabbage Tree Creek.” The Mountain
Home and the Cabbage Tree Creek freehold are not much

i t i vift on the Malbon : an underlie shaft and a verti-

Mt. Molloy (Q.) has had a fair development at
the 370 ft. level; the downward continuation of payable
ore is a good feature. There is a dyke formation, and
that at least may be expected to last to a great depth.
Such failures as Mt. Garnet and Muldiva have cast doubt
over N Q. mines ; but when the geological conditions
vary so much, the failure of one mine is no guide what-
ever to the future of another. The Molloy tramway to
junction with the Mareeba-Cairns railway should prove a
good thing for the Port Douglas and Mossnian district.
Mt. Molloy is only 22 miles from the coast; the extension
of the tramway beyond Rifle Creek towards Pt. Douglas
should increase settlement in that inaccessible pait.

The prospectus of Bennion’s Block the show
which was to save Croydon (Q.), but got water-logged

ss. shares, of which Bennion takes 30,000 W* e “ t

then he pays for 5000 contribs. If Bennion had called the

West Mutooroo (S. Aus.) isn’t Mutooroo, but is
miles distant. Still it pads the hoof as a Mutooroo show,
when it hasn’t even Mutooroo qualities. It is a big body
of iron sulphides with some odd splashes of copper—an
excellent flux quarry, but not a copper mine. West
Mutooroo might have been let alone to settle down to its
proper horizon, which will be tinted with the reflection
of calls, were it not for the nature of its reports. The
latest “ pitch ” is about “an important development at
the mine.” This development turns out thus:
“A bulk parcel of ore” (no quantity given) taken
from the trucks at Cockburn contains some excellent
specimens of erubescite, bornite and chalcopyrite (all
rich sulphide of copper).” This is very neat, but the
little word “ specimens” gives the show away ; for there
is nothing very special about bornite or yellow copper
pyrites specimens. The true inwardness of the story is
shown a little later, where it is stated that a truck-load
sample gave 26‘0 per cent, sulphur, while a little sample
of 121 b. gave 28'6 per cent, sulphur and I's per cent,
copper. Therefore West Mutooroo resolves itself into
a sulphur property —though 20ft. of a face isn t anything
too big when reliance has to be put on sulphur alone—
with a trace of copper. This is to be concentrated up to
40 per cent, sulphur, and carted and trained to its des*
tination, wherever that may be. Will the gentlemen who
put out this nice little specimen report also furnish the
sellin<>- price of the ore for sulphuric acid manufacture,

* * * *
W. A. Horn seconded Chairman Moreing’s
motion in London to adopt the Great t ingall report, re-
commending that the co. take an option over the Burra
Coppermine (S.A.). He told the shareholders— During
the period the mine was worked they never went below
330 ft. From that shallow depth they extracted £4,000,000
worth of copper ore. At that time there was no railway,
wages were high, and the whole of the ore had to be
carted to the seaboard, 100 miles, over an unmade road.
What is to be gained in the long run by talking rubbish
like that? Part of that Burra railway line was built as
far back as 1860 ; and trains were running into the Burra
in 1877 The mine was shut down, not because there was
no railway, but in spite of the railway. That develop-
ment work was shamefully neglected is true ; and that
the Fingall 00. is quite justified in spending £lO,OOO to
see if there is anything below the big cauldron of copper-
ore now emptied, is also true. But certainly the mine
was not shut down because it was too expensive to mine
and cart the ore ; it was shut down simply because there
was no ore to mine, and 25 railways wouldn’t have
affected that position one ounce.

“ Coote” : I reckon you can’t produce another
mine in Australasia worked like one we have here in
Tasmania—and it’s so like Tasmania. One Phillips owns
it It is a tin show at Heemskirk, perched right on the
rocky cliffs facing Trial Harbor, 14 miles from Zeehan.
It carries a lode of very rich brown tin oxide. Phillips
has a hole sunk about 12ft. right into the side of the
mountain. He breaks the stuff out, carries it up a bush
ladder in a bucket, bums it to soften it, then breaks out
the richest portion and bags it, and gets on an average
about two bags of 70 per cent, tin per week. (Five or 50
times as much could be got by different methods.) He
can get his tin packed to Zeehan for 6s. 6d. per bag, but
he says he can “ earn good enough wages ” by wheeling
it So he wheels it—two bags a time, the 14 miles over a
roiwh corduroy track, in a barrow ! When spoken to
once about selling out, he was insulted, and said that he
had work enough to last his lifetime, and he might as
well be working there as anywhere else.

From a man at Cairns (Q.), who ought to know
what he is writing about: —“ If the Chillagoe 00. would
treat ore for the small man from the numerous payable
shows within reasonable distance of railway, the whole
of the furnaces would soon be in use. Under their pre-
sent system, rarely can they keep more than two furnaces
going! yet ore can be seen stacked along the railway from
Mareeba to Chillagoe, waiting for a chance to get a
smelting turn. Men will not stay at the smelters, and
why? They can do better elsewhere, under better con-
ditions. The officers (and their name is legion) are highly
paid; the rank and file, including the mechanics, are
under-paid. Result, a permanent staff cannot be kept,
and men only take the work as a stopgap. The directors
surely must know this in Melbourne, and it seems absurd
of them to tell their shareholders that once the
Almaden to Etheridge railway is completed there
will be an inexhaustible supply of ore. There
will be a vast supply, but it will be refractory.
And, in any case, it will be some two years before the
line gets to the Etheridge, and meantime share-
holders and bondholders will be growing impatient; and
when they come to ascertain that the profits of this
Mareeba to Chillagoe railway are being swamped in pay-
ing salaries of a huge staff, while four of their smelting
furnaces are often idle for want of ore, though the ore is
waiting, there will be ructions. Already some of the
most forceful of the men who are interested talk of writ-
ing to the London press to try to get the bondholders
to take a hand in altering things. Let the Melbourne
board authorise Mr. Back, the general manager, who is_ a
universal favorite, to pay good wages to the mechanics
and men, and cut the price for treatment of ore down to
the actual cost of treatment, plus 10s. per ton for the co.,
as they stated they would do in their original prospectus,
and there will be no dearth of ore for the whole of their
furnaces, and the co. will pay dividends before the Ethe-
ridge line is completed.
The mostly-English co. that now owns the
Premantle (W.A.) smelters is not having- any too much
luck with its northern prospecting copper shows. There
was some hope that things would be rushed along, and
the smelters kept going ; but that has been found impos-
sible The Smelter 00., which was engineered by Kauf-
man’ got on to velvet through handling the rich ore from
Golden Horseshoe ; apart from that, it wouldn t have
had a much less disastrous time than the old co. had.

A new improved drill maker and sharpener is
superseding the somewhat expensive method of sharpen-
ing drills by hand. The machine will be welcomed by all
mining 1 cos., because the men are able to sharpen up to
about 140 drills an hour without trouble, consequently
increasing the output and reducing the cost, which secures
to the mines substantial benefit. These machines have
been supplied to leading mines every where by the makers,
Messrs. Word Bros., of San Francisco, who have ap-
pointed W. & J. Lempriere, of Melbourne and Sydney, the
sole Australian agents. The machine has the distinction
of being a drill maker as well as drill sharpener.*

The Dorset Bucket Dredging Company is
building a complete plant for its claim in Tasmania.
The dredge is to be capable of treating 3000 cubic yards
of material per day ; the buckets have each a capacity of
6 cubic feet; and the tables have a surface of over 2880
square feet; the ladder is 66 feet in length between the
centres, and can dredge to a depth of 38 feet; a inch
centrifugal pump supplies the water to treat the material
in the sluice boxes, and in addition there is a 6 inch high
lift pump to operate on the nozzles : the material is dis-
integrated in a revolving screen, which is 33 feet long and
6 feet in diameter ; the machinery is supplied by power
from two 60 H.H.P. horizontal engines which are supplied
bv steam from an externally fired multitubular boiler,
working under a pressure of 1501bs. to the square inch.
The p ntoons to carry the plant are constructed of
Tasmanian hardwood, and are 124 feet long by 40 feet
wide To facilitate working at night the dredge is fitted
up with an efficient electric lighting plant. The whole
nlant is the largest of its kind in the Southern Hemis-
phere, and is in the main being constructed by the Austral
Otis Engineering Company, of South Melbourne, under
the supervision of Robert McLintock, Eng.neer, of
Dunedin.* «

“ Lindy Lou” : Be “ Quan ” (B. 23/5/’O7)
and his growl concerning places with triplicate
names. A little village, situated barely twenty-
seven miles from Sydney, on the Western line,
can raise “Quan” to the extent of one. The
railway station isMountDruitt; the post-office and
public school display their shingles as Colyton ;
the church rears up its board as Rupertswood ;
while the inhabitants claim that they live at
Rope’s Creek. Heaven only knows how many
previous convictions the place has that it should
be driven to hide itself behind three aliases.
THE STOCK QUESTION.
PUBLICAN : “No mistake, this ’ere Donkin's a
smart bloke! ”
THE TEAT-PULLER: “Dunno. How many cows
kin he milk a day! ”
Somebody bought the right, title and in-
terest in 2000 donkeys for £lO in Adelaide the
other day. The donkeys are running at large on
certain mountainous State lands in S.A., and the
purchaser has the sole right to capture them. He
has already bought a donkey trap, and left for the
scene of action. But some politician has missed
an opportunity of buying an ideal Legislative
Council constituency dirt cheap. Fancy 2000
good constituents going at a little over a penny
each !
“G.S.K.”: The harangue of “Back-
blox ” (B. 16/5/’O7) re hotels met with in Outback
coach-travelling is a gross libel on some of the
most praiseworthy and plucky Australians. Have
travelled Outback from Broken Hill to Queens-
land, by way of Milparinka and Tibooburra, and
can vouch that there are NO “ half-crown ” hotels
along that route. Have ordered meals in private
dining-room at Milparinka, Tibooburra, Iduna
Park, and other wayside hotels, and never paid
more than Is. or Is. 6d. per meal. The hotels at
Fowler’s Gap, Iduna Park, and the others I have
mentioned, compare favorably with any of the
second-class hotels of the city. Again, re “ Back-
blox ” talk about having to get down to open
gates on the road. Does he recollect how many
gates exist between Broken Hill and Wompah
(Queensland border fence)? Let me tell him.
Not more than four —in a distance of 246 miles.
His assertion that the road is often blocked by
camel teams seems to show that “ Backblox ” has
never been beyond Argent-street, Broken Hill;
else he would know that the camel teams ha-ve
their own “pads,” which are always away from
the road. Surely, “ Backblox ”is a city dreamer
or a pal of Judkins.
* * * *
A gentleman whose signature resembles a
mad beetle writes
Re Narracoorte caves and petrified blackfellow, “ Flin-
derino ” and “ Brice Burn ” are out of it. In the winter
of 1862 I remember seeing a commercial traveller, his
pack of samples in a bluey across his shoulders. And, by
the way, he was minus boots, which were slung round his
neck, tied together with laces or summat. Later on,
it became known that the samples consisted of the
petrified blackfellow referred to.
The history of the defunct nigger read thus: In the
early ’forties (say 1844) the blacks were troubling the
local wool kings by discarding ’possum for mutton.
Whereupon one of the magnates, taking' the law into his
own hands, determined to stop at least one Combo from
continuing in the mutton butchering' business. After
the discussion the retailer took up his quarters in the
cave, where he remained sole occupant for 17 years or
thereaway. The commercial traveller referred to, scent-
ing satnpies, removed the mummified remains, but was
overtaken by the limb of the law, and the ancient butcher
was returned to his stalactited chamber, and securely (as
it was thought) walled in. He disappeared again, and
was a very much-travelled nigger when he finally reached
a museum (some say the British Museum) in England.
While it was in the custody of the police at Mount Gambier
I inspected the sample, which was an ordinary aboriginal,
in a semi-stooping position, and resembled hard, dark
tanned leather.
* * * *
“Mangrove”: I agree with David H.
Richards (B. 25/4/’O7) that the horse is an intelli-
gent animal, but“D.H.R.” undoubtedly pokes
borak at the public when he refers to the pre-
cautions taken by drovers’ horses to avoid
detection when appropriating grass belonging to
t’other chap. The drover’s prad is like his boss
—when hungry or thirsty, he takes whatever he
comes across. The only difference is that the
boss, by experience, knows and fears the sacred
rights of “proputty,” of which the gee-gee takes
no account. As to horses’ keenness of sight,
“D.H.R.” is correct. Time and again I have
known a horse detect a cow sneaking off camp at
night to go back to the calf which she left behind
on the track during the day. This, too, when the
half-sleepy rider knew nought of the hull’s sister’s
escape. Ride a good night horse round a cattle
camp, when the bovines are contented and at rest,
and, as he crawls around on his heat, like a thirsty
John plodding through Surry Hills in December,
he seems the laziest quadruped on the face of the
planet. But mount the same horse the following
night, when the cattle are restless and inclined to
rush. As he steps out springily, with flashing
eyes and ears pricked, listening intently for the
slightest movement among the mob, he seems a
bunch of nerves. A corner of the cattle (scared,
perhaps, by the cry of a night bird) may run in.
You will feel your horse’s heartbeat like a piston-
rod till the cattle settle down again. But let the
mob rush, and you experience a sensation with
which no other can compare. You are borne
madly along by an animal possessed, that will do
or dare anything in his mad desire to head the
cattle. Nor will his ardor cool off till the beasts
are once more steady, and give no sign of further
trouble. Then he becomes the same lazy, in-
different, hoof-dragging, old noddy as before,
drifting.along in his sleep, with no care for any-
thing under the canopy of night.

“Janor”: Can corroborate “Silas Snell’s”
statement (B. 9/5/’O7) that “the Australian bear
can’t always he attacked with impunity,” for
away back in the ’6o’s I witnessed a duel
between a bloke and a bear, and the bear came
out on top. Mr. Koala was enjoying a pleasant
Sunday afternoon in the cool shade of a leafy
stringy-bark tree, foreninst the pub, and his
eloquence annoyed the bloke, and spoiled the
taste of his beer, and made him so dashed wild
that he walked up the tree to “wooden ” out the
animal. The bear roosted on a long horizontal
limb, and when the hunter appeared in the crown
of the tree, he waltzed gracefully further out.
The bloke rapidly overhauled him. Then, iust as
the “woodener” was about to fall, there was a
crash. The limb parted from the trunk, and, with
the same old thud, piled the scrap in a tangled
heap of leaves and branches at the base of the
tree. Mr. Koala emerged unhurt, and scooted
for the timber, uttering unprintable remarks.
The bloke was dead. I drank the rest of his
beer.

“ PoLstan ” : Talking of tiger-cats (“ Silas
Snell,” B. 9/5/’O7), I once caught one in a rabbit-
trap, and it was also a boshter. Going round
with a lantern, I was under the delusion that my
•victim was a mere bunny. Then I suddenly felt
350 hooks tearing me to smithers. I created re-
moteness between myself and the beast. The
next morning my mate and Iwent to investigate ;
we found the trap still securely pinned, with giant
pussy mounting reluctant guard, and looking
indignant. “Letth’ poor beggar out,” said my
mate, with a soft expression of mercy on his
countenance. “ Pussy, pussy, pussy ! Poor
puss ”- The gentle animal made a leap, and
tore a pound or so out of Jim’s leg. Jim’s face
lost its sympathetic expression, and seizing another
trap by the spike, he passed the tiger one on the
ear and laid her out. Then he travelled around
the landscape on one leg, while I took the carcase
out and threw it many yards. But no sooner had
the corpse struck the ground than it burst into
galvanised action and swarmed all over Jim once
more. Jim bore down towards me through the
yell-laden air, but I’d had enough cat, and
making a laconic burst for the handiest tree, I
slid up the trunk, and pulled the tree up after me.
Jim passed on, leaving a comet-like tail of dust,
noise, leaves, &c., behind him as he went. Ulti-
mately he got the cat by the throttle, and spoilt
it, but not without sustaining great damage dur-
ing the frenzied process.

An item of interest to anyone with a bad
cough is that which tells him of an almost certain
cure. Angier’s Emulsion cures the most obstinate
cough. There is nothing equal to it for the lungs,
while it is a great aid to digestion and a splendid
tonic. In three sizes, of all chemists.*
Cormorants (shags) and turtles were, a
couple of years ago, declared thistles, or noxious
weeds, or pests, or something, in S. Aus.; and the
local small boy of the beach earns his Sunday-
school copper collection by slaughtering them at
a penny a time. For some reason or other, the
Lands Department has charge of the cormorant
and the turtle, presumably because they belong
to the sea, and in two years it has paid out for
over 20,000 of the fish-destroying birds, and for
nearly 60,000 turtles.

“ Flinderino ” writes from S.A. :
The seaport towns in the south-east of South Australia
take the bun for deadness. Port Macdonnell, where poet
Gordon spent his cradle days, is quiet enough, but
Beaehport and Kingston are much deader. The old-
fashioned white, sandstone houses, and the chalky-white
streets make one’s eyes tired with the glare ; and their
brightness forms a marked contrast with the inhabitants’
duiness. The arrival of the train three times a week
provides a little excitement. The youngsters, who are
not quite so tired as their elders, walk out half-a-mile to
meet the train, and ride hack on the footboard. About
once a month a coastal craft gives a look in and lands a
case of kerosene, a bag of sugar, and occasionally a case
of herrings or sardines. Once every couple of years some-
body capsizes a fishing-boat and gets drowned, but other-
wise death or sickness is unknown. Undertakers and
doctors are not required. As there are no wooden houses
to speak of, a house-afire seldom occurs, so 'even that
excitement is denied the residents. In the wool season a
commotion is caused by one or two oversea vessels calling
in to pick up wool. Each town possesses a harbor master,
and he comes in very handy to take around the plate
on a Sunday, or to take the chair at a concert.
Once a year, however, extraordinary excitement pre-
vads at Beaehport, when a steamer arrives from New-
castle with a load cf coal for the Mount Gambier gas-
works. Men are very scarce in the district, and, to assist
in unloading the coal, labor has to he got from Millicent
and other inland towns, including Tantanoola. (Tanta-
noola is where the tigers come from.) Farm laborers,
shearers, and rouseabouts cause amusement to the sea-
faring folk by coming aboard armed with potato spades,
hay forks, and miscellaneous agricultural implements.
Some of the men have never been on a ship before in their
lives. Many of the younger ones gaze upon the ocean for
the first time. If the sea happens to be a little choppy,
the majority of them have to throw up the job before it
is half finished.
Beaehport owns a salt-water lake known as the Pool of
Siloam. It is credited with possessing curative powers
of no small value. Anything, from an ache in the toe-
nail to a pain in the pulpit, is cured by bathing in the
Pool. Like the Blue Lake at Mount Gambier, and several
other lakes in the neighborhood, the Pool of Siloam is
“ unfathomable.”
“Itan”: At most of the oyster-bearing
inlets of the North Coast of N.S.W., close to the
oyster beds, there are large deposits of shells,
many feet above high-water mark. In some cases
the shell refuse is feet deep. One local theory is
that the coast has gradually risen, and carried the
shells with it. Another—which looks likelier—is
that our black brother Bill liked his oyster
supper. It seems pretty certain that the inland
blacks used to make yearly visits to the
coast for a fish diet when they tired of roast
’possum and baked snake, and as there are no
traces of battlefields near the oyster beds, or
skulls, or human bones, the chances are that
during the period there was some sort of tribal
truce. Probably the brethren swapped hunting
grounds during fish-time.

“Agricola ” : A bullock, though an unim-
aginative-looking beast, is yet a victim of peculiar
fancies. As stated by various scribes, he will
prefer death to working in company with a bull.
Another of bis peculiarities is a love for the smell
of new moleskins. The bullocky who dons a pair
of new moleskin rammies, and then attempts to
yoke up his team, is a man deserving of all
sympathy. The brutes crowd round him, and
sniff, and bellow delightedly, and try to lick him
wherever they can reach him with their tongues.
He may bellow
back at- them,
and spank
them on the
horns with his
spanner, but
unless he
changes those
pants he’ll be
lucky if he
finishes yoking
up inside an
hour. I once
saw a bul-
locky’s new-
chum assistant
sitting on a
fence, shout-
ing for help,
with sixteen
apparently in-
furiated bul-
locks fighting
and struggling
for a place in
the front row
of the orches-
trastalls. Even
after it had
been explained
to him that his
new moles
were the rea-
son of the aj>
plause his
mind was not
relieved, and
he tendered
his resigna-
tion, to take
effect from
straight-away.
The other day
I met a bnl-
locky gazing
more in anger
than in sorrow
at half the
gable and one
chimney of a new pair of moleskins which he
had left in an exposed position while he
went for a billy of water. The team had
divided the balance of the garment as a
memento, and put it in its scrap-book. That
bullocky didn't say anything, but his eyi s
bulged like red tram-lamps, and he made
large chewing motions with his mouth, and every
now and then he choked, and his head swelled to
twice its proper size, and he leaped in the air and
tried to reach down a star. His countenance was
crimson, with a leaning to purple, and I thought. I
saw a faint smoke arise from him. He seemed to
want to say something. In the end I believe he
wrote it down and handed it to the Chief Bullock.

“David H. Richards”: Apropos of that
ant-campaign, chronicled by “Draillibor” (B.
16/5/’O7), I once watched a pitched battle between
an army of meat-ants and a force of the tiny black
order. The carnivorous ones seemed to be secesh-
ing from their home, and in their course they
invaded hostile territory. The invaded immedi-
ately foregathered in great numbers, and falling
valiantly upon their enemies, smote them, hip and
thigh, to some considerable tune. The fight isn’t
always to the bulky, nor the race to the loud.
The intrepid little ebon objects fastened in great
numbers to the legs of the meat breed, whose
utmost efforts to shake them off were futile. Glue
wasn’t a circumstance to those ants. The engage-
ment only lasted about fifteen minutes. The
meat-chewers who managed to get through the
fighting lines fled like a daily paper looking for a
Federal capital. At the end of the fray theie
were huge heaps of beef-ants, defunct and
mangled, and crowds of little black ones, wiping
their foreheads, and saying what a fine morning it
was ! Another time I saw an energetically-con-
ducted argument betwixt a party of sugar-ants and
the meat specimens. The meat-eaters licked the
other variety hollow, and pursued them to their
nest, where they put most of them to the sword.
I think this last was a religious war.
■* * * »
“Combo” from the Northern Territory :
The native question has been thrashed out in the other
States of the Commonwealth, but South Australia has
fieen quite callous in the matter of the aborigines, and
the Northern Territory presents a shocking picture of
neglect. There is no law in the Territory to prevent the
blacks mixing with other races. They have no reserves,
are not given rations or blankets, and loaf around the
camps for a livelihood. A shocking state of immorality
exists. Europeans and Asiatics are permitted to live
with native women, and nearly every camp, whether
Chinese or otherwise, has one or more females, some
mere children under 16 years of age. There is no pro-
tection for the natives. The medical officer in Palmer-
ston is the Protector, bub he is only allowed £5O per
annum, which amount is spent in the town. There the
aborigines are well clothed, and to all outward appear-
ances well cared for. But what a cruel picture the
back country presents ! The principal population is
Chinese. In some places there are no whites at all;
the Driffield, Union and Burrundie are examples.
Young children are obtained from the blacks in
return for provisions, tobacco, liquor, or opium ;
and the police do not interfere. They will tell you
they have no power to interfere, as there is no law to
prevent a black female living with a Chinaman. In my
peregrinations all over the Chow-infested mining areas
I have been told of and have witnessed harrowing
cruelties to native children —horrors that cannot he pub-
lished in the columns of a newspaper. Now that opium
is not so easily procurable as formerly, every Chintse
humpy has the inevitable bottle of square gin, which is
kept chiefly for the lubras, although the Chows consume a
g-reat deal themselves. I invite bookseller Cole or any
other Asiatic advocate to come up to the Northern
Territory and spend a month or so amongst these pagan
hovels. If the spectacles he will see there do not cure
him, then he is past redemption.

Now, if from any sudden chill
A cold your nose should tint,
On no account take anything
For it but Peppermint.
We all are liable to colds,
Or frequently to cough,
On hand keep Woods’ mighty Cure,
Don’t put this matter off.*
KITTY MAGINNIS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oi’m not Oirish—ye’ll only nadehearin’
Me spake to be shure that Oi’m not;
But to-night Oi’m a true son av Erin,
From the sole av me fut to the shpot
Where the hair av me head growin’ thin is ;
Oi’ve been dancin’, colloguin’, and all,
All night wid Miss Kitty Maginnis,
The belle av the Eganstown hall!
Aw, Geraldine, Sheila, and Eily !
They floated like quanes before me,
Inthrojooced wid a bow be me wily
Ould frind, Pat Maginnis, M.O. !
But faith, Oi am thinkin’ ’tis pardon
Oi’d be askin’ av Pat, did he guess
Av thim toimes at the ind av th’ gyarden
Wid Miss Kitty that kep’ sayin’ “ Yes ! ”
In th’ dark wid an Oirishman’s sisther,
’Tis there that ye’ll learn to feel glad,
Wid y’r head loike a shwellin’ orchisthra,
And y’r hear-rt loike th’ divil gone mad !
F’r y’r sins wan he wan are forgiven,
Till y’re boulder than iver y’ve been ;
Thin oh, ’tis the porthals av Hivin—
And there’s just th’ wan saint to be seen !
Wid her pretty whoite hands in a quiver,
And her heart batin’ clear as a bell,
And her eyes thryin’ har-rder than iver
To hoide th’ swate sayorets they tell.
Wid no daylight betune y’r two noses,
Yet you thremblin’ in fear y’ shud miss
Her mouth pink an’ tendher as roses—
Och, ’tis howly, an Oirish gurl’s kiss !

REAPING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ah, there’s singing at the sowing
And there’s laughing at the growing,
But there’s weeping at the mowing
Of the wild-oat yield.
Yes, there’s weeping, weeping, weeping
At the bitter harvest reaping,
As the scythe goes sweeping, sweeping
O’er the wild-oat field.
VIC.

“Harry C.N.,” writing from Winton
(Queensland), gives a cheerless picture of a journey
between Longreach and that town :
Five or six miles outside the town dead cattle could be
seen lying along the stock route, and this continued for
the 122 miles we covered. It appeared these cattle were
amongst the 70,000 that were driven along this track last
November. Now and again one would notice a dead
horse. The only vegetation consisted of the gidyea,
“ the dead finish,” mulga, spinifex, lignum vitae, and the
strange “roly-poly.” To those who have never seen this
plant, it is one of Nature’s marvels. Growing to a height
of about 20in., it looks like the back of a porcupine. It
overturns sideways when a strong wind arrives, and rolls
along and so scatters its seeds over the soil. At Maneroo
we encountered great mobs of sheep, one lot numbering
some 13,000. In many cases the ewes were dropping
their lambs, and the lambs were being left on the road to
die. In one place where hundreds of cattle were seen
lying dead, thousands of young lambs were noticed dead
also. The heat of the sun dries the carcases, and there is
absolutely no stench.
* * * *
“Rotorua” writes from Waihi (Maori-
land): —
You give far too much credit to G. P. Donnelly (B.
2/5/’O7) and far too little to his wife and to the Maoris of
the East Coast. G.P.’s mand among the Maoris is nil,
except in racing matters. His wife is the great element
in the partnership. In any case, most of the East Coast
Maoris are grafters, so the Donnelly entourage is no excep-
tion. All through Hawke’s Bay and Poverty Bay will
be found successful Maori farmers. At the Motu, above
Gisborne, is a co-operative butter factory, started years
ago by whites with only two dusky suppliers ; to-day, all
suppliers are Maoris, and the brown brethren milk up to
50 cows, and draw up to £6O per month for butterfat.
In the Cook and Waiapu counties the natives have
recently erected over 100 miles of telephone, and they
have subscribed liberally to the new Waiapu hospital.
In the Bay of Plenty they have long grown large
maize crops, and here in our bush are half a score
whose graft compares favorably with that of their white
mates. The limited Maori population here supplies the bulk
of Waihi’s fish. Unfortunately, the prophet disease,
endemic in Taranaki, is sporadic on the East Coast, and is
responsible for much intermittent idleness. . Toa’s mana
has survived the non-materialisation of King Ned, and
the Coast is now anxiously awaiting an eruption of White
Island and an accompanying tidal wave, which is to
sweep all pakehas from the Bay. The harder-headed
Gisborne natives stopped Toa on the frontier, and threw
mud at his alleged inspiration. Given individualisation
of titles, and most Maoris would graft as well as the
average white. Small blame to them if they fail to see
the fun of working for the loafers of the hapu.
THE MOTOR GAR.
From a pedestrian’s point of view.
“ Lindy Lou " : “ Oxo” (B. 9/5/’O7) won-
ders that the crows which gorge on the carcases
of poisoned rabbits do not fall victims to their
appetites. It is only necessary to examine a car-
case after the crows have abandoned it to discover
the reason of their immunity. The stomach and
entrails are torn out and dragged about, but
not devoured, and those are the only paits
which contain any poison. Of course, there is
plenty of the smell of phosphorus about the poi-
soned rabbit to warn his consumer to go slow.
Foxes, also, are very careful to let the phos-
pliorised one’s stomach alone, though they bite off
the head and eat samples of the body.

HIS REV: “Ah, Pat! Pat! If ye persist in goin' in there ye'll he lost.”

Twice at least did the old military have a
bit of fighting to do in N.S. Wales. In 1816,.
when the Hawkesbury aborigines committed many
outrages on the settlers, Governor Macquarie
proclaimed martial law in the country to the west
of Vinegar (now Rouse) Hill, and sent the soldiers
against our colored brother Binghi. Again, in
1823, when the Bathurst blacks gave considerable
trouble to the local whites, Governor Brisbane
pursued a similar course in the country west of
Mount York. The result was bad for our trouble-
some brother in both cases. In fact, before the:
campaigns ended, he was mostly our departed
brother.
