“Agricola”: Usually, in this district- —
Tumut (N.S.W.) —the emu is an infrequent fowl,,
excepting in the hills ’way back from population.
But since the ripening of the briar berries, there
has been a great influx of the large rooster and his.
family. They wander through the briar-infested
country in mobs of 15 or 20. Their workmanship,
in skinning the briar-bushes is good. The other
day I noticed half-a-dozen heavily loaded bushes,,
and the next morning there was scarcely a berry
to be seen, but the de-
ficiency was accounted for
by the number of emu
tracks. The emus are dis-
tinctly unpopular with
the smaller birds, on
account of the size of their
appetites. A flock of
loudly protesting parrots,
accompanies each mob of
emus, but the ungainly-
looking objects dine
stolidly, and do not even
bother to gaze in the direc-
tion of the chatterers.
Rabbits also accompany
them, but from a different
motive, for the emus, in
roughly snapping off the
berries, shake a good num-
ber to the ground. The
bunnies have not the
slightest fear of them, and
scramble and fight around their legs for the
coveted morsels, and find themselves ignored also.
The emu seems to have less capacity for taking
notice than a Chinese joss.
» * * *
The land-owner in Australia is mostly a
vandal. He not only destroys the timber without
leaving even a shade tree here and there, but he
takes no pride in any landmark. When W. C.
Wentworth built a homestead at Burburgate
(Boggabri, N.S.W.) he left intact an aboriginal
graveyard, where there were some trees carved by
the blacks m a most interesting fashion. But to-
day the graveyard is levelled and the trees are
gone to the sawmill. Even the True Cross would
go to the sawmill if the Australian big landlord
could get at it.
The Bulletin closed its mean man com-
petition sometime back, otherwise Barragan, hero
of this story, would at least have been considered
worthy of dishonorable mention. Barragan is a
dairyman on the edge of an outback suburb that
has grown up among the original gums and scrub.
For years he had in his employ, as herdsman, an
old man known as Teddy. Teddy was supposed
to be a bit daft, and certainly he was very vener-
able. Last winter’s cold and wet got him down,
and he became unable to do more than crawl
about, so he was discharged from his ancient
office. Teddy seemed to be incapable of grasping
the fact that he had ‘" got brusher,” and used to
go after Barragan’s cows as usual, and the spec-
tacle of Barragan running the decrepit veteran
•off his premises ceased to excite the interest of
the neighbors. But one day a man caught Barra-
gan kicking Teddy through the gate, and repri-
manded him for his cruelty. “Show a little
humanity,” said the stranger. “Humanity’s all
very well,” replied Barragan, “but I ain’t goin’
to let the old beggar play no dirty tricks on me.
Why, if I didn’t watch him he’d crawl under my
bouse to die.”

“The Waster,” from Condoboliri(N.S. W.):
I have been trying to trap foxes lately, with indifferent
success, but with some excitement. I was using 141 b.
half-moon dog-traps. This morning, while going round
the traps, I put up a gigantic emu, whicn raced away
along the netting fence I was following. Against this
fence were two traps. He missed the first one, but
plumped his pedal extremity into the second, and then
things occurred. The trap was merely attached to a light
piece of wood, and the emu tore the whole lot out of the
ground at once, and began describing revolutions back
along the fence towards me. As the whirling mass of feet
and feathers, dead grass, and dog-trap revoluted past me
I fired a charge of No.- 4 into space. The result
was to w-heel the whole calamity in my direction. I
jumped for the fence, but was too late. Something
bumped me, and I was knocked yards. I looked
up in time to see the travelling earthquake col-
lide with the top wires of the fence. I don’t know what
he wanted the wires for, but he took them along ; also
half a post and a considerable amount of netting. In the
next paddcck a black boy was hunting up the plough
horses, on foot. He heard the row when the fence went,
and looked back, to see the whizzing mass of devastation'
making straight for him. I suppose the emu reckoned
all roads led to Rome just then, if he studied tne matter
at all; anyway, those were the roads he was travelling.
The darkie at once lost all interest in horses, and gave a
splendid exposition of the art of tree-climbing. He w'as
a quarter of a mile from me, but I could see his eyes glar-
ing like the headlights of a train, and they stuck out so
that I could have blown them off if I had breathed hard
enough. Our colored brother was up among the twigs
when the end-of-the-world passed beneath in a cloud of
dust, feathers and vegetation, the trap-chain rattling,
and the hoarse, whistling grunt of the emu cutting
through it all. It was too much. With a yell, he leapt
up a bit further, the branches gave, and he fairly
hummed by the time he hit the ground.
As I went into the homestead for a cart to shift the
ruins, I noticed a gap suddenly cloven in the sky-line of
a low range of hills about ten miles to the northward.
I have been wondering since if the emu passed that way.
I DON'T CARE!
Trouble’s knockin’ at the door !
I don’t care !
Grief and Woe play on the floor—
I don’t care !
Spoon-fed chickens know no joy,
I’ve had heaps without, alloy—
More, maybe, than was my share —
I don’t care !
Fortune knocked when I was out!
I don’t care !
Often-times I’ve felt her clout—
I don’t care !
Fools make money, wise men hold
With the miser’s clutch to gold ;
Win and waste—for want prepare !
I don’t care !
My best boy spliced my pal to-day
I don’t care !
Caused a flutter, quite aufait !
I don’t care!
There are Johnnies far more true,
Brown-eyed, grey-eyed, hazel, blue !
Man’s the bait in Hymen’s snare !
I don’t care !
Death is calling soon, they say,
I don’t care !
All must go the Wormy Way !
I don’t care !
When the scoutin’ shadow comes,
Blow no bugles, beat no drums—
I ain’t frightened, I’ll be there—
I don’t care !

THE METHODIST MUG.
Hearken, brethren, ’tis Fitchett that’s prating
If he fell to this planet from space,
’Midst the millions of sects here gyrating
He could pick out the Methodist’s face.
If a Methody’s phiz, as he down flits
To earth, smites him hard on the luff,
One glance is enough, Fitchett knows it’s
A Methodist’s mug.
Such discernment, dear brethren, 's o’er-rated ;
Dull, indeed, is the man who can’t trace
In lineaments lank, elongated.
The cheerless, cold, Methodist’s face,
If there’s one thro’ life’s revels and joys sits
Sour and dour with the scowl of a Thug,
By such signs, brother Fitchett doth know it’s
A Methodist’s mug.

OF COURSE NOT.
The circumstances of the election do
not indicate that the Council, as such,
has fallen in the estimation of the elec-
tors.—Argus on the tired Vic. Leg. Council
elections.
Forinstance, this sort of thing could not

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Don’t blame poor little Judkins. When
he makes a fuss about nothing it’s only because
he thinks it his bounding duty.
T. M. Slattery, who has been doing well
in California, has crossed over to England to re-
present some American interests there.
The Bulletin feels consoled a little for
the loss of Peter Airey out of Queensland politics.
Now “P. Luftig” will have time to write verses
again.
“Hiphen” (from London); The Daily
Mail described Tommy Bent, on his arrival
here, asa “fine, upstanding, robust man—atypical
colonial.” Help!
Newton J. Moore, Premier of Westralia,
on his way to Brisbane got his first glimpse of the
east coast of Australia. He has only been out of
his native Groperland twice.
“Evangelist ” and show-woman Ada Ward
says, “I would never trust a minister of the
gispel who smokes.” Well, what about trusting
a preacheress who wears corsets and tight boots
and hair-dye ?
The democratic Scott Bennett who, after
one experience of Vic. Parliament, left Ballarat a
prey to Anti-Sosh, is now manager of a store run
on socialistic lines in Melbourne. Scotr lectures
on Sundays on “The New Theology.”
Bishop Marsden, Australia’s first home-
grown gaiters, born in Sydney in 1832, is still
living—in England—though it is 22 years since he
gave up the See of Bathurst. He is the son of
Charles, who was the only son of Parson Marsden,
of early N.S. Wales.
The Queensland Assembly acquired a par-
son at the recent elections. A retired sin-shifter —
Brentnall—has long hung up his shovel hat in the
Upper House, but the shepherd or toiler in the
vineyard is a novelty in the Assembly. The new
sky-admiral is Adamson, of Maryborough.
Passed out suddenly through apoplexy,
Dr. James Cox, of Collins-street, Melbourne. His
diploma (M. 8., Melbourne) was dated 1875. For
many years he had been recognised as the leading
aural surgeon of Victoria, and was the pioneer
operator for the removal of post-nasal growths.
Mr. Stephen Forster, who died at Peter-
sham, Sydney, last week, aged 71 years, was an
old-time Australian, having arrived with his
parents, in the early ’fifties. Soon after his
arrival, the father, also Stephen Forster, started as
a blacksmith in Harrington-street, near Gallows
Hill. He developed a big iron business, and was
the first to manufacture iron bedsteads in Aus-
t -alia, an industry still continued by his grandsons
at Ultimo.
NO DIFFERENCE NOW.
Under a proposed new law goods sent to Britain from
the colonies will have to be stamped “Made Abroad,”
and be in that respect on the same footing as those of
Germany, France, and other nations.— Daily Paper.
THE OLD MAN : “ You come in the back way
after this—same as the others.”
A much-convicted Victorian casta horrible
reflection at Acting-Premier Davies the other
day. After he had spent a Pleasant Sunday
Afternoon parcelling up portables in a Warragul
draper’s shop, they arrested him once more; and
then he turned out to be a man whom Davies had
lately released from Pentridge, without any
undue publicity, on the ground that he was a
poor, helpless, broken old person, and beyond any
further mischief.
Died up North, John Archibald, who was
“ called ” to the Queensland Legislative Council
in 1897, and managed to sit there 10 years without
doing anything worth mentioning. Originally a
school teacher, Archibald turned his hand to many
things after he got to Queensland. He helped
to fine Charters Towers’ earliest drunks, and then
was made warden at Ravensw r ood. But the tail-
end of his life was spent grinding wheat—he was
the largest part of the Dominion and Brisbane
Milling Cos. —and he was a lucky speculator in
many things besides wheat and flour.
Joe Wolinski, son of the Rabbi, and
painter of Sydney Art Gallery picture, “ After
Life’s Fitful Fever,” is in the front rank of suc-
cessful Australian artists in London, with three
pictures to his credit in the Royal Academy. Joe
was a sign-writer, with a fine tenor voice. He
has pursued the Muses —Music and Painting—
together for some ten years, and neither of the
ladies seems to be jealous.
There is no danger of Australian enthusiast
Fred McCubbin echoing, on return from his trip
to Europe, the sentiments of another (imported)
personage in Melbourne paint circles. “Do you
know,” the latter dignitary solemnly assured a
“welcome home” assemblage, “I never once
thought of Australia all the time I was away.”
No one emitted a crushing response, but even a
novice in thought-reading could interpret the
silence as “ That’s all right, old man. Australia
didn’t fret itself into an early mausoleum about
you.”
“ S.S.” : Purves, K.C., saying nice things
about Panton, on the occasion of the latter’s
birthday and the celebration of his belated retire-
ment from the Melbourne Police Court bench,
assured the brusque P.M. that he had net only
the admiration and respect of the Bar. but was
appreciated by all who knew him, even by those
lie sentenced. Lawyer Purves has often been
authorised to speak for those gentry, but here he
goes beyond his brief. As a matter of fact,
Panton, P.M., is the best-hated man in Mel-
bourne Gaol, less for his sentences than for his
parts of speech. It was rarely that the long,
angular P.M. sent a man to gaol without an
angry homily which embodied the magistrate’s
very aristocratic view of men and things, and
prisoners bitterly resent that as an abuse of
privilege.
“ Scribe ” : The Rev. W. Fifcchett’s re-
mark that he knows Anglicans by their collars,
Baptists by the cut of their hair, and Methodists
by the kindness and intellectuality of their faces,
suggests two or three things; but, firstly, that the
Rev. Fitchett is a Methodist parson. He is also
editor of a magazine that reeks of sour paste, and
has the track of the shears all over it. In this
publication we find the kindness of W. H. Fitchett
to W. H. Fitchett, and the intellectuality of
everybody ele. The magazine is called Ltfe, and
in the midst of Life Mr. Fitchett is in debt to all
his contemporaries. Therein he demonstrates
that the saving grace he possesses is the grace of
saving by taking for nothing what ordinary con-
scionable sinners would pay for. He holds fast
to that which is good, as the Scripture teacheth.
Cash is good. I sent a book to Life for review.
This intellectual Methodist with the good, kind
face, scissored out a whole chapter, and printed
it, but he did not give my hooka notice. This is
Methodistical kindness with a method, but “if he
he not kind to me, what care I how kind he be ! ”
One of the most curious things about the
House of Dodery, now coming to pieces in Tas-
mania, is that Dodery himself is a Tipperary man.
That a man from the very stronghold of revolution
in Ireland should give his name to a House of
little Fat men in Tasmania is one of the queer
happenings of politics. Dodery was born and
reared in Clonmel, the town in which Smith
O’Brien and his comrades in arms at the Battle of
Ballingarry were sentenced to be hanged, drawn,
and quartered. Later
on, it was decided
that O'Brien and Co.,
Limited, should be sent
to Tasmania instead,
but tiie fool of an Act
said that the convicts
must'be hanged, drawn,
and quartered unless
they gave their consent
not to be so mauled
and strangled. And
the prisoners refused
to allow the sentence
to be altered, and a
short Act had to he
passed through Parlia-
ment to compel them
to permit the Govern
ment not to hang,
draw, or quarter them.
Then O’Brien and his
friends were sent out
to V.D.L. for life.
Dodery went out free
and took a pub in
Longford, which pub
is now the headquar-
ters of the coursing
men in those parts.
Then when O’Brien
and his friends were released from durance in
V.D.L., and a deputation of colonists waited on
Smith O’Brien at John Pascoe Fawkner’s old
Cornwall Hotel to say good-bye, a man named
Dodery was among the crowd.
About Panton, P.M. :
The retirement of J. A. Panton, Melbourne’s P.M.,
should let loose smne reminiscences. On early Bendigo
he dispensed the best justice he had about him in a can-
vas tent, 12 xB. He did it seated on a packing case.
From ’5B to ’62 he travelled in Europe, and sketched and
painted. ’Sixty-three saw him P.M. in the district from
Heidelberg to Wood’s Point ; after which came a spell at
Geelong, and then Melbourne in the seventies. One of
his treasures is a little silver medal, awarded bv the
Edinburgh Naval and Military- Academy, in ’49, for sketch-
ing from Nature. In ’65 he livened up the pages of Mel-
bourne Punch with some sketches entitled “ The Adven-
tures of Captain Achilles von Humboldt Blowhard ; being
an account of a trip to Wood’s Point.” Perhaps Panton’s
nest work was done in helping to found the Victorian
Academy of Arts, since merged into the Victorian Artists’
Society. In this he was associated with Louis Bouvelot,
Frederick Woodhouse, Hubert de Castella, Chester
Earles, J. S. Mackennal (father of sculptor Bertram), and
others. His portfolio will be priceless some day, contain-
ing as it does authentic and vivid sketches of scenes of the
golden days at Forest Creek, Bendigo, Wood’s Point, etc.
“ Dux” : “ J.A.C.’s” mention (B. 9/5/’O7)
of that jolly good fellow, the late Captain Jackson
Barry, calls to mind the time when he was Mayor
of Cromwell (M.L.), in which town he carried on
a butchery business. Hori Grffy’s visit was a
red-letter day for Cromwell, and a reception,
speeches, and other dreary conventionalities were
arranged. But Barry upset matters. He com-
mandeered the Governor, took him familiarly by
the arm, and led him off to see “ the finest blanky
pigs in the whole adjective country.” A crowd
of angry councillors followed the pair around pig
sties and cattle pens, and heard Barry confide to
his Excellency that “these blanky idiots behind
want me to wear a coat of vermin, or something
or other, now that I’m Mayor.” The reception
was quite a failure. Last time I saw “the
Captain ” was in Wellington, when he pressed
me to buy his book, “Ups and Downs,” which
he earnestly guaranteed as “the finest blanky
book ever invented /” It was invented.

A MUSEUM OF THE NEXT GENERATION.
HORRIFIED MATRON : “ Come away, James! Don't attempt to look at the wicked things.”
W.A. Aborigines Protector Prinsep—next
to Railway Commissioner George the most
abused man in Westralia’s 975,000 square miles—
goes to England on twelve months’ leave, and
will then retire. Prinsep owes his comparative
affluence to Bigjohn. He was made Under-
secretary of Mines by Forrest without ever
having been down a shaft, and, when it became
necessary to modernise the department, Forrest
created him Protector of Aborigines, although
it isn’t certain that he had ever seen a nigger ex-
cept in pictures. Prinsep is president of the
Perth Art Society, and one of the few Westralians
whom old Governor Bedford has thawed to. A
cousin of his was Val Prinsep, the Academician,
who passed over recently.
Another belated story of ‘‘The Victorian
Four ”■—the comic Tory four who made such
an exhibition of themselves at the Senate election
of ’O3. Derham, of the Vic. Employers’ Federa-
tion, who was the still, strong man of the group,
secured a front room in the Victoria Coffee
Palace, Collins-street, for election night, so that
he might watch the returns as they were posted
outside the Argus office and consider their effect
on the crowd. At about nine, Derham turned up
at the Coffee Palace and was met by Mick the
night porter, a Socialist of the most bitter and
persecuting type. Mick didn’t know Derham,
nor did Derham know Mick but Mick greeted
the big man as politely as his election excitement
would permit. In reply to a question, he said
that the results were prime. “ And how are the
Victorian Four getting on for the Senate ? ” in-
quired Derham, blandly. “Getting on!” cried
Mick, ecstatically. “They’re getting done—
that’s how they’re getting on. Good enough for
the cows, too —trying to drag down the working-
man. They’re done like a dinner, Derham and
his push.” Derham was too. knocked over to
resent Mick’s honest criticism, and sadly dragged
his way upstairs to read the writing on the Argus
wall.
Cissie Zathar, the plaintiff in the Syrian
breach-of-promise case, which struggled along all
last week in Sydney, is a self-possessed, attractive
young woman, who looks neither the 24 years of
age she admits nor the 40 years of age alleged by
the other side. Cissie would probably be put
down as 30 by the majority of people. Like the
heroine of the “Song of Solomon,” she is dark
but comely, of pronounced Semitic type, and
altogether a pleasing sort of Rose of Sharon.
Cissie cannot read or write, even in her own
language, but she is a highly-intelligent woman
all the same, and probably the best witness that
the No. 2 Jury Court has seen for many a day.
Unlike most witnesses, she puts all her conversa-
tions in the first person, has never to be called to
order for transgressing the rules of evidence, and
in cross-examination answers so quickly and cer-
tainly qs to give the impression of truthfulness
and exactitude. When Cissie was asked why she
didn’t alarm the household when, as she alleged,
her fiance attempted to anticipate the marriage
ceremony, she caused a laugh in court by in-
quiring naively, "Why should I scream, when
he was going to marry me ? ”
Things are going badly with Dill Macky
these days. For years he has been a lecturer in
the N.S. W. Scotchbyterian Theological Hall, the
factory where they make the meenisters; but
lately there was trouble among his students.
The Assembly of the sect appointed a committee
to inquire into the students’ grievances, and that
committee (which was largely Dill’s own com-
mittee) unanimously recommended that Dill
should be “ relieved ” of the teaching of the
seniors and put; down to the ABC class. There-
upon Dill was insulted and resigned his lecture-
ship ; and now he leaves the Scarlet Woman and
the Cardinal alone for a minute to pitch into the
other lecturers, and charge them with being the
Scarlet Woman in disguise, or something like
that. “ There is now no one standing between
the students and the flood of rationalism which
is sweeping into our Church,” he shrieks. The
other lecturers reply that brother Dill is seeing
bogeys again, and that there wasn’t anything
about orthodoxy in the row at all; all that was
the matter was that the students said Dill didn’t
teach effectively, being too busy running after
the Scarlet Woman with a clod in his hand, and
they wouldn’t put up with him any longer.
“ Some little time ago,” says Mr. J. G.
Boyle, Kelmscott, W.A., “I was suffering from
a severe cold, which threatened to take a serious
form. Hearing so much about Chamberlain’s
Cough Remedy, I decided to give it a trial, and
to my great satisfaction was completely cured by
the time I had finished the first bottle.”*
About a stout gentleman who has left
many friends behind him :
The death of the Vic. Commandant, Colonel Ricardo,
in the hunting field, was due to his being what the
doctors call a man of full habit. His smart, commanding
deportment couldn’t conceal the fact that his weight-
some 16st. and more—was disproportionate to his height,
and because he carried too much flesh he was advised-
ill-advised—to go in for a severe course of horse-riding to
reduce his figure. He had formerly been a first-class
horseman, hut, owing to his bulk, the effort of taking his
mount over a fence caused him to break a small blood-
vessel in the brain. This accounted for his subsequent
fall from his horse, head foremost on to a stump, and
again it was his weight that told. A lighter man might
have survived the unlucky fall, but the massive colonel
fractured his skull so badly that he never recovered con-
sciousness. There are many stout middle-aged gentle-
men who play the giddy jockey without bringing them-
selves to grief, nevertheless they are distinctly out of
place in the hunting field. They should get themselves
into good condition by walking exercise before resuming
practice in the saddle.
GOOSE, HAWK , OR GAZEEKA?
Baron Rothschild declared that the attack being made
by President Roosevelt on the great railway corporations
of the United States, the income-tax and other problems
that are agitating Prance, and the Socialistic movement
in England, are all “killing the goose that lays the
golden eggs.” —Cable News.
THE PLUTOCRAT: “Stop it, now, and don’t be
foolish! Gant y’ see I’m the goose that lays
the golding egg? ”
THE DEMOCRAT : “ It’s no use talkin’, sonny.
Y’ can’t get a goose-skin t’ fit a hawk pro-
perly. Besides, geese ain’t so expensive t’ keep
as you are, and they ain’t in the habit of
eatin’ their own eggs; but, anyhow, I just
want t’ point out that you didn’t lay the
blessed earth! ”
A most peculiar jury (called to mind while
the personnel of Paddy Crick’s 12 good men and
true was under discussion one day outside Sydney
Criminal Court) was that which led to the con-
viction, over 30 years ago, in Parramatta, of
Sculptor Lorando Jones for blasphemy. The
jurymen that found Jones guilty (they did the
deed without leaving the box) were all B’s—Bell,
Benson, Bettington, Booth, Braddick, two Ber-
gins, two Byrnes, and three Browns. They all
swore on the Bible, and Buchanan appeared for
the defence.

RAINCOATS.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 11, 1907.
My Dear Akini,—
The trouble in Portugal over the stout King
who refuses to pay his debts may yet open
the glad vista of a stiddy job for a royalty who is
known personally to many Australians. Prince
Miguel, the Pretender to the throne of the little
roadless kingdom, was out here three years ago, and
put up at the Union Club as the Duke of Braganza.
A son—whose incognito, or alias , or alibi, or what
not, was Count de Neiva —accompanied him, and
wrought much havoc in pigeon-slaying, poker-
playing and other elevated circles. Society has
never found out exactly why these two elevated
folk came to Australia, but they were said to have
some dark and bulky purpose in view. One
proposition is that they put up at the Union Club
to be near Nicholas Willis’s office. Portugal is
their improvement lease if they can get it.
A passing incident in the Syrian alleged breach-
of-promise case, wherein comely Cissie Zathar
demands £lOOO from Moses Hanna, is the state-
ment of old Father Hanna as to why the lady did
not marry Joseph Abood. According to Dad
Hanna’s story, Joseph Abood wanted a wife, and
thought Cissie Zathar might do. So when she
came down to Sydney, Joseph Abood saw
her. “He gazed upon her, and then went from
the shop. ” Whether Cissie denies the story or
not, and whether it is true or not, it remains on
record as a description of the Syrian way of in-
specting the goods, as related in simple phrase by
•old Joseph. Apparently in Syria an appoint-
ment is made with he goods; the prospective
buyer calls and looks in the countenance of the
property; and if he is nit satisfied he just goes
away and leaves the job lot standing there. He
doesn’t even shake the hand of the article.
Since elbow sleeves became the rule for morn-
ing, noon, and night, long gloves have been a very
expensive necessity. Now the reaction is about
to set in. It has begun already in Paris, where
the sleeves of the mighty and the flighty are once
more to the wrist, and gloves are conspicuous by
their absence. To be guilty of wearing gloves is
to provoke a smile of horror. “ What ! are your
hands, then, so dirty that you must cover them ? ”
All the same, the no-gloves fashion is hardly
worth considering. The glove goes away for a
short change, and comes back with renewed vigor,
like Aunt Mary’s rheumatism, or the lost cat.
New spring models in millinery are already
lurking in certain milliners’showrooms in Sydney.
Some of them are expected to be worn at Miss
Kelso King’s wedding, this month. That will
be an evening function, and very hatty and
dressy. New hats will be off the face. The
pent-house lurch is “out.” “ Matinee Coronets”
are waiting to be worn. They are circles of straw,
with drooping tips or plumes, or rings of flowers.
Anyhow, they have not a sign of crown. The
“Peter Fan” model is a fez-like, close-fitting in-
cident, with tiny flowers on its brimless circu-
larity. Big Dolly Yarden kinds of head-gear will
have their day, so the authorities assert. In
etcetera novelties the “kimono belt” is in the
van. One I saw was of wide, hand-painted
ribbon. The whole decoration is in one—one
problem. (Can't think of a more descriptive
word.) There are the belt, and braces, and sleeves.
Worn over a single net-blouse in white or black
the effect ought to be ripping. The “sleeve”
of the kimono belt is only alleged. It is really
an armhole of the silk, although the wearing of
an “ armhole ” sounds like a confusion of terms.
Also, it vaguely recalls the Homan father who
bequeathed to his Bacchanalian eldest son the
keyhole, and nothing else. He said that he had
often seen the young man searching for it in the
small hours, with an intensity that suggested that
he wanted it more than anything else on earth.
Hugh Ward talks seriously of settling in
Sydney—“the most charming city on earth.”
Mrs. Hugh will probably take up the work of a
musical conservatorium here. She sings occa-
sionally in Sydney now, but the occasions never
seem to be advertised ; at any rate, I never hear
of them until there is nothing left to he said,
except that I wish I had been there. No matter
how earnestly I snatch at the greased forelock of
time in this matter, it always slips.
At the Fresh Air League ball the gowning of
two young Hordern Missuses took the vote : Mrs.
A. G. and Mrs. A. S. Hordern. They are
cousins, or some such, by marriage, to the only
Sam. They have been here a few months, and
the frocks they’ve sprung on Sydney are abso-
lutely hurrah. In fact, they are hip-hip-hurrah.
Sky-blue cripe and lace don’t sound much, hut
the artistry of the fashioning made the frock look
everything that was desirable in human covering.
The wearer of the gown had vine leaves in her
hair. This must not be read in the Ibsenish sense
—the leaves were artificial, and worn as orna-
ments. Ivory satin, and hand-painted chiffon
blue satin, and white lace were employed in the
make-up of Mrs. A. S. Hordern’s triumph. A
large bunch of debutantes was presented to the
Governor and Miss Rawson. Mrs. Harry Vale,
back from her honeymoon, also came in for the
vice-regal bow and complimentary word. The
iball was, as usual, a mighty financial success.
Under the significant title “ Honeyed Husks,”
the Saturday Review dropped hot ashes on the
programme of entertainment for the Premiers of
Greater Britain. It said : “We are playing right
into the hands of those, whoever and wherever
•they may be, who want to see the assembly of
1907 end in smiles and cigar smoke.” Mr.
Deakin’s phrase (a protest against the secrecy of
the Conference), “little precis slipped out day
by day between some trembling crevice,” seems
to be generally acclaimed as a winged phrase.
It is not flattering to other nice young women
with the extraneous Premiers, this starring of
Miss Botha ; also Miss Botha’s charm of manner,
her face, her frocks, her photographs. Even
Henry Lucy (or William Amelia, whichever it is)
drivels about her in the S.M. Hebald. She’s the
only bud on the bush, according to all accounts.
Yet Miss Aileen Ward, of Maoriland, is reckoned
a beauty, and has another precious gift—a Voice.
The London Symphony orchestra gave a special
concert in honor of the Premiers. Sir Alexander
Mackenzie conducted his own overture “Bri-
tannia,” and Dr. Harriss, a Canadian composer,
his own “Coronation Mass, Ed. VII.” Com-
menting on the programme, a noted English
critic (Arthur Symons) asked : “Why should our
colonial guests have been offered first a specimen
of our bad music and then, at much greater
length, a specimen of theirs ? ‘ Britannia ’ is
noisy and meaningless . . . The Mass begins with
an overture which seems to be the overture to a
ballet. . . dull prosings . . . destitute of religious
feeling.” This is the place where one is glad
Dr. Harriss is not Australian. Canada can keep
his Coronation Mass.
Mr. Goodwyn, general manager for Australia
of the Ocean Accident Co., is engaged to Miss
Vera Boyle, second daughter of Mr. C. J. Royle,
of Wahroonga.
Dr. Knight, LL.D., a nephew of ex-Chief
Justice Prendergast, of Maoriland, and his wife
have been in Sydney, visiting relatives and attend-
ing grand opera. When uncle Prendergast was
Lieutenant-Governor, nephew Knight acted as
private secretary.
Carreno’s clear-skinned, olive-toned face shows
no line. Her hair is greying, but her eyes have
the brightness of youth. Hers is perhaps the
strongest, most resolute woman’s face Sydney has
seen ; yet it has no suggestion of masculinity.
With all its strength it is a sweet face. As she
has lived fifteen years in the United States, and
before that, as a youngster, in London, she speaks
English like an Australian. At her headquarters,
in Berlin, she has pupils from every part of the
world. Americans predominate amongst the
foreign pupils. One Australian —Miss Hooper—
is from Sydney. The Carreno speaks of her as
“ gifted and charming.” Only three lessons a
day are given by the Mistress. The pupils are
prepared for the maternal star by daughter
Teresita (married in Melbourne last week), who,
if not herself a star, is at least a bit of star dust
or an asteroid.
The interior of the Town Hall was the most
glittering place in Sydney on Friday night, when
Carreno played Beethoven’s Emperor Concerto,
with the Amateur Orchestral Society. The
Rawsons and staff. Miss Wilson, Miss Vaughan,
and the Mayoral Hughes, all in their most costly
clothes, filled the unstrategie but vice-regal out-
post nearest the orchestral thunder. Sydney
society packed the rest of the hall and galleries.
The Carreno’s playing, like her facial expression,
proves iron will and subtle sweetness. She
thunders in big tone or orchestral effects, and anon
the lovely music (especially when she’s playing
Chopin) conies welling up with fairy delicacy
(honey and dew and seed pearls are in it). Criti-
cal men assert that women-players may interpret
Chopin poetically, but that they cannot do him
or Beethoven or Brahms full justice in an original,
illuminative sense. Still, these same men-things
do allow to one woman-player, and that’s
Carreno, the masculine attributes of nervous
force and imagination.
Piano-players will note the development of the
triceps muscles in the Carreno arms. Long con-
flict with a piano in the heavy-weight division has
done it.
Lady Northcote and a Saumarez-Smith brace of
girls were at the Carreno Saturday recital. The
Carreno gown was green sequined net over green.
Nary an ornament did she wear, and she came
to her work like a gentlewoman going to pay the
gas bill in a hurry.
When Conried was about to produce “ Hansel
and Gretel ” in the Metropolitan Opera House, he
got Humperdinck, its composer, over from his
own Lagerland to conduct. Mr. Max Rowan
tells the yarn. He was there at the time. Hum-
perdinck, looking like a cabman or a third-rate
fiddler out of a job, arrived at the Opera House.
For twenty minutes or so, door-keepers and flun-
keys at various entrances “ fired ” Humperdinck.
His look, and his name, and his lack of English
made just the combination that a flunkey vjould
fire. The Sydney-side observer felt sure that he
had met the shabby person in foreign parts, and
that he was a Somebody in Something, and
suggested that his name, whatever it was, be
taken to Conried. That was done, and presently
Conried was entreating the composer to look upon
him as his special doormat.
Lady Northcote, two of the Saumarez Smith
girls, and Captain Christian-Martyr-on-the-grid-
iron Fyers, were at the matinee of “Hansel and
Gretel.” The fairy opera is produced only at
matinees to suit the children, who stay from it
with unanimity. Not half-a-dozen of the
knicltered or short-frocked persuasion were
present.
To-morrow (Wednesday) Alderman Allen
Taylor’s portrait will be presented to its original
by the State Governor. A little ceremony and
some afternoon tea will lift the proceedings out
of ordinary Wednesday dreariness. A dreary day
is Wednesday, half-way to nowhere, with the
shadow of washing day behind and the shadow
of Sunday in front.
Mr. Dalglish—whose mother was a de Lauret,
of Goulburn, and sister of Mrs. Ted Simpson—
last month wedded, in the Cold Land, a maiden
wearing the inspiring name of Caroline de Burgh
Purves. The event happened at the Brompton
Oratory. The bridegroom soldier is in the ultra-
smart Black Watch regiment. This family ap-
pears to have a habit of marrying into the R.C.
aristocracy of Britain; another brother, who
leads the life bucolic near his family’s
town of origin, espoused Miss Maxwell-Scott,
niece of a full-blown Duke—he of Norfolk,
to wit. Mrs. Dalgl ish -Bellasis (mother of
the Black Watch bridegroom) gave £30,000
to finish the dome of the Oratory. This is
not a current item, yet it can’t be called a stag-
nant one. Donations of 30,000 lovely sovereigns
keep green a long time, and this one won’t sink
into a sere and yellow circumstance till the
Oratory wants another dome.
Miss Clark, daughter of Justice Inglis Clark,
of Tasmania, is in Sydney, and is staying with
Mrs. Boultbee, of Mosman. She and another
nice girl pal from Hobart did Maoriland before
coming hereto bite our unrivalled atmosphere.
Many well-known Melbournians are in Sydney,
either for atmospheric reasons or on business’
or else because they want to meet somebody here’
or because they want to avoid somebody over
there, or for some other motive. Glancing up
from my bit of cereal at the luncheon table of a
consequential hotel, I saw Mr. Judkins (looking
like a Cupid of 40), Mr. “Deeds” Fitchett,
Minister Swinburne, Mr. George Allen, and a
distinguished Melbourne editor man (who bristles
if you put his name in print), all feeding the
simple life. Mrs. Fred McEvoy (a calamitous
mushroom hat, trimmed with the very latest
French pen-wipers in art shades, couldn’t detract
from her prettiness) is another visitor. The
“Dick” Rowes (she a very fair and handsome
Circumstance, who has pleased Sydney in a
white cloth frock with blue facings) are still here,
but intend going to a station near Gundagai—a
place with an unspellable name as long as two
rabbits.
Mr. G. H. Reid had just finished luncheon.
He had had a lemon-squash, a liqueur with his
coffee, later a whisky and soda, still later two
more liqueurs, and he was on the verge of order-
ing another when an admirer interposed with
advice about mixtures. “Why,” said Mr. Reid,
settling his bulk back in his chair, “why, you
don’t think the drinks will ever meet, do you ? ”
Rev. .T. Hargrave, of St. Silas’ Anglican
Church, Waterloo, is retiring from active work
on the grounds of ill-health. He did one remark-
able thing in his life; he married the very sur-
prising George Dean, master mariner of a harbor
ferry boat, to Miss Seymour. And many results
followed that wholly unfashionable event. George
Dean tried to poison his wife and failed. He was
tried, and sentenced to death, and then held on
further inquiry to be not guilty, and released ;
and then he was proved guilty, and got a long
sentence for saying he wasn’t; and one or more
of his relations went mad, and one solicitor was
struck off the rolls, and two solicitors were tried,
and other things happened. The mild old parson,
who set all this going, had no idea how loaded it
was.
A curious tale of the strenuous life that a woman
may lead is that which has just drifted in from
Adelaide. The lady was behind the bar, and
the story goes that she went to the office of a male
person, at midnight, to get some books. She
said he drugged her, and that she only woke long
afterwards to find herself deserted, locked in,
and robbed of her jewellery—-£l4 worth. On the
other hand, he said that he bought the
jewellery off her, and that she wanted it back,
and was so wild when she failed to recover her
gauds that she burned down the office. The jury
believed the lady’s statement, but either way it
reads like a case of leading the strenuous life. If
it had been proved that the lady burned down the
office in her wrath, the event would fairly rank
with the case of Samson, who pulled down the
Philistine theatre.
Mr. Glencross Swift recently left for foreign
parts “to stay with Charlie Heidsieck.” Charles
is one of the Trustees of the Rheims Cathedral.
Whether this is an honor to Charlie or to the
Cathedral the man in the Australian street won’t
know. (To me it sounds very like being church-
warden to the Mosque of Omar.) The Jackdaw
of Rheims is better known to the bloque on the
highway than the cathedral of the same name.
But the Swift party—he was telling, dejectedly, a
Sydney woman of what was ahead of him. A
gorgeously liveried footman will bring him a pint
bottle of the champagne at 7’30 a.m. At lunch a
quart of the same bubble will lie at his elbow.
And after the meal the host will say : “Ve vill
now go to ze zellar to taste ze vines to suit Aus-
tralee.” “ Really,” said Swift’s listener, “I must
go home and sleep off this conversation. It has
gone to my head.”
Prof. MacCallum was the examiner in this
story, and the examinee is now a Sydney barrister.
The Prof, asked the student for his reading of the
lines from Macbeth, “This my hand will rather the
multitudinous seas incarnadine, making the green
one red.” And the student, who hadn’t even
read the drama through, gravely answered, “ I
take it to be a prophetic utterance referring to
the Battle of the Boyne.”
I was idly turning over Whitaker’s Peerage
in search of something or other when I discovered
that there is in the United Kingdom a Baron
Newbottle. It is an old title —there have been-
Barons Newbottle for 316 years. Also there is a
Baron Kerr, of Newbottle. Now I understand
the feelings of that Scriptural person who called
himself a “servant” and wanted to “depart in
peace.” I could also depart more peacefully now
that I have found the lordly family of Newbottle.
Life is not all a blank.
A man writes this :
A new style of headgear for wimmen has arrived. To
the eye of a mere man, it looks very much like
an ordinary sou’-wester, although the material is
different, and it is usually of another color. It
has no front, but extends horizontally to the rear
for a considerable distance. The particular point
about it, however—a point which will make the
hearts of certain tradespeople to leap, as leaps the wild
rabbit upon the precipice—is that a large number of
little curls (price one guinea each, at the very best shops)
are stuck in to fill up the vacant space at the back, and
bulge there. This arrangement has a peculiar effect, as
though Miss had put on a hat too large for her, and had
been forced to fill up the space at the back with padding.
It is a noticeable fact that women with black hair prefer
to wear another kind of sun-guard, and chance being out
of fashion. It is the little yellow devil with the easily-
matched and curly locks that rushes the milliner with
her order for the up-to-date rig.
I wish my friends would stop sending me that
venerable wheeze about “ the widow’s mite.”
Since the Bible began to be read, and men began
marrying widows, every small man who espoused
a large widow has been called by his friends and
enemies “ the widow’s mite.” Then there is the
other joke about “ the widow’s might ” displayed
in capturing him ; and when a lady, reputed to
have a tombstone in the family, appeared in pan-
tomime, a genius came in personally with a harrow-
ing jest about “ the widow’s tights.”
Mrs. Harry Chisholm, of Goulburn, is in town
for the (alleged) winter, and has taken Mrs.
Arthur MacArthur’s house, in Alberto-terrace. A
well-known mansion that has recently changed
ownership is “Pomeroy,” at Potts Point. An
old identity—Mrs. Rundle—occupied it for many
years. She died recently. Mr. Julius Caro has
bought the place.
Messrs. Hadfield and Daplyn, the hon. treas.
and hon. sec. respectively of the fund for pur-
chasing Mr. Lister-Lister’s picture, “ The Golden
Splendor of the Bush,” rise simultaneously to
inform subscribers that the trustees of the
Gallery have accepted the picture with great
enthusiasm. It will be formally handed over to
them next Friday afternoon.
From my Brisbane correspondent:
The festivities of the North proved too much for the
Chelmsford dame. She unable to participate in the
joys of tb£ Charters Towers reception, and after that she
threw up the sponge, and it never came down again.
She returns to Brisbane, accompanied by the Du Cane,
for rest and repairs, before the coming of the Northcotes.
Her frail-looking husband, much bucked up by his
junketing, will continue the tour alone, and captivate
the hearts of tropical girls with his youthful smile.
The town this week has been full of three kinds of par-
sons, and the air full of synod. There was the parson of
the Bush Brother variety, who dresses in tweeds, smokes
a cherry-wood pipe, and has an affection for the wilds.
There was the bleating;, town-bred Judkins, full of city
mission and gloomy detail of the “ fallen.” And, lastly,
there was a drove of newly-arrived, moon-faced, mos-
quito-bitten lambs, straight from the Oxford fold, fired
with a burning enthusiasm to save the degenerate colonial.
Lastly there was the Archbishop—one of him—who in-
dulged in quite an array of little jokes just before the
plate went round. He told how, in one town where he
flattered himself that his appearance had made a real
impression, he overheard the youngest old maid telling
her aunt that the bishop was “such a very plain man.”
Also, that the back-blockers laughed at his legs (which
was a shame, as he really is not Chippendale). He inti-
mated, however, that he did not object to the title of
“ bloke.”
On Saturday afternoon there was the usual reception at
gloomy Bishopsbourne, where the chief Reverend, ham-
pered by a lack of garden seats or dining-room chairs,
gave tea and fruit salad (both tepid) to about 600 good
friends of the church, many of whom grew quite weary
leaning against the wall. There were not even enough
curates to go round.
Last week Ken Mackenzie, Lieutenant in the Common-
wealth Army, heir-apparent of a local banker, became
the fortunate husband of Gladys Hill, a placid little lady,
who looks the part of one of the 20 love-sick maidens, but
who is in truth a practical little body, and calculated to
gladden any man’s premises.
Merchant John Arthur and his second hope, Violet of
the unruly locks, are on the road from England by the
Commonwealth. Violet, the self-willed, has given her
hand and good warm heart to a person of the name of
Campbell, and the knot is to be tied on the bride’s
arrival at Adelaide.
It is said that a bride who recently suffered from
bruises, served out with a lavish strap by her husband,
has seen her lawyer, but what he said to her is not
known. Anyhow, she has gone to your sinful but lovely
city to train as a nurse.
The death of Victorian Military Commandant Ricardo
recalls the almost forgotten episode of how the eldest
daughter of Mr. and Mrs. William Blakeney met her
death at the Ricardo station at Fassifern. Mounted by
her host upon an old racer, she lost nerve when he bolted.
After screaming madly for assistance she fainted, and fell
out of the saddle, striking her head upon a stump. Then
the scared animal drag-ged her along till life was extinct.
It was almost exactly the kind of death that Ricardo
met himself.
Yours Affectionately,
ARENEL
A good idea comes from a Maoriland
correspondent of Sydney Herald—that Maori-
land should combine with Australia to build a
little navy. The destinies of M.L. and Australia
are in the same boat,'and they will probably have
to face danger together one day.
At the inter-State Conference a resolution
was carried that the Federal legislation against dis-
honest secret commissions should be extended to
the States. The resolution was put “ without dis-
cussion.” But it will be a long time before some
of the States follow the honest Federal lead in
this matter.
Mothers need have no hesitancy in giving
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy to the little ones.
It is perfectly safe.*
The recent elections showed up once more
the superfluity of the Vic. House of Rich Land-
lords. One member was wanted for each of the
17 provinces. Ten of the 17 seats were uncon-
tested. Of the other seven, four were metropoli-
tan districts, and in these only 31, 36, 30, and 25
per cent, of the electors voted. In the country
the interest was deader still. At dozens of poll-
ing places but two or three votes were recorded,
while one booth was kept open the statutory nine
hours, with all the necessary officials in attend-
ance, that one solitary voter might mark his
paper. And even then his vote w T as informal.
The only argument needed to convince you
of the value of Angier’s Emulsion for coughs and
lung affections is a trial. It is used by doctors
the world over. Send 4d. for postage to the
Angier Chemical Co., Ltd., 7 Barrack-street,
Sydney, for a free sample bottle, and give it a
trial.*
, Daniel White, who, 30 years ago, helped
to found the Victorian Chamber of Manufactures,
and who has been treasurer of the institute
for 21 years, was entertained recently at a
smoke concert. Nat Levi (who was present) and
Mr. White are the fathers of the Chamber. But
Nat Levi has been more in the public eye. He it
was who beat the great R. D. Ireland, Q.C., when
the latter, having accepted the Attorney-General-
shipin the Heales Ministry, went up for re-election
for Maryborough. Up to that time there had
been none of the seed of Abraham in the Vic-
torian Parliament.
Sydney breathes more freely now that
Professor Starr Jordan has left it in peace. This
Yankee oratorical high-flier, whose real name is
surely Star Jawer, inflicted innumerable lectures
on various public bodies, none of which lectures
taught anything; he told countless “funny
stories,” which were either moss-grown or stupid ;
and for “eloquence” he wasn’t even in Dan
O’Connor’s class. Why are these wearisome
speakers imported from abroad? If there is a
demand for jaw with frills on it, Neild is still
with us, and threatening to write another book of
Jawbone poems.
The timely use of Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy will prevent pneumonia.*
Melb. Argus puts it gently, like this :
“British manufacturers have been driven, in a
manner, by the strenuous competition of foreign
countries, to cheapen the cost of production by
the use of adulterants.” But the Argus. fiscal
policy teaches that the strenuous competition of
foreign countries is a magnificent thing, and that
it brings all manner of blessings in its train, and
that anyone who objects to it is a Protectionist
and a fool. We were never told before that Free-
trade and strenuous foreign competition led to
fraud, lying, and poisoning—at least, the Argus
never mentioned it before.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, June 10, 1907.
Mt Dear Yarrie.—
What’s the matter with our Benedicts and
Beatrices ? They honeymoon in boarding-houses,
set up their domestic gods on Mrs. Todgers’ altar,
bring up their offspring under her eye. Later,
they quarrel, and live in separate hash-turrets, and
divorce each other, and marry again on hash-
tower principles, and in the end lose all idea of
living in houses of their own. Do the hash-houses
reap a harvest of coin and scandal ’cos Ben thinks
Bee incapable of filling the most becoming duties
in the world, and because his appetite sadly mis-
trusts her ? Or is it that the blessed girl jibs at
the prospect of a little responsibility and care ?
Whatever the reason, it’s a foolish procedure.
Nothing incubates wifely scepticism like a circle
of interested and unoccupied cats; and nothing
vitiates husbandly tastes more than the social diet
offered him by a houseful of “paying guests” (or
owing guests). Romance is throttled by a newly-
married fear of ridicule. Effort is discouraged by
the lack of domestic incentive. And, more
serious still, figures become submerged in fat, the
fat of laziness. Oh, dear young friends, do be
courageous ! Start housekeeping on a deficiency
of floor-coverings and cash, rather than risk your
souls in the lazy snobocracy of a hash-foundry.
“ Parsifal ”is advertising well in Adelaide. A
saved soul (no connection at all with the manage-
ment), signing himself “A Young Man Grate-
ful,” acknowledges his redemption in S.A.
Register. He alleges that previous to seeing
“Parsifal” he had made an appointment of a
wicked nature, with sinister intent. On witness-
ing the spectacular sermon, he saw the error of
his ways; and no longer considered himself justi-
fied in keeping the said engagement. He didn’t
say whether lie intended warning the girl that
she needn’t trouble to come out that evening, or
whether he meant to leave her catching cold
indefinitely to no purpose at a street corner, while
he watched Missus Brune point a moral with a
plump leg clad in fleshings.
The two loving youngsters who eloped from
Brighton have at last sent cheerful and casual
word of their whereabouts to two sets of indignant
and anxious parents. They are both just in their
teens. For a week or so before the elopement
girl chums had been remarking that “she is
wearing a wedding ring,” but they set it down as
a sentimental joke on her part. As a matter of
fact, the little secretly-wed one was just waiting
her boy-lover’s word to “ fly,” and when he told
her to fly she flew. He also flew. They are
coming back to be kissed and forgiven—or
spanked. Why not spanked ?
A listener repeats an ominous conversation
which was voiced while the late Colonel Ricardo
was selecting a mount to carry him to the hunt
where he met his death. Talking with his wife
and Colonel Johnson, he remarked, in answer to
some sally : “Well, and if I should be killed on
Monday, I’ll go out in an easy, quick fashion that
won’t give anyone trouble, nor hurt me much.
What more can a man want ?” The poor fellow’s
end occasions honest regret on the part of all who
knew him, and great is the sympathy for his
widow. She was the brightest and most likeable
of military wives.
The late Colonel leaves two small daughters
(besides grown-up children of his former marriage).
The present Mrs. Ricardo was a Brisbane girl,
Miss Ina Thomson, daughter of Dr. Thomson,
of the Northern capital. The hearty old sur-
geon, her father, was for many years chief
medical authority in Bananaland.
The unwholesomeness of the habit of attending
the other fellow’s funeral (it is also unwholesome to
attend your own, but, alas, unavoidable) wasproved
again by the sudden death of Colonel Kelly last
week. He attended Colonel Ricardo’s funeral on
Thursday, apparently in the best of health, came
back, took to his bed, and died. To go to a funeral
is to undertake, as a rule, a long, wearisome, nerve-
racking journey, under the most depressing of
conditions. Sometimes the weather is too cold ;
sometimes it is too hot, and when the day is
really fine, going to a funeral is the worst sort of
use you can put it to. A safe rule of life is to go
to no funeral while you are alive, and to only one
when you are dead.
The fear of what the world will say almost
amounts to a disease, with some people. A pair
of Melbourne parents—a very Proper couple—
have a son who recently married a barmaid living
in another State. The damsel’s father owned the
pub in which she toiled, but he didn’t approve of
paying for outside help when he had it in the
family, so the girl was naturally a beer-puller
with the best of them—a barmaid ! When this
terrible news reached the young man’s family,
they were much put about, not being aware that
the young lady’s papa was the landlord of the pub.
However, it was decided to put the best face on
matters, and, it being the young man’s intention
to live in his wife’s State, it was decided to
spread abroad the intelligence that their hopeful
had married a clergyman’s daughter ! This
announcement was received in certain quarters
with rather more astonishment (not to speak of
amusement) than the family expected, notably
among the young men who knew John. What
they couldn’t understand was how any clergyman
tolerated that wild and whooping young man long
enough for John and the daughter to become
really acquainted.
Nurses at the big hospitals are making moan
about a new rule added to the already long and
stringent “ theatrical ” list (such a grim tragedy-
theatre is theirs). A nun-like cowl is added to
their dress—worn to cover every bit of hair, even
to the eyebrows—and almost covering the mouth.
The warmth of the theatre and the scent of
anaesthetics are sufficiently trying, and often
cause faintness. With this new harness these
things are appalling. One point of objection with
the nurses is that the doctors are not forced to do
likewise. Many doctors will not sacrifice their
pampered moustaches to the barber’s razor—and
“ their moustaches are worse than our eyebrows ”
cry the indignant Nightingales. Um—perhaps !
It would be a good topic for discussion whether
men’s moustaches or women’s eyebrows have done
more mischief since Man began to be ashamed of
the stump vestige of his tail.
The Musical Society rather spread itself to
welcome Madame Albani on Saturday night.
There was no padding to the party—just musicians,
Canadians and press. The prima donna, hale
and hearty, swept to her place of honor a vision
of emerald green velvet, ermine and lace, with a
diamond cross flashing on that white throat of
hers. Mr. Albani was with her—good Mr. Gye
who, on his own, doesn’t give you a bit the idea
of. just being somebody’s husband. Mildred
Jones, of the contralto voice, towers over her
party. The head she rears is crowned with such
glorious chestnut hair that one can forgive it for
rearing. Myrtle Meggy, the pretty and dimpled
Sydney girl,” takes Melbourne comfortably, and
helps, with thd three nice young men of the com-
pany, to give you the idea that it is a bright
combination.
Marie Hall, of the demure, hipless contour,
clothes her quaint flatness in garments of unob-
trusive but real artistic merit. At her first re-
cital, she fiddled in a greeny gown, with belt and
skirt-border of darker velvet. There were gar-
lands of pale spring-flowers, built of embossed
chiffon, clinging to the sheeny folds, and alto-
gether the costume, though too “ old ” and staid
for its small, serious wearer, was worth study.
Marie’s smooth, lovely “tone” suggests a singing
brook flowing across the strings of her Strad.
Those who sigh for typhoons and tornadoes of
harmonic emotion find the recitals just a little
missish; not tame exactly, but missish.
This is “Tryphena” from London: —
It’s a thieving nation. Every tube-lift and ’bus has the
injunction, “ Beware of Pickpockets—Male and Female.”
The effect of this proclamation can be imagined. Every-
one looks upon you as a pilfering cat, and you regard
every wretch in return as a prospective robber. Hence
the deadly cold air of suspicion that dwells in the solemn
face of the Londoner. For it is a smileless phiz. I perch
up at the side of a bell-toppered ’bus-driver and quiz the
funereal procession of Strand and Fleet-street pedestrians.
The Jehu jokes. He’s the one unprofessional humorist in
Fogtown. Every other jester gets a mighty fine salary
for rouging his nose and being funny for a few minutes
at the music-halls. But the nation grins not—neither does
it roar. Even the tinted Venus, who prevails at the cafes
and in the theatre promenades and in Hyde Park, masks
her wickedness in a cold-storage stare. All expression is
painted, or enamelled, out of her face. She seems to be
trying to look like a flattered portrait of herself, and to
deceive the bulk of the multitude into believing she’s a
languid aristocrat. The bulk doesn’t believe any such
thing. Suspicion again. The real Vere de Vere has an in-
variable companion—a fat poodle in fancy rig. If it doesn’t
happen to be a poodle, it’s an emaciated Johnnie looking
like a devilled bone. Really the pup is a more cheerful
sight. I saw a ducal dame, at the Army and Navy Stores,
trying a hyacinth blue ribbon on her pug’s neck. It
didn’t please her. She selected a pastel tone, and the
pet snarled, at which sign of intelligence she kissed his
dirty little ear. On another afternoon, I saw a motoriste
pull up at a theatre door and unearth a toy terrier from
her furs. He shivered in the cutting air, and started a
sneeze. These aristocratic yelpers are very artful.
Madame searched in the terrier’s velvet coat (it fitted like
a glove) and took out a lace hanky, the size of a tram-
ticket, and with this she dried the terrier’s wet nose.
I walked on, half-dazed, till a male philanderer pinched
my elbow. I gripped my bag of small cash possessions,
and fled. There’s always a male hanging loose about
ready to ogle a girl. This one had probably been
ogling while I was thinking the deep dog-thought’s
quoted above, and had pinched my funny-bone to draw
my attention to his ogle.
The name that stands tallest in Anglo-Australian stage
records is that of Alice Crawford. Last night, at a ban-
quet, I heard sub rosa that Sir Charles Wyndham and
Richard Harding Davis were suppering the Bendigo
dazzler after her “ Twelth Night” show, and then taking
her on to a large Ducal reception. And Alice has reached
a period in her grease career when she talks of her Art
and her genius to big theatre guns at the dinner-table.
“ I not only had good notices in Berlin. I was the only
principal to get any at all in most of the papers,” she told
a supper muster of dizzily important people. Alice is
arrogant these days, and actually allowed to be so by
virtue of her beauty and illuminating genius. She re-
fuses to wear dresses prescribed by Manager-Actor Tree,
and orders others. He doesn’t demur even. “ Alice will
have her way,” he explains petulantly. And she does.
It’s marvellous to find the Bendigo recitress of several
summers ago having her way with one of the most
dominating theatre-chiefs in this hub.
I had my first view of Alice, after a gulf of years, at a
gay little supper dance at the Grafton galleries. One
goes in bunches of fojir and six there, after the theatre,
and two-steps and waltzes (Boston fashion) into the small
hours of the Sabbath. It’s the smart rendezvous of the
theatrical and the high-class Bohemienne. One charming
Sydney grass-widow bore me along in her party. I found
her in her emerald spangled chiffons the most chic and
juvenile two-stepper in the whirling throng. Mostly
Madame Bohemia is plain, painted, passAe, but dressed
divinety, and you feel the Johnnie who whirls her is
merely dancing with Costly Clothes. The woman is
nothing. It’s the Dress.
This night, Alice Crawford wore a cerulean-blue crepe-
de-chi ne Empire rig—more of a drapery than a dress.
She lounged among a group of buzzing males—sullen,
handsome, distrait. The wife of a theatrical manager
pointed her out. I gaped. The whole character of the
face has changed. It is heavy, very handsome, but
sullen. The laughing, boisterous, cheeky Bendigonienne
has become a top artiste of the London boards at the ex-
pense, evidently, of her exuberant nature. Hard work,
competition, and final victory have resulted in a per-
sonality strong, magnetic, but in no way sweet. Alice
has, for a certainty, tasted the bitterness of a London
struggle.
Talking of galleries. I’ve looked on the famous resur-
rected Raphael canvas at the Dore Galleries. The asser-
tion is that the masterpiece is a genuine Raphael. It is
a masterpiece, and bears a distinct and unmistakable
likeness to all the other Raphael productions. “ The
Holy Family ” is the subject, but the group of figures is
not so wonderful as the coloring, which is rich and inspir-
ing. On no canvas but a Raphael will you find the wonder-
ful blues of the Virgin Mary’s robe, or the deep, deep,
bruised strawberry tone of the neighboring Saint’s
draperies. It’s a banquet of color. You can’t leave it.
You can’t conceive the effect of deep color till you see
this Raphael gem.
I want to see some more resurrected Raphaels. One’s
mind wants some art glory of the kind now and then in
this Big Smoke, to clear the mental dust-bin.
Lawyer Whiting’s youthful-looking spouse and
their two pretty daughters jaunted Sydney ward,
last week, on their way to Japan. This is the
girls’ first foreign jaunt; but they probably know
their own Australia better than any other Mel-
bourne girls. Their mother has made an ener-
getic programme of inland tours for the much-
indulged darlings, instead of the usual rush to
England. They are smart and pretty girls. One
does really clever art in black and white. The
other has a curve verse-ward.
The furs are very head-and-taily this season.
Instead of one’s fox fur terminating in one modest
little head, the correct thing is to dot them, fore
and aft and all over, with decapitations like a
Bluebeard’s room. And the many tails make the
girls look like Rabbit-O.
Gaston Mervale (whose real name off-stage is
Mistowski) belongs to c a very clever family. His
father is a Professor of Science and Languages
somewhere in Fog City, and a brother, Alf
Mistowski, makes a meteor-like appearance at
Australian Universities once a year as musical
examiner for English Trinity College. For this
work Alfred draws the nice little salary of £25 a
w’eek, plus expenses. He is also a composer of
some note, and that helps to eke out his humble
crust and keep the wolf off the verandah.
Warrnambool had a stirring wedding party last
week, and quite a many Melbournites trekked
down to the town that has missed the ’bus, and
helped in the marriage of Majah Vicars-Foote s
jolly little daughter Florrie to Mr. John
McKenzie, of “Calrossie,” down Yarram way.
All the round-about station folk came long dis-
tances for the shivoo, the Ardleigh, Anderson,
and Summers clans in the lead. The setting of
the ceremony was violet—millions of violets made
the decorations/

One mayoral ball is usually very like another,
but Hawthorn broke out of the rut. The hall was
fair to look upon, decked out in pastel shades ut
blue and pink ; and the stage was a triumph. An
imposing flight of shallow steps, crimson carpeted,
led up from the ballroom to the platform. From
the said platform you might wander at will into a
fairy dell, hung with the multi-colored lights of the
journalist’s dream. One of the many scintillating
mayoresses evidently felt soulful. She wandered
into that fairy dell, and almost instantly found her-
self set alight by a fairy lamp. Councillor Riddell
rushed to the rescue, and averted the panic that
would probably have arisen if the municipal lady
had rushed through the hall wrapped in flames
eight feet high. Just now, with all the mayoral
balls coming on, a conflagration of mayoresses
would be an awful calamity to suburban society.
The timely councillor damaged his hands pretty
considerably, and the fair dame he rescued is much
concerned thereat.
Enthusiasts were surprised into a trip to G'oade
Island, the bottle works, and thereabouts, last
Saturday. The Yarra doesn’t run to width in
any of its aristocratic reaches, so the Australian
University crews had to wrestle for victory down
Footscray way. The smell isn’t nearly as fortify-
ing as of yore. In fact, they’ve improved the
good old familiar aroma right out of the Yarra.
But the setting to the race was not edifying.
Even the glimpse of golf links in the distance
failed to create enthusiasm. Some big club or
another plays away down here, within sight of
nothing much except the Quarantine Station.
Somebody told me—he was a member, and ought
to know—that the links are most accessible; you
go to Footscray and take a cab from there !
Ralph Straus, who is a novelist, a bibliophile,
a zoologist, and an Englishman, is now in Mel-
bourne. He came over the water because he
heard that Australia is a wonderful country, and
remains for a while because he realises that it is.
He will spend what time he may accumulating
data and information concerning Australian poli-
tics and society, aspirations and achievements.
Apart from his aims, Ralph Straus is a boyish
personage with a predilection for loud socks and
unusual overcoats. He sounds ingenuous, looks
immature, and declares quite youthful admiration
for our girls. “They’re delicious to look upon,
delightful to talk with. They’re so debonair;
so unaffected—and what a large proportion of
’em are more than ordinarily intelligent ! And
Australia itself—why, you live on the Hub of the
Future!”

From “Rosna,” in Adelaide :
The Birthday Cup, postponed from Saturday to Mon-
day because the multitude declined to swim to the fray,
was a dark, deep, dreary disappointment, so far as the
frockocracy was concerned. The weather was uncet-
tain, and the gorgeous chiffony erections had to be re-
linquished in favor of the plain, hard-working coat and
skirt. Such millinery outbursts as transpired were
mostly dejected resurrections of last year’s poultry
feathers. Many of the usual race-gadders stayed firmly at
home by the fire, and only an occasional scion of the
landed gentry left the pursuit of the frolicsome sheep,
and swam the family motor to town with the chauffeur’s
eye just appearing above the slush. Some of the few
brilliant exceptions that dotted the blotted landscape
were Mrs. Bert Saunders, in a gorgeous cream cloth
garment, with pink roses and blue plumes in her smait
head-piece ; Miss Flossie Melrose, in old-rose cloth,
trickily faced with white, and a black picture hat ; Mrs.
Albert Gebhardt, in a glorious confection of royal blut,
bespattered with cream passementerie, and topped with a
green hat; Miss Shiela Deeley, smartly garbed in navy,
and some others too few to mention.
’Tis said that over 3000 people emulated the breathless
sardine, crowded in the Black Hole of its tin, and went
to Pirie-street Methodist Church the night Ada Ward
performed there. Adelaide looked upon the guileless
Ada as a species of religious comic opera, and, with
“Parsifal” raging at the Theatre Royal at the same
time, felt it was in great luck. But after that one great
upheaval the populace wavered between the rival shows.
Their “Parsifal” weighed down the scales against Ada’s
mortified bonnet and whirl of arms and denunciation, and
Adelaide’s shekels turned steadily tou'ardsthe Williamson
coffers. Ada left us in the middle of the week, and a few-
belated persons gathered at the station and sang a hymn
or two. On the whole, this city declines to take the
“saved one” seriously, but it thinks her a pretty good
show.
The Royal British Nurses’ Association sets its annual
ball rolling at the Town Hall on June 20th ; and the
whole medical profession, to say nothing of a horde of
mended and grateful patients, are expected to help the
affair along. Their Exes., and the Chief Judicial Ways,
and various Premiers, Mayors, and other small deer
patron the ball, and a strong committee is pondering
over the egg of some great scheme which will makeitthe
most resplendent spree of the century—if the egg doesn’t
addle.

Voice w'ill Breathe ” as usual.
Four nights and a matinee has Mary Ann Brune come
into her fortune at the Theatre Royal. Her species is
such an elusive rarity in South Aus. just now that all the
harassed marms of the place attended, in the hope of
seeing her expiate the sins of her class at the stake. And
instead they saw her come into a fortune, and leave off
even the little, incompetent Miryanning that she did,
and make one servant less in an already almost servant-
less land. All the sympathy of thed.c. was for Mary
Ann’s harassed missus ; if she had come in for a fortune
and her domestic help had been arrested for stealing a
plated spoon, the circle would have been pleased. This
State wants more domestic handmaiden much more than
it wants less rabbit, and it doesn’t care a straw about
Danysz.
The Adelaide Skating Club closed the Glaciarium door
in the public face on Friday last (7th), and invited the
very ultra and elect population to slither and fall. The
youthful and energetic members of society still cling to
the icy pastime, but they no longer slither in democratic
company. The skating club slides privately o’ Monday
nights, and now and then it invites those who are horri-
fied at the Labah pahty to an invitation affair. Two
energetic secretaries, Messrs. Willie Gunson and J. Gow,
manipulate the club and examine the credentials of pro-
posed guests, and those Who are suspected of being in-
sufficiently horrified at the Labah pahty are not asked.
Yours affectionately,
JOHNNA.

Another Ma State Grievance against the
Commonwealth ; it is the S.M. Herald that has
dug up this one :
There may be Ways of getting the debt safely out of the
reach of the capacious maw of the Federal authorities,
in spite of the power the Constitution gives it.
The dear old lady’s affection for her debt is very
pathetic. Evidently, she would rather have an
unpaid bill than her marriage lines any day.
Mr. J. Richmond, who knows Papua well,
and has wandered with a theodolite and an axe
over most of it, calls public attention to the hope-
lessness of effective development whilst the Com-
monwealth is so mean with its salaries. For a
surveyor-general there is now offered £450 a year,
without quarters. That won’t tempt a good man
to go out into the wilds.
The week’s arguments for Secession :
Since Federation the Australian drink bill has cost
£90,000,000 (J. Cook at Methodist Meeting, Sydney Town
Hall)-
and
The Birthday Honors list, when it does appear, will
probably be blank, as far as this State is concerned. It
is possible, however, that one or two gentlemen pro-

One of J. Carruthers’ mean little ways of
being nasty to the Australian Government is to
abolish all concessions to the military on N.S.W.
railways. In time of war, the Federation has
power to take possession of the railways ; and
this should imply some right of consultation in
regard to military use in time of peace. But the
buzzing Joseph doesn’t see that, and smiles a
Quilpish smile as he makes himself disagreeable
to N.S.W. soldiers because they are under Fede-
ral authority.
Joseph Carruthers, at the Conference of
State Premiers, became such a mean and hope-
lessly parish worm over the Northern Territory
matter that even the Sydney Telegraph, which is
a good deal of a parish institution itself, spoke
severely to him in its leading article of June 5.
And this quite surprised Carruthers. Apparently
he thought that it was his business to Go The
Whole Worm, and when his best supporter told
him plainly that the whole worm was too much
worm, he was an amazed public caterpillar.
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is a medicine
of great worth and merit. Try it when you have
a cold.*
Supposing the Australian Government
decided to amend the Constitution, and the

capital site, with even 100 square miles around the
G.P.O. as the Federal territory, would Car-
ruth ers, then be satisfied ?

Even the Age can’t rid itself of the
appalling notion that the Vic. Leg. Council must
be a House of all Fat; and when a Labor candi-
date appeared for Melbourne North, it published
a shrieky head-line, “A Seat in Jeopardy,” and

The big hungry legal action for the re-
covery of Church and School lands, started,
and promptly lost, by Primate Smith against
the N.S.W. Government, is the outcome
of a deeply and carefully laid scheme of
Dr. Broughton, the first Archbishop. Had
the plan worked out as he intended, the
Anglican Church hereabouts would have owned
more land than any other church in the world.
The late Speaker Abbott said he had, as a lawyer,
gone through the scheme, and had it not been
interfered with by various politicians it would
have made the Anglican body in this country the
wealthiest corporate body in Christendom.
Abbott considered Broughton a financial genius,
and grieved that his stupendous and incredible
snatch or steal had not come off.
The resumed Gobbagombolin estate is a
good example of how N.S.W. Legislative Council
helps to rob the people. When the Closer Settle-
ment question was under consideration, the
Assembly decided that land should be com-
pulsorily resumed at the owner's valuation
for land-tax, plus 10 per cent, for disturb-
ance. The Nominee Council knocked that
provision out. The owners of Gobbagom-
bolin valued their estate for land-tax at
£1 13s. 9d. per acre ; or at least that was
the rate at which they paid. The improve-
ments were valued at 12s. 6d. per acre,
making £2 6s. 3d. per acre. Yet when the
State resumed the place with the bound-
less name, the owners were paid £3 7s. 3d.
per acre. So either the State has been
done out of a lot of land-tax in the past,
or it is being done out of a vast amount
of purchase money now. Either way the
N.S.W. Upper House is responsible. And
not a man in the Council has been sent
there by the people’s vote ; in fact, a very
large proportion of them were sent there
because the people voted against them when
they stood for the Assembly.
The Victorian .Railway Department is
doing its best to show that we must open up our
own iron deposits. The department recently
imported a shipment of Krupp’s iron plates, and
fitted up its engine boilers with them. It has
now been discovered that the plates are defective,
and liable to blow out at any time. Anyhow, the
whole of the engines fitted from this particular
shipment have been withdrawn from service, and
are now lying at Newport unfit for use. They
will have to be practically reconstructed, and the
fine old imported material will promptly be
scrapped. A few possibilities suggest themselves.
Firstly, are there any other shipments of this
splendid foreign material wandering about on the
Australian railways ? Secondly, if there are, what
is the possibility of getting George Reid to drive
the loco, till he ascertains what is the precise
bursting-point of the plates ?

The crowd which is trying to block the
land-tax and the bre dung up of big N.S. W.
estates is pointing out that it is all wrong to say
that farmers are being driven into Queensland—
that “the return of estimated population in
Queensland shows that Queensland actually lost
male population during the Mu.ch quarter.”
Quite true. Queensland did lose population—
Kanaka population. The ingenious crowd forgot
to mention that it was in the March quarter that
Tommy Tanna was sent home.
Already quite a herd of legal men are
looking hungrily at the proposed billet of Hired
Anti-Federal Grumbler. Yet it isn’t so good a
job as it looks. The States are to pay this in-
dividual up to £3OO a year, and he
is to inspect Federal Bills and
find infringements upon State
Rights. In between times
he can go fishing. When
he finds an alleged in-
fringement, the Austra-
lian Parliament will
probably remark that if
there is anything in the
discovery the High Court
can deal with the matter after
the Act is passed, and it will tell the ab-
surd official to run away and play. That
will make him feel quite futile. And as
there have been 6J years of Federation
without any infringement of State Rights,
the wretched official hasn’t much chance
of finding anything for his salary. Yet if
he doesn’t find anything he will presently
be sacked _as useless. And if he finds
bogus infringements his legal reputation
will become a joke and a laughing-stock.
Why shouldn’t he hunt for the Holy Grail in
between times so that the States will get some-
thing for their money? Any fairly-swift official
could run after the two chimeras at the same time.
