the fact that :it was
nearly shaken out of
existence by earth-
quakes during 1901 and
1902. Dismal Massey,
the leader of the poor
old toothless Conserva-
tive party —the party
which, when it was in
power, ran relief works
at 2s. Gd. a day and
soup kitchens in the
principal M.L. towns—
this tine old Massey
visited Cheviot re-
cently, and told an
astonished audience
that he would fight for
them and the freehold
till his dead body was
carried off the Opposi-
tion benches. Every one
of his hearers remem-
bered the time, some
16 years ago, when
Massey and his crowd fought tooth and nail to let
old squatter Robinson keep the freehold of
Cheviot. Sixteen years ago Massey wouldn’t let
the “ common people ” have the freehold or the
leasehold or anything else, and the folks who are
now on Cheviot might have died in the back
streets of Auckland, or on the “Devil’s Half-
Acre” in Dunedin, for all the Tory class cared.
And now Massey lays his paw on his stomach and
offers to perish for them, talking furiously to the
last.
It is notified that, the Commerce Act
having been five months in operation, no further
grace will be given to importers to familiarise
themselves with its provisions, and future
breaches will be dealt with in the Special Customs
Court. Australia is very kind to the importer.
In old English days the man who was caught
swindling the Customs was speedily thrown into
prison, when he wasn’t shot on sight; and even
to-day the casual spieler who stops the country
yokel in Bourke or George-street and sells him a
brummy ring as gold is promptly given six months
as a rogue and a vagabond. But the importer
who sells tin tailings as pure silk has a special
court all to his own cheek, so that he shan’t have
to rub against the other rogue, and gets off with
a fine of about a tenth of the sum he filched from
the public.
The Austral seasons come and go
In summer’s heat and winter’s snow ;
And spring unfolds her fresh green shoots ;
And autumn brings her gift of fruits.
Each change is fraught with human ills,
With coughs and colds and sudden chills ;
But life is safe, and health is sure
By taking Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*
Still another crowd, which is mostly
horrified when the union bricklayer or miner
wants preference for unionists, is vigorously
demanding preference for itself. This is the
Institute of Accountants of Victoria, which wants
the Federal Attorney-General to put in the new
Companies Act, a clause giving members of the
institute an absolute monopoly of companies’ audit
work in Victoria. There are some reasons why
auditors should be licensed; but it isn’t for private
coteries to do the licensing. Notoriously some of
the best accountants in the States don’t belong
to any of these “institutes,” simply because they
don’t need the poor little advt. which member-
ship confers.
A traveller lately discovered a bridge at
the back of Bowen (Q.), where it was lost about
30 years ago. This structure, which cost many
thousands of pounds—now a pretty rotten perma-
nent and reproductive asset to set against Cohen’s
loan—was put up in 1872, at a time when Ravens-
wood trafficked direct with Bowen. When
Charters Towers sprang into existence trade
filtered that way, en route to Townsville, so
Bowen was neglected, and millions were spent to
make Townsville a passable harbor. (Philp re-
presents Townsville.) Thus the great bridge
went out of use after it had carried across the
Burdekin only a few hundred tons of merchandise.
In the course of time floods came down and swept
away the approaches, so the structure was left
standing in midstream, weird and woebegone.
The other day a traveller went that way and dis-
covered it—the permanent and reproductive asset
that had been lost, strayed and forgotten.
From “ Caber ” :—-
Bob Philp has a deal of luck on his side. At the late
Queensland elections Labor presented him with five seats,
and Kidston handed him three. His candidates won on
minority votes at Cairns, Carnarvon, Murilla, Musgrave,
Woollongabba, South Brisbane (2), and the Valley. I
anticipated this, but expected Philp would also retain
the four seats he lost, and win Warwick, Nundah,
Aubigtiy, Oambooya, and the second seat for Drayton
and Toowoomba, so was ready to swear he won hi have
a working majority. It’s Bob’s turn to swear no.v. Any
hope he may have for a coalition with either Kidston or
Bowman doesn’t look too promising, and just there his
luck peters out. Kidston wouldn’t join him, and if he
did, Kerr, Jackson, Cowap, Maxwell, Kenna, Grant,
Brennan, and Woods would return to the Labor camp,
with a strong probability of M‘ln tyre and Blair accom-
panying them. A coalition with Bowman is too preposter-
ous ; thus Philp may consider himself removed from active
service. If Kidston has the wisdom of the serpent about
the establishment, now is the psychological moment to
yank it out. Philp should he made Speaker, and in less
than six months Janny Leahy, his successor, would
bump the Tory party over so many political logs ttiat a
pitiful wreck would be a complimentary term to apply to
it.
THE CREEP OF THE ASIATIC .
The remodelling of Sydney Parliament
House proceeds apace. Carruthers inspcrs ap
provingly and often a suite of rooms designed
for the Premier —an office, a private study
and a bedroom, all fitted with polished rose-
wood, marble mantels, and stamped metal ceil-
ings. A dreadful rumor has it, however, that
boiler-maker and Opposition leader McGowen
has also been seen approvingly inspecting the
rooms, just as the next heir may call and inspect
the house when the old man isn’t feeling well, or
as the crow may inspect the sheep when that
animal’s woolly race is nearly run.
Tasmanian Senator Dobson, drifting re-
cently through Sydney, dropped the opinion that
“it would be a great blunder to establish the
Federal capital in the bush.” In this respect
Dobson and his kind are suggestive of the lout
who was urged by his father to get married, and
who defended himself on the ground that he
didn’t know any girls. “Bustle about and get
to know some, same as I had to do,” said the old
man. And the lout snarled back: “Yes, that’s
all very well. You married mother, but I’ve got
to marry a strange girl.” To Dobson and his
kind Sydney and Melbourne are mother, and the
bush capital is the strange girl. They can’t
understand that mother was also a strange girl
once, and that Sydney and Melbourne were
also bush capitals at one time. So, for
that matter, were London and Rome and
Babylon and Calcutta, and every other
metropolis. Australia badly wants another
city ; far too much of its population is centred in
its two big capitals. The only way to make a new
city, is to start it just as Sydney and Melbourne
were started—in the bush. And if Dobson & Co.
could understand the simple proposition that the
strange girl beside the Snowy River will also be-
come mother in course of time, they would talk
less foolishness about “the bush capital.” The
term itself shows their mental confusion. While
it is bush it isn’t a capital, and when it becomes
a capital it won’t be bush.
The fact that Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy
is pleasant to take has made it a favorite with
mothers everywhere.*

There is no doubt that New South Wales had better
be alive to the (act that she has not many friends. —

Too true, Joe !
A crowd of enemies has N.S.W.,
And if you’ve half a-mo’ to spare I’ll trouble you
To name a greater than her Premier wild,
Who apes the antics of a pampered child—
Inventing Ma State woes that his poor self
May keep from being placed upon the shelf.
Don’t you, Joe,
By this late time of day the hopeless folly see
Of stocking only baseless wails as policy?
What foes of State are worse than those with hungers
For chance to pose as vamped-up grievance-mongers f
Go to, Joe !

SPORTING
NOTIONS
“Old ’Un”: Bulletin par. (6/6/’O7), anent
Wren’s booming of Squires, is to the point, and correct.
Squires may be a wonder, but he hasn’t proved it yet.
Kling, Murphy, Bill Smith, Mike Williams and co. were
as so many ninepins set up to be toppled over. Felix gave
at least as good as he got in 11 rough rounds with
Squires at Newcastle, and no more deserved disqualifica-
tion for fouling than his opponent, as each transgressed
frequently, but the black never tackled the ex-miner
with the same heart again. ’Twas like Mick Dooley
opposed to that other great Australian natural fighter of
years ago—Joe Goddard. Don’t say Bill Squires was
taught by Bill Smith. If Smith tried to teach Squires, he
failed badly. The latter’s position is akin to that of the
bullocky with his language : “It wasn’t l’arned ; it’s a
gift.” Squires is lucky in getting Burns, who fights to
suit the Australian, and, though a few pounds heavier,
has 3in, less height and not near the same reach—disad-
vantages which should take some getting over unless
made up for by greater skill.
• * * *
The most remarkable thing about the unex-
pected is the frequency with which it happens. In fact,
it is always doing so, but nobody seems to get accus-
tomed to it. This explains the stupefaction with which
Sydney was overwhelmed when it heard that Queensland
had defeated its Rugby team by II to six, in the dull,
damp afternoon of Saturday last. It was an outrage of
the worst sort, for of the last 10 matches played, Queens-
land had gained only one. Last year the whole four
went to the Ma State by 11 to nine, eight to six, 25 to
three, and 13 to eight. For Queensland to win at all was
looked upon as amazing, but to do so by 11 to six was
regarded as quite impossible. The N.S.W. team does not
appear to have played together at all, while Queensland
entered the field with a magnificent forward team, a
pack that played with such perfect combination as to
suggest much practice together, and with an unselfish-
ness that is far above rubies or fat cigars. And it had its
reward—ll to six.
Much wonder has been expressed at the “in-
different ” play of the Sydney cracks. As a matter of fact,
it is a tribute to their sterling Rugby qualities. The best
men in club Rugby are not individualists —they are ex-
cellent parts of good machines. D’Alpuget, the reliable
and solid full-back of Eastern Suburbs, was not recog-
nisable in the nervous tumbler of Saturday afternoon,
and yet they were the same man. Messenger, the
brilliant three-quarter of the same fine team, was dull as
a tallow rush-light compared to the sparkling three-
quarter, whose forwards and half-backs live to feed him,
and who, in turn, feeds his wing men, to take it back again
later on. Just at present everybody is blaming the team
that lost, and mentioning names of sterling players who
ought to be dropped out of the next team. But in a few
days they will come to their senses and blame, not the
players, but the management. The team should have
been picked weeks ago and played together at least half-
a-dozen times, and then the downfall might not have
happened.
Play was somewhat rough on both sides, but the
only serious casualty was the breaking of the leg of N.S. W.
captain, Harold Judd, who collided with one of his own
three-quarters. Although his loss was a serious matter
to his side, there remains the discomforting fact that all
the scoring was done in the first half, while Judd was on
deck, so that N.S.W. was beaten before its skipper was
laid out. Playing with the wind, Queensland made all
its points in the first half, and, when N.S.W. got the wind
in the second half, it failed to add a point to its score,
although it certainly did prevent Queensland increasing
its figures.
* * * *
It remained for the League (Australian rules)
inter-State team to hold up the N.S. W. end of the log.
Port Adelaide, the S.A. premier team, came to slay; and
devour ; but N.S.W. 18 did the devouring, Port Adelaide
being eaten to the tune of 57 points to 44. Only some
3000 people rolled up to see N.S.W. win, while 25,000
swarmed along to see their State w’alloped on the fatal
Rugby field. It u'as the superior speed of the Sydney
Leaguers which enabled them to outclass their Adelaide
rivals. Hitherto no one has taken N.S.W. Australian
football very seriously, but this decisive win, by 13
points, is a fair warning that the Cornstalk leaguers are
no longer small potatoes. At first Port Adelaide showed
superior skill in taking marks, but after a little practice
the Cornstalks began to do the kangaroo act with equal
skill, and then their superior speed began to tell.
In the Sydney Soccer football game Pyrmont
and Glebe are still at the head of the competition with
10 points each, each team having managed to down
its opponents. Pyrmont, in particular, was called
upon to prove its quality by Petersham, the baby club.
Balmain and Ultimo are in second place with eight points
each, Rozelle and Petersham third with five, and Navy,
by virtue of its bye, is two.
Glebe won its match against Rozelle by the
narrow margin of one goal, the figures being four to three.
But the interest of the day was centred on the meeting
of the precocious infant, Petersham, with Pyrmont, last
year’s premier team. In the first half the baby had the
advantage of a strong wind, and although Pyrmont,
by a lucky chance, did score a goal, it put in
most of its time saving its meat by very narrow
margins. The spell found the scores—Pyrmont one,
Petersham nil. With the wind behind it in
the second half Pyrmont came down like the Assyrian,
and like that ancient party came to grief. After a fear-
some struggle round the Petersham goal the play sud-
denly raged away to Pyrmont goal, and Petersham
scored, equalising the figures. The premier team found
itself hard pressed, but managed to avoid any further
damage, although it had quite a number of narrow
escapes, the ball twice going over instead of under the
bar. Then Petersham’s wind gave out, its strength
turned to froth, like the beer that is half uncorked, and
Pyrmont scored four goals in short order, and won by five
to one. The other match, that between Balmain and
Ultimo, ended in a draw, each team scoring a goal.
* * * *
Last week both the Yic. League and Associa-
tion suspended their premiership matches, the former to
meet Ballarat and Bendigo, the latter to attend to S.
Australia. The Oroweaters sent over the heaviest team
they have exported for years, the result being a complete
surprise. Instead of a rough scramble, in which weight
beat skill, the 10,000 spectators got far and away the
finest game seen in Melbourne this season. There were
faults, of course. The play was slow at the start, and
there was far too much throwing of the ball; nevertheless
there were no scrimmages or scuffling ruck play
—the ball was travelling from end to end of
the ground the whole time. The big men were very
slow in getting a move on, and in the middle of
the second quarter the Vies, had put up five goals
three behinds to one goal four behinds, and the crowd
was silently preparing to move homewards. But once
the Oroweaters got warm they levelled matters, and at
half-time the tally was Vic., 7.6 ; S.A., 6.5. Lemon-time
saw S. A. men leading by 10.9 to 9.9, and, if they didn’t tire,
a strenuous fourth quarter was promised. It came as it
comes but once in aseason. The big fellows from Price’s
State threw their last ounce into it, and in 15 minutes
had notched three more goals, while the Bentites could
only score a goal and five behinds. It looked all over,
but the dark blues kept their heads and, playing
straight to one another, put up three goals
in twice as many minutes. The crowd yelled itself
hoarse. The Vies, had a five-point lead, and it was but
two minutes from “time.” But a goal would gives. A.
the victory, if it could get one. From the bounce in the
centre a tall fellow jumped and punched the ball to a
waiting mate outside the crush. He kicked it on again,
but his mate fumbled and the leather went out of bounds.
Tossed in, the manoeuvre was repeated, and a Croweater
“marked” 50.yds. from goal at an angle. It was odds
against his sending it through, and it was S. A.’s last hope
—the timekeeper had picked up the bell. Swiftly the
Croweater passed the ball to a mate 20yds. nearer goal,
who held it. Carefully placing the oval, he took his run,
and even as the ball rose the bell rang, but it sailed
straight between the posts, and South ’Oss had won a
fairly-played, hard-fought and beautifully open game by
one point—93 to 92. One question the game unfortunately
leaves undecided —whether weight and height are better
than pace and trickiness. Some of the Vic. League teams
have of late gone in for weight alone—with some skill, of
course—and so far they are at the top of the tree ; how
they will fare when the wet weather makes the grounds
mere swamps remains to be seen. On the Melbourne
ground a League 18 met a Ballarat team, while another
squad went up to Bendigo to try conclusions with the
locals. In both cases the results justified the forecasts of
the crowds that unanimously stayed away, the League
beating Ballarat by 170 points to 25 and Bendigo by
94 to 53.
* * * *
Those South Melbourne pushites who bar-
rack for “ Skinner’s hirelings ” had a great day on Prince
of Wales’ birthday, when, just as the game against
Geelong concluded, an aged St. Kilda barracker opined
that that club would make shows of the lot of them.
Having as little respect for age as they have for them-
selves, some South larrikins hustled the old boy, and
on a constable interfering “went for” the John whole-
sale. Three were arrested, with great difficulty, several
cab windows and constables’ helmets being wrecked in
the process. Fines of £7, £3, and £2 met the cases in
the opinion of the honorariums.
* * * *
Country football seems even more willing than
the city variety. At a recent Wednesday afternoon match
at Port Campbell, near Camperdown (Vic.), two players
had to be taken to hospital, one with two ribs broken, the
other suffering from an ill-directed kick in the abdomen.
At another local match, the same day, Mortlake, which
was discussing matters with Camperdown, was so in-
censed at a goal umpire for allegedly giving a goal as a
behind that nearly the whole 18 went for the one lone
man, tore his flags to pieces, and chased him off the field.
He managed to escape on his bike, but he’ll think twice
before umpiring for Mortlake again.
* * * *
“Pivot” writes in an illogical and discrepant
manner, alleging that The Bulletin’s Melbourne scribe
belongs to the Stinking Fish party. He doesn’t say
exactly why he thinks so, but apparently his ire is aroused
because the City of Dreadful Sleep—Geelong, to wit—
can’t raise a football team that has any hopes of the
premiership. He says, “’Wood, South, Carlton, Rich-
mond, Prahran, Essendon (A.), Preston, West, all have
Geelong’ players shining in their ranks.” Perhaps so ;
but, to take a particular instance, a South player—a
man who was not born in Geelong-, who has played
for three years for Richmond and two for South,
but who works in Geelong because he has a decent billet
there —hardly belongs to that Abode of Sleep. Doubtless
many players have been seduced away from the Geelong
club by metropolitan offers of billets ; but so they have
from other cities and States ; so they are in other games,
and in other professions. This writer, be it noted, is not
advocating professional football —he merely states the facts
as he learns them. He has always advocated district foot-
ball, but while the League club managers and those of
Essendon (A.), Prahran, Williamstown, among others,
act as they do, there is no hope for district football. As
for Geelong refusing to pay its players, which “ Pivot ”
asserts is the case, and which seems to be assigned as the
reason of its backwardness—well, these League secre-
taries have been challenged to make sworn de-
clarations that they do not pay any players, and
their only answer has been a monumental silence.
Anyhow, what does Geelong do with its cash ? It gets
the same share of the finals as the other clubs, and its
proportion of its own matches just as, say, Saints and
Melbourne, which last year divided with it the honor of
tailing the list of League clubs. If it shouts no suits of
clothes, boots, hot sea baths, etc., this spotless and
wholly non-professional club must have a nice little pile
stowed away somewhere.
* * * •
“ Nulla ” : Saw it remarked (B. 6/6/’O7) that the
superiority of N.S.W. city football teams compared to the
country ’bailers represented a state of things not found
in Maoriland. It wasn’t found in N.S.W. either in earlier
days. I remember when the Sydney lads used to go down
pretty frequently before the Western Union team. In
fact, until the secession of the clubs now forming the
Central Western Union, things were always pretty even,
and the last match the combined westerners played
(about ’94) wound up with a city defeat. The twin
curses of big landlordism and centralisation are no doubt
responsible for the decadence of country sport. Booting
a ball won’t keep a man in bread and butter, to say
nothing of beer, in the country, and, in pursuing a job,
he drifts away, so that it is rare to see the same mem-
bers in a team two years running. It is different in the
city. There the teams are like the Australian XI.; a man
only drops out through sheer senile decay. Wickham,
for instance, must remember the days when football con-
sisted in letting the ball drift to oblivion, or some other
place equally indefinite, while the plavers kicked one
another till all, including the air, was blue. The days, in
fact, when, as an occasional rheumy-eyed has-been will
inform us, “ We played like asterisked blanks.”
* * » *
They have got football form down pretty fine
in England. The 20 leading clubs that form the League
play 38 matches each, and in the season just ended no
club won more than 22, or lost more than 20 ! (This is
explained by the fact that “soccer” matches end in a
tie quite once in every four times.) With two points
counted for a win, and one for a draw, the best and
worst of the professional clubs come out thus :
Goals.
Played. Won. Lost. Drawn. Points. For. Against
Newcastle 38 22 9 7 51 74 46
Stoke ..38 8 20 10 26 41 64
When the last mail left, the clubs were all trying to fill
any weak spots in their teams for next September. (A
close season of four months, May Ist to September Ist, is
rigidly' enforced, for fear the public should get sick of
the game.) As an example, a Birmingham club, not even
in the first division of the League—but anxious to get
there—was paying a London team £4OO for a transfer of
its talented “centre-half-back.” The .youth is 24, and
has already played for a Lancashire club, for Leicester,
and in London. How local interest is kept up in the im-
portations is difficult to see. Of the eleven men that by
birth represented Scotland in the internationals this
year, eight were members of various clubs in England !
Objection is being taken to the careless manner
in which the League footballers dress for the game in
Sydney. The first-grade teams are the worst offenders,
frequently' appearing on the ground with bare legs (from
knee to ankle only) and with these legs unshaven. Years
ago, some of the swimmers took to shaving their supports
and using bear’s grease on them liberally. It wasn’t real
bear’s grease, of course—you never get that unless you
slay' your own bear —but it was what is called bear’s
grease. The result was something that would have
turned Julius Knight green with envy, and hundreds of
girls flocked to the carnivals where the beautiful
objects were on view. The Leaguers in Sydney badly
require to do something to draw the crowds, and if the
teams that play in the enclosed grounds were to go in for
beauty-leg cultivation it would probably start a rush of
girls, and the girls would bring their own boys with them
for educational purposes.
* * ♦ *
At the Targo Florio reliability motor-car race,
lately run in Sicily, the cars that came first, second, and
third, also all those that came after them, right up to the
car that finished ninth, were shod with Michelin tyres.
The race extended over 400 miles of rough mountain
country; so the Michelin tyre did well. Likewise the
winner and second in the Targo Florio Voiturette Oup
race were shod with the same good old reliable tyre.*
* » * *
The impending retirement of the energetic
fighting secretary of N.S.W. League of Wheelmen, Hugh
D. Mclntosh, will be a severe blow to that body. Pro-
fessional racing cyclists are, in the mass, unmitigated
pointers and scoundrels, and Fighting Mac has grown
a-weary of trying to keep them honest and straight in
spite of themselves. In between the four years of wield-
ing the axe on poolers and other crooked riders he has
built up a huge catering business in Sydney, and, in
addition, now runs the tourist resorts at National
Park, Sydney', and Mt. Kosciusko, Monaro. His four
years’ experience as secretary of a racing body, in which
the animals that did the racing talk, would make an in-
teresting book. Mr. Mclntosh was a great believer in
Faulconbridge’s maxim, “Straight let us seek, or
straight we shall be sought! ” and when a crooked rider
suddenly got an inspiration, as one did that night
he sent one man sprawling on the grass and threw
the other against the pickets, he usually wants a little
time to work out a reasonable chain of accidents and
reasons. Mclntosh never gave him that time, but came
down on him suddenly. Then the man whose brains
had largely run to calves, got mixed, blundered, and gave
his case away.
* * * *
One of the bitterest prejudices the average anti-
gambling Oarruthersite parson owns is against the totali-
sator system of betting. These saintly ones do not ask
that wagering shall be squelched utterly. They are not
quite so unworldly and foolish as that, though they do
believe in anti-Sosh, B. Smith, the malignancy of Victoria,
and such-like futilities. What they want is to see the
devastating curse “ regulated,” as the cant phrase goes.
The ideal they set before themselves, judging by their
attitude in regard to Wade’s recent anti-gaming measure,
is a racecourse enclosure, spotted with well-pleased
Israelites and their mostly' indifferently-pleased clients,
all betting for unlimited sums under the benevolent eye
and protection of the law. Now gambling by totalisator
is necessarily limited in extent. Even in Paris, where ten
money'ed people go racing to every one here, the invest-
ment of £lOO on a horse will send him out favorite in five
races out of six, and that by a process as mechanical as
the working of a penny-in-the-slot machine. In a word,
the moment a certain comparatively trivial amount is
passed through the pari-mutuel machine for any particu-
lar animal, it ceases to be worth the owner’s while, or
anyone else’s, to back that animal further. Under the
bookmaking sy’stem which obtains here, and which the
Reform parsons are helping to foster and maintain,
money may be won or lost practically to any
extent. If the layer of odds is sufficiently venturesome,
or has the horse stiffened, or happens to be broke anyhow
and “ going for the gloves,” or any other old thing of the
sort, there are no limits to the sums he may risk. And,
in any case, a book running well into the thousands is
nothing’ exceptional where most of our penoillers in a
fair way of business are concerned. The (theoretically)
anti-gambling parson, who in practice is in favor of the
biggest and most flagrant form of gambling we have here,
constitutes one of the queerest of anomalies.

That money for betting isn’t so plentiful in the
Wheat State this as last year was evidenced at Adelaide
Birthday meet, which ended on Saturday. Three days’
tote turn-over reached £29,361, as against £31,087 twelve
months before. Big event (Alderman Cup) of last day
saw the once Wren-owned Pius favorite, but neither he
nor the two next fancied—Cairngorm and Red Streak—
were placed. The winner, Ganymedes, was among the least
fancied, and paid £l6 odd. Cairngorm’s comparatively
poor showing suggests that there was much of fluke in
his Birthday triumph.

gether.

The recently-announced retirement of that
brilliant performer, Achilles, brings to mind that during
six seasons of racing he won 25 times in 45 starts, and
gathered £8930 prize money. Achilles was bred at the
Tillimbystud (N.S.W.), and is by the Nordenfeldt horse
Medallion, from the Goldsbrough mare Sea Swallow.
* * •* *
Moorefield (Sydney)races(Saturday)introdueed
a promising youngster in Cryograft, who won Two-Year-
Old Handicap (sfur.) quite easily, beating such known
good ones as Scotch Annie (favorite) and Maesmore,
also others, pointless. If Cryograft can stay—and there
was everything in her run to indicate that she possesses
that quality—there’s plenty' of money ahead for the stable.
The filly is by Grafton from Cryolite, and strongly inbred
to Stockwell.
• * » •
N.S.W. and Victoria having, by special legisla-
tion, recognised and established on a legal footing the
hitherto “unregistered racing,” serious doubts have
arisen in both States as to the power of the A.J.C. and
V.R.C. to refuse the entries of horses whose owners are
guilty of no offence but competing at unregistered meet-
ings. In N.S.W. the A.J.C. has practically admitted the
injustice of doing so, by removing the embargo from
something like a hundred horses, and a similar
number of owners, trainers, and jockey's, and
it has announced that another batch will be
pardoned later on. In Victoria, the metropolitan pony
clubs are run by John Wren, and the V.R.C. is not taking
any Wren at any price. It threw down the glove in a
most unmistakable manner by wiping out the entry of a
horse named Cuirass, for the Birthday meeting, on the
ground that the nag had competed at one of Wren’s
meetings. Wren and the owner are talking about taking
legal action to prove that the V.R.C. had no power to
strike out the entry, and the V.R.C. says, in effect, “Fire
away! ” If Wren and the owner do fire away,
it will be a most interesting case. But the
amusing feature of the business is that in N.S.W.
the unregistered association is talking of disqualifying
the men who take advantage of the A. J.C.’s “ clemency.”
Whatever may happen in Victoria there seems little
doubt what will eventuate in N.S.W. Any row will
simply bring into existence the “ Advisory Board ” pro-
vided for by the Wade Act, which will take the matter
right out of the hands of the wrangling clubs and settle
it over their heads, and, most likely, in a manner that
will please none of them. The money hunger seems to
be at the root of the trouble —not any high-minded con-
sideration for the “ best interests” of the sport that does
so much “ to improve the breed of horses.”
•* * * *
The inter-Association bowling match between
N.S.W. and the Northern Association (Newcastle) took
place on Wickham green (Newcastle), under a damp sky,
on wet grass, and with greasy wood. Whether it was
that the N.S.W. men were so wet inside that the exterior
dampness was unnoticed, writer knoweth not, but they
certainly scored in a remarkable manner, winning by 119
to 83.
While these things were happening at New-
castle, at Ashfield green (Sydney) Mosman and Balmain
fours were struggling for the Rink Championship. Mos-
man had never a hoDe, Balmain winning by 39 to 11.
*** * *
Saturday last was Redfern’s day out at hurling.
The first-grade team beat that of City by 24 points to
five, and the second beat the City 11. by 18 points to
seven. Thisplaces Redfern at the head of both competi-
tions, with four points in the first grade and 12 in the
second. City I. and Annandale are two points each. In
the second-grade competition Marrickville, Glebe, and
City 11. have 11, two and one points each respectively.
* * *
Another unbeaten team met its Cannae on
Saturday last in Sydney, when the crack Petersham
crowd of baseballers was defeated by Chatswood by 11 to
nil. Not only was it a record hiding, but it was ad-
ministered in record time, the wiping-out of the team
occupying exactly five minutes. Paddington also ad-
ministered “ a well-deserved castigation ” to North
Sydney, flooring the Northmen by 11 to six, and Woollahra
crushed University, at Centennial Park, by 19 to six.

“ Hank ” : lie your par. (B. 6/6/’O7) anent cham-
pion wrestler Weber’s retirement, and Rennert’s assump-
tion of the title. Rennert challenged Weber long before
the latter fixed up his last match with Singh, but Weber
made excuses, and, all along, showed disinclination to

that Weber and Rennert were together in the Hacken-
schmidt troupe during the latter’s Sydney engagement.

Weber’s side-tracking of Rennert for Buttan Singh hasn’t

apparently still has vivid recollections of the summary
manner in which Hackenschmidt dealt with him, and
Rennert is an apt and a hall-marked pupil of the Rus-
sian’s. Rennert did not come from New Guinea ; he
never saw that section excepting on the map. Gus was
in the engine-room of the German liner Prinz Regent
Luitpold, and when persuaded by the late Mr. Hajek and
Mr. W. F. Corbett to remain in Sydney, he couldn’t speak
a word of English. Peculiarly enough, Pagel, the strong
man, while engaged as kitchen hand in a Macquarie-place
(Sydney) restaurant, was discovered by the same two
people.
* * » *
Melbourne Harriers’ five miles novice and
second teams race cross country—the first event of the
season—attracted some 120 runners (representing ten
teams) to Oakleigh, last Saturday afternoon. They got
away with a scrambling rush, the first mile or so being
especially hot. Over the actual cross-country the going
and weather conditions tended to fast times, so the peds
put the pace on. Young Geo. Stillwell, a promising wind-
catcher and cousin to ex-champion ten-miler Aleck Still-
well, was prominent right along the line, eventually
winning by 150yds. The time, 28min. 48sec., is a record
for the course. On time allowance, E. C. Kelly, a ’Varsity
ped, got away with the Novice, and Melbourne A team
blew out Essendon in the teams race. The first half-
dozen to leg it home in the aforesaid invariably show up
well in the big ten-miler at Caulfield in September—
unless they die, or get otherwise disabled in the mean-
while.
THE HOLY SPORT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Judkins, at a Y.M.C.A. dinner to Melbourne press-
men, said billiards, boxing-, wrestling and prayer were
excellent exercises for young men. But Juddy is a fierce
opponent of boxing in the professional halls.
When godly youths put up their dukes,
And give each other toko,
Dear Juddy utters no rebukes—
A blessing he’ll invpke-o
For when they’ve punched their noses they
Will fall upon their knees and pray,
And that condones the fistic play,
Likewise the damaged boko.
But if the tough, unholy pug.
Wades in his foe to rattle,
And hits his man a heavy slug,
Jud hates such raffish cattle,
And must till Willie Judkins be
Appointed pious referee,
To pray both long and fervently
Before and after battle.
For prayer as exercise our ad-
’Miration won’t diminish.
We’ve heard the elders rave like mad
In voices fierce if tinnish,
And think the new athletic age
In praying contests will engage,
And saints upon their, knees will rage
For points, or to a finish !
Vic.

A CIVIC OFFICER
FORTIFIES HIS SYSTEM AGAINST PLAGUE AND
DISEASE BY USING DR. MORSE’S INDIAN
ROOT PILLS.
Mr. Samuel Williams, who has been engaged
for the past three years by the Municipal Board
of Health in connection with the cleansing of the
infected areas of Sydney, states that he takes Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills regularly, and thus
keeps his blood pure and his system in good
health. He writes “ 9 Wymess-street, Sydney.
—For over 2J years I have been employed by the
MR. SAMUEL WILLIAMS.
(From a photo.)
City Council in connec-
tion with the Sanitary
Department, cleansing
the city and wharves in
the infected areas. A'"
though I have been in-
oculated, I have always
found that the best pre-
ventive against disease
and common ailments is
to keep the body well,
and in this respect there
is no better remedy than
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills. My wife and
family use them occasion-
ally, and while we have
them in the house we
never require the doctor
or any other medicine.
You have my permission to use this, together
with my photo.” Cleanliness is next to Godli-
ness, but it is not alone necessary to keep the
outside clean; the interior must also be kept in a
sanitary condition. Many a tenement in Sydney
from all outside appearances looks clean and
healthy, but a coat of paint on the outside does
not prevent the germs of disease and pestilence
breeding in the dirty, unwholesome inside. So
with the human frame. The microbes enter the
system, and if the bowels are constipated and un-
healthy, and the blood impure, there it finds a
veritable harbor to germinate and gather in
another victim. Why is it that medical men so
seldom fall victims to disease? Well, the reason
is they consult themselves, and knowing that the
true secret of health is the regularity of the
system and the maintenance of unvitiated blood,
they take every precaution to this end. thou-
sands of men and women have learnt this secret,
and like our correspondent (Mr. Williams) use
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and fear neither
disease or any of the common ailments to which
mankind is heir. Be your own doctor. Learn
that a pure and regular system is the only proof
against disease and ill-health. Experience has
taught many this lesson. You may learn by their
experience. Ask your friends, neighbors, and
relatives what Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills ha e
done for them.

McKenzie v. Grace suit, recently con-
cluded in Sydney, provided the spectacle
of shoddy divided against itself. Reid was
briefed by the McKenzie faction, and he enve-
loped linen-draperism in a mighty Rabelaisian
cloud of laughter. The drama— what there was
of it—was supplied by the various accusations
and cross-accusations of subornation that were
hurled around (apparently everyone was ap-
proached in this case, barring the Judge). Much
Blandoltian humor was provided by Reid in his
researches into the color, size, texture, etc., of
certain articles of female underwear, which, with
an ancient and battered giddiness, he archly
termed “ unmentionables.” Finally, in answer
to one pressing question, a female witness tartly
informed George that he knew as much about
these articles as she did —whereat the ‘‘outsize”
lawyer-politician blushfully subsided.
Carru bhers’ fevered anxiety at the Bris-
bane Conference to distract N.S.W. electors’
attention from his own littleness as a State poli-
tician, by trying to talk big at the Australian
Government, was so apparent that the other
State Premiers got sick of him at last, and spoke
their disgust to the press interviewers as soon as
the foolish conclave’s adjournment gave them a
loose lip. Some opinions;—
From the initial stages the proceedings lacked unani-
mity, and the discussions were tinged with provincial
influences, and colored in some instances by an apparent
desire to make use of the Conference for political pur-
poses. — Premier Evantt ( Tag.).
Mr. Oarruthers appeared to be influenced by the coming
elections. — Acting-Premier Davies (Vic.).
He was surprised, during his present visit to the
Eastern States, to learn that there was a desire to secure
a reversi m to pre-Federal habits of Government.—
Premier Moore (W.A.).
He could not help noticing the utter lack of sympa-
thy expressed by Mr. Oarruthers toward the Common-
wealth, which, if he had his way, would not be allowed to
stir a peg or move in anything. New South Wales has
been assisted in keeping up its white Australia policy,
for which the Commonwealth has paid £187,328, and the
Queensland sugar bounty amounted to £512,319. South
Australia was as much entitled to consideration in deal-
ing with her Northern Territory as the other States had
been in respect of their particular questions.— Treasurer
Peake (S.A.).
Then Price, of 8.A., condemned the whole silly
show, in which Oarruthers was star performer, in
one smashing sentence :
No distinct Australian spirit was displayed at all.
By sending out 1,000,000 copies of a good,
clear map of Australia, Anthony Hordern & Sons
give a valuable tip to some people, who need it
badly, that Sydney isn’t N.S. Wales, and that
there is quite a lot of the Commonwealth that
isn’t even N.S. Wales. The map (about 24in. x
18in.) is so full of up-to-date information that he
is a very well-informed Australian to whom it
can’t teach something. It is to be had for one
penny—and you don’t get many things worth as
much at the price.*
Under the wild heading “ Wake up, New
South Wales!” one J. L. Adams commenced a
letter to Sydney Telegraph last week :
The Premier shows that Federation is leading New
South Wales into bankruptcy.
And yet Oarruthers is going round boasting of the
biggest surplus N.S.W 7 . ever had, and says that
owing to the extraordinary prosperity of the
State he is going to take off a lot of taxa-
tion. The capacity of the Anti-Australian
and Stinking Fisherman for wild assertions is
something marvellous.
Westralia is not having too much luck
with some of its recent State-assisted immigrants.
Among a late batch were some Hebrew youths
who were previously inmates of a London
reformatory school. Stolen property, belonging
to passengers on the steamer by which they
travelled, was found in their possession on
arrival, and part of this consignment now ranks
among the most expert thieves in the West.
Charges of evil fame, breaking and entering, and
stealing have been preferred against some of them ;
one of their number is now serving time at Fre-
mantle, and three others of the band have been
committed for trial.
The State Rights party—which is the
anti-Federal party in a thin disguise—is beginning
to find that the beast that Oarruthers has been
recommending can bite. Westralia has been
exercising its “ State Rights ” to levy “penalis-
ing inspection charges ”on t’otherside fruit; now
S. Australia exercises its “State Rights ” to prac-
tically shut out N.S. Wales - fruit; and a month
or so ago Victoria exercised its “State Rights”
and made a bigger concession to its own poor
little coal mines. The State that has been bitten
worst each time is N.S. Wales; wherefore it
begins to have doubts about the Joss it has been
worshipping lately. The North Queensland Chow
frequently hammers his Joss when he prays for
rain, and Joss sends a flood ; and Sydney Tele-
graph and other recent followers of the petty
Oarruthers start to feel very much like the
northern Chow.
The result of the Flinders election en-
courages Price and Peake, of S. Australia, to
stick to their resolution to go straight to the
British Government for a new Constitution on
Transvaal lines, if the Council will not accept a
reasonable measure for its own reform. At pre-
sent the Council represents only the propertied
two-sevenths of the electors entitled to vote for
the State Assembly and for both Federal Houses,
and for 15 years it has blocked every Govern-
ment’s scheme for reforming the franchise on
which it is elected'.
Late S.M. Herald manager Sam Cook
seems to have returned a much better Australian
than when he left Sydney.. After seeing all the
great places and great men of the Empire, Sam
comes back to express the opinion, in published
interviews, that Sydney is the best place of the
lot; that Australia presents the best conditions
of life of all countries, and that the best men in
public life here. Labor as well as Anti-Sosh, com-
pare, in point of ability, favorably with those of
Westminster. Further, when Teddy Roosevelt
asked Sam why Australians called themselves
colonials, Sam turned vehemently on the man
with the big stick and said that they didn’t.
A reliable medicine for croup, and one that
should always be kept at hand for immediate use
is Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy.*
Australian Statist Knibbs has given the
Canadian boomsters and the Australian Stinking
Fish people a nut to crack, in a memorandum to
the Minister for Home Affairs on the text:
“Though you hear a great deal about immigra-
tion to Canada, you don’t hear anything about
emigration.” Knibbs says there are no reliable
Canadian figures hut the census tables, and from
them lie deduces that between 1891 and 1901
Canadian population increased from 4,833,239 to
5,371,315, or a net increase of 538,076. In the
same 10 years the natural increase by excess of
births over deaths was 757,688; so Canada, in
those 10 years, instead of gaining population hv
excess of arrivals, actually lost 219,612 of its
natural increase by excess of departures.
Right in the heart of Melbourne city, at
the top end of Collins-street, where the big trees
round Parliament House look into the Fitzroy
Gardens, there is heard every morning and even-
ing the grand old laugh of the kookaburra. There
is a little colony of ’burras somewhere among those
trees, and they assemble regularly round the
empty Parliament House and smile. They have
been there for years, and when Parliament is in
session, and Joe Cook has been snarling through
an all-night sitting, it is refreshing to hear out-
side, as the morning comes, the honest laugh of the
’burra. It’s a fine healthy-minded bird that can
raise such a robust guffaw over the ashes of the
Cook eloquence.
“S.S.”: It is a mistake to think that
Tommy Bent doesn’t take himself seriously as a
singer. I heard him attempt to sing a hymn
before a gathering of helpless, innocent children
at South Melbourne, and he went through it like
a cheap motor breaking down by the wayside,
Then he told the youngsters that he had learned
all he knew of singing in a Sunday-school choir,
and advised them all to stick to their choir-prac-
tice that they might, when called upon, be, in a
position “to grace any occasion with song. If
T. Bent is not called upon to sing before the
King, it will be somebody else’s fault, be sure of
that. Thomas would sing before the heavenly
choir, if the chance came in his way.

TPHESE JAPANESE HAND WARMERS are as wonderful as they are cheap. When in use they will give forth a
1 delightfully soothing warmth of about 120 degrees. The heating power is produced by a Japanese preparation,
which, when lit, smoulders away very gradually, giving out the warmth through the metal case of the HAND
WARMER or HOT BOX. The Preparation is already made up
Sti
ms-
in Cartridge-shaped packages, each containing a sufficient
quantity for about four hours’ warmth. One of these
Cartridges is placed in the Case, lit at one end with a match,
and the lid put on. Bear in mind that there is no smoke, and
no smell whatever, and, as the preparation only smoulders,
there is NOT THE SLIGHTEST DANGER OP FIRE WHAT-
SOEVER. We positively guarantee this to every purchaser,
and assure you that our JAPANESE HAND WARMERS can be
placed amongst the flimsiest material, or in any position in
the pocket or elsewhere with absolute safety.

FARM LABOURERS.
THE INTELLIGENCE DEPARTMENT
WILL BE GLAD TO HEAR FROM
FARMERS WHO HAVE VACANCIES
FOR FARM HANDS.
Full particulars should be given as to whether experienced hands or beginners are required, and also
as to wages offered. A large number of capable men are reaching New South Wales weekly from the
agricultural districts of Great Britain.
H. C. L. ANDERSON, Director Intelligence Department,

The Inky Way.
“David H. Richards ” : “Prank Morton ” (B. 23/5/’O7)
is right when he says tliat copy got off'the beaten tracks
is not required by reputable newspapers in this part of
the globe ; and, anyhow, at current rates, it would not be
worth while producing- it. There is any quantity of good
copy to be had in Sydney, but it won’t pay for the trouble
required to get it. The Bulletin is the only paper that
will print anything smacking of originality and truth that
touches institutions; etc. ; and, of course, it couldn’t give
every scribe a living, even were it twice as big. Not far
from where I live there is a mission headquarters, or
backquarters, or something, that thieves money in
the name of the Lord by very shady methods.
I would be quite willing to undergo the extreme
inconvenience and repugnant associations that would be
necessary' to learn its inmost workings and to affidavit a
written account, but the daily rags would either scoff at
such copy (preferring an article on “The Glory of our
Parks,” or “The Life of a Jackeroo”), or they would
want it as a letter. I know another alleged charitable
institution in Sydney' that would yield first-class copy,
but no journalist would tackle the task of getting it for
the sake of a paragraph. He’d want to make a fiver out
of the job, at least. But our daily' suppress is too smug,
or too spineless, or too something.
* * » •
Dunedin Star has decided that it will publish no more
accounts of “large and enthusiastic meetings” unless
one of its own staff really gazes upon the size and the
enthusiasm. The Maoriland papers are nOt given to pay'-
ing any more men than they can help, so they have been
quite content to take reports of meetings from secretaries
and other interested people, so long as the reports were
written plainly and on one side of the paper only. This
sort of thing would probably have gone on for another 50
years had not an enterprising gentleman set to work to
manufacture public opinion. He knew' the weakness of
the local press, and turned it to account. He held
enormous and enthusiastic meetings about something or
other, and held them often and in many places, and filled
up the dailies with good accounts of the stirring speeches
delivered thereat. One night, a Star man sought out
one-of these meetings. He found it to consist of half-a-
dozen friends, who sat round a table drinking whisky and
soda, while the promoter sat at a typewriter deftly
tapping off his report. Then the Star shut down on the
manufacturer of public opinion with a heavy bang.
* c * *
“Hexham”: Re pirated leaders (B. 2/5/07), I once
worked under a North of England editor who, five days a
week, stole political articles from far-away published ex-
changes. He used to localise or topicalise the first and
last sentences with “The telegraph informs us,” or
“Such is not, however, the opinion of the Liberals of
South Shields,” and let the rest rip.
* e » »
Refreshing to pick up a recent number of the New
Zealand Times. It contains an effective exposure by Dr.
Mason of various patent medicine frauds, and a straight-
forward article criticising the Maoriland Exhibition’s idea
of e couraging home literature, which concludes
“There is already a sufficient nucleus of Maoriland
prose to furnish material for a special and badly-needed
class of school-books, and the files of the Sydney Bulle-
tin, and volumes of verse sufficient to make a sma.l
library, may' be drawn upon almost inexhaustibly by any
firm that cares to produce a series of Maoriland poetry
books. The average Maoriland child or man or woman
is hopelessly ignorant of the small beginnings of
our literature, and nobody takes any' pains to give them
enlightenment. After all, perhaps the Exhibition story
competition had better be thankfully received, small as
may be its effectiveness, and awful as may' be its fruits.
We observe that the judge is an Australian magazine
editor and proprietor, who, by furnishing Australasia
with certain excellent magazines, consisting almost
entirely of foreign ‘borrowed’ matter, has probably
done more to de-popularise and damage Australian
literary production than any man of his time. Mr.
Fitchett may be a good judge of short stories ; we do not
know. He is certainly unknown to literary Australia,
and we must profess surprise that the Exhibition authori-
ties have not appointed a Maoriland judge, or, at the
very least, a literary critic of some standing.”
That’s the sort of straight talk we don’t get from Aus-
tralian dailies.

slowly compiling a leader-writer’s phrase-book, contain-
ing the very cream of opening sentences. A few
samples:—
“It will be noted with approval that ”... (Sets the
tone for an outpouring of maternal praise.)
“ The community will experience a sense of relief in
learning ”...
“Nothing but congratulation is to be offered to the
Ministry' on ” ...
“ We learn with unfeigned delight that ”...
That is the gamut up which the “ cordial ” article runs.
When the Great Daily sets out to state its wrath it has
another scale:—
“ General uneasiness has been aroused by the announce-
ment that ” .. i
“ The limits of propriety are almost exceeded in the
action of ” ...
“ Grave dissatisfaction will be felt” . . .
“‘A gross scandal’ is the only term which we can
apply' to ...

THE BLOOD AND THUNDER BANNER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
These remarks (by Colonel Foster, R.E.) suggested to
us that it would be well to give a series of articles de-
scribing the chief features of great campaigns and battles.
They will be written by Mr. Donald Macdonald. —Bugle
Call of the Argus.
Ho 1 Granny Argus rises from her blood-bespattered lair ;
She waves the office besom, and unfurls Medusa hair;
She drummeth with a loud pom-pom, and blows a crim-
son air.
She wields her goose quill like a sword, her gamp becomes
a gun,
In word hysterics she’ll record how gory fields were won,
Till paralytic types, Goud Lord, in red ink columns run.
On corpses raised the Editor will sell his tall hat dear;
And Donald Mae will hold the floor, and spill the ruddy
beer
For bugler he, and matador, and football buccaneer.
He’ll tell bloodthirsty 7 histories of battles long ago,
How Xerxes fell to Pericles, how Judkins bit the foe,
And Hannibal, who crossed the seas, blew out to Scipio.
We’ll hear old battle canticles of bones in storied urns,
Napoleonic chronicles for which still Granny yearns,
And (when they sign their articles) how Bill Squires outed
Burns.
The school kid and the office boy will read with faces
pale
Till Deadwood of the Gulch will cloy, and Fitchett’s
Bleeds will stale,
And battles dire ’twixt ’Wood and ’Boy in warlike fervor
fail.
In bugle tones the staff will speak of blood-stained
modesty 7,
Professor Tucker, once a week, talk blood philology,
And leaderettes anemic squeak their bloody policy.
For, bellicose, the Editor will blushing battles baste,
His scissors drip imported gore—he’ll splash the purple
paste
Till sanguine juice is spattered o’er the journalistic waste
YIC.
LOUIS ESSON.

Yes-No’s deputy, the unpleasant Joe
Ooolc, has been laying a church-hall foundation
stone, and, applauding the church that provided
miniature rifle-ranges, &c., said “it was a duty
to defend the blessings of the civilisation we
possessed against the attacks of an inferior
civilisation,'and therefore every man and boy
should learn the use of the rifle.” A bullet may
be the unpleasant Cook’s way of protecting the
civilisation we possess ; but when the foreigner
attacks us with his goods, made under the condi-
tions of his “inferiorcivilisation,” what’s the use
of a bullet then ? Wo are not even allowed to
shoot J. Cook, though he encourages those goods
to come.
A little Sydney pub, somewhat historic, is
in the market. It is at the corner of Elizabeth
and Kettle streets, Redfern, and is known as
The Australian Eleven Hotel. The street, named
after Jack Kettle, the old-time cricketer, forms a
part of what was the Albert Cricket Ground of
the ’sixties and ’seventies. Jack Kettle in the
late ’sixties opened the Cricketers’ Rest in Pitt-
street, Redfern. The pub at the Albert Ground
was established in 1872 by Erederick Lewis Lyons
or Lyon, who was, writer believes, a lithographer
by profession ; he called the house The Old Lion.
He kept it for about six years, when Jack Kettle
came into possession and called it the Australian
Eleven Hotel, and it has retained the name ever
since. The property is still in the Kettle family,
though the site of the old Albert Ground is now
covered with bricks and mortar, and harbors a
colony of bundle-carrying Syrians.

The Premiers’ Conference got very sick of
the cantankerous Carruthers after a week of his
Police Court manners and methods. The last
straw was when he asked the rest to join him in
being cantankerous with regard to the taking
over of the Northern Territory. Disregarding the
fact that the Territory didn’t originally belong
to South Australia, and wasn’t tacked on to
it till 1863, and then was only tacked on
to it in an indefinite sort of way, because it
couldn’t any longer be left lying around loose,
and had to be temporarily fastened on to some-
thing—disregarding all this, Carruthers said that
South Aus. was merely “palming off” its bad
debts on to the rest, and sticking to its good ones.
Carruthers’ utter inability to see the matter from
anything but the small State-insect point of view so
disgusted some other States, which could see that
there was a matter of Australian safety involved,
that Victoria and Queensland simply declined to
vote on his sore-headed motion. But he fooled
the Westralian pair into supporting him; and
when they reflect that the Territory agree-
ment provides for the construction of the Wes-
tralian transcontinental railway, as well as the
Northern Territory railway, and that, voting
against one they vote against the other, they
will, no doubt, be a very disgusted pair indeed.
“ Stony ” sends this :
That considerably decayed Melbourne body, the
Accountants and Clerks’ Association, at present sojourn-
ing in rooms over a fish-shop, has been made the subject
of several unkind attacks in connection with the clerk-
sweating question. Once more it has been mentioned by
poorly-paid clerks as an old-established institution which
has failed to justify its existence. At present, apart from
the fact that it is a club, badly-furnished, and smelling of
dead fish, and patronised by a very small percentage of its
alleged 1000 members, the Association is merely a labor
bureau, which spasmodically advertises for three office
boys at 7s. 6d. a week, and a junior typist at 15s.
In a letter to the Argus (16/5/’O7) the Board of this
Association made several remarks which had moss on
them, and were gone in the knees. Like this: “That
sweating exists we are not prepared to deny, but, speak-
ing from the experience of the association work, we have
no direct evidence that such is the case.” In a letter,
written on the same day, and not in reply to the Board’s
effusion, an emphatic cuss wrote to the Argus (printed
17/5/’O7) that “ thirty shillings seems to be the recognised
wage now to offer clerks, and I have not heard of the
association placing any one of its members even at that
highly-remunerative salary for some considerable time.”
Another outburst in the letter of the blind, mumbling
old A. and O. Board is that “as regards the ques-
tion of legislati n, we fail to see that such a
step would tend to the betterment of the con-
dition of the clerk. It would make little differ-
ence to the seniors, but the services of the least efficient
would speedily be dispensed with. . . . The attempt to
organise the clerks in any numbers is practically hope-
less.” This admitted impotence of the A. and O. Ass., as
well as its senility in argument, must finally settle its
claim to be regarded as a pro-clerical show. Instead of
paying a secretary £3 or £4-a week to hunt up 30-bob-a-
-week jobs, and to provide Board-rooms for a Board that
won’t do anything but talk about the unsweated con-
dition of the pen-pusher, the sweated one should rush
the Clerks’ Union and clamor for admittance, and, if he
gets the sack for trying to better himself by legislating,
let him go and work in a factory or handle a pick and
shovel. It’s astonishing how much self-respect a man
can feel after a day’s hard work with his muscles, par-
ticularly when he is paid more for it than for shoving a
quill over a sheet of pale-blue paper for 10 hours a day.
The British War Office has issued a really
remarkable little handbook for the benefit of
soldiers. It contains a list of technical terms, and
some of the definitions are models of perspicuity.
For instance: “Explosive—that which explodes ” ;
“interior—the inside”; “ignite—to set on fire.”
Plainly, when the enemy comes he will find Bull
ready and waiting.
A suggestion by “ Num,” who seems suffi-
ciently callous to be a large promoter of wild
cats :
I have a rlieme for providing the public with cheap,
healthy amusement, and, at the same time, reducing the
State expenditure on criminal detention, and affording
those who have offended against the law a better oppor-
tunity of reform than they now possess. The scheme is
not wholly original. Its main principles were practised
in ancient Borne, and all I propose is to modify the
Bornan procedure. Briefly, my idea is to revive gladia-
torial combats, having a display, say, every three months.
The combatants would consist of long-sentence prisoners
doing time in different gaols. What I would do is
to make every prisoner with five years’ detention still
ahead of him a gladiator. He would be allowed
three months in which to become proficient in the use of
the net and trident or the broadsword, whichever he pre-
ferred, and would then be pitted against a fellow
prisoner of his own weight and skill. The victors at each
set of games would have the remainder of their sen-
tences . automatically remitted, and go forth free
men with laurel on their brows. The losers would
be accorded a big funeral, and the public would
have a good afternoon’s excitement. In some cases,
of course, there would be no victor, and both com-
batants would be buried. That would be the State’s
gain. As it would be manifestly unfair to allow one out of
every two individuals who had committed capital offences
to go free, I wou.d suggest that murderers should be
allowed to accumulate until there are ten or a dozen in
stock. Then I would give each a sword or tomahawk and
turn the lot loose in the arena, to battle until there was
one left. It would be a thrilling spectacle, and the
community could well afford to allow one murderer his
liberty with the certainty of getting rid of nine or 11.
Besides in most cases the law would soon get
a second shot at the prisoner who escaped.
When Darby Smith and Spud Malone went forth free as
the victors from the January games, they would
immediately resume their profession as garroters and
burglars, and, in all probability, would be again serving
another long sentence by April. The result would be
that they would appear for the July display, and, meeting
one another, would reduce their ranks by fifty per cent.
The same thing would apply all round, and in about
eighteen months there would be quite a scarcity of high-
class criminals—in fact a time would come when the last
of the crowd would have to appear in the arena alone,
and fight with himself. I would like to hear the opinion
of Inspector-General Neitenstein on my project.
Auckland (M.L.) is again considering the
advisability of cutting through the mile and a
half of land that separates Waitemata and
Manukau harbors, and thus bringing the city of
Auckland 480 miles nearer the west, whence all
the great ships come. With the opening of the
North Island Trunk Railway, the ancient capital
of Maoriland expects to become the distributing
port for the whole of the North Island, but in its
trade with Australia 1J miles of land add about
500 miles to the distance, as compared with
Wellington, while in the trade between Auckland
and the Maoriland West Coast that same 1 \ miles
of obstruction make 1000 miles extra distance.
The cutting of the canal would place Auckland on
an equal footing with the great central seaport,
and as in the course of time there is bound to be a
big population around Auckland owing to the
immense deposits of coal, manganese and other
valuable minerals at Whangarei, the commence-
ment of that canal will be the signal for a terrific
land boom in the northern city and parts
adjacent thereto. The canal has been talked of
ever since the far-off day when Hori Grey first
mentioned it, but southern jealousy has always
been strong enough to prevent the Government
attempting the work. Now that Auckland has
grown vvealthy, the city talks of putting up the
money itself. In the meantime the usual “ strong I
syndicate” has been formed, and is taking levels,
sinking holes, and doing other preliminaries while
it amb es round talking about a “concession.”
They are great on “concessions” in Auckland. |

Legal Notice.
WHEREAS by an Order of the High Court of Justice
Chancery Division dated the sth March 1907
made in the Matter of the Estate of William Parker de-
ceased Blundell v. Parker 1906 P. No. 1674, the following
Enquiry was directed namely whether Frederick Parker
the son of the said Testator’s son William Parker was
living or dead at the date of the death of the said Tes-
tator and if he died since the Testator who is his legal
personal representative.
Notice is hereby given that the said Frederick Parker
or if he died since the death of the Testator on the 26th
September 1888 his legal personal representatives and all
persons claiming to be interested under the said Enquiry
are by their Solicitors on or before the 12th day of Oc-
tober 1907 to come in and prove their claims at the
Chambers of Mr. Justice Warrington and Mr. Justice
Parker at the Royal Courts of Justice Strand London or
in default thereof they will be peremptorily excluded
from the benefit of the said Order.
Friday the 18th day of October 1907 at 11 o’clock in the
forenoon at the said Chambers is appointed for hearing
and adjudicating upon the Claims.
Dated this 23rd day of April 1907.
RICHD. WHITE,
Master.
Note.—The above named Frederick Parker went to
Australia in or about the year 1882 and was last heard of
at Adelaide South Australia in the year 1884.
CLAUDIUS GEO.' ALGAR,
17 Abchurch Lane, London,
England,' Solicitor.

BE BEADY for WINTER’S TRIALS
BY BEING STRONG!
Nature uses Winter for a dual purpose in the matter of health.
Winter benefits those people who are in sound health, for the cooler
temperatures invigorate the system. Food is eaten with more zest, a
desire for exercise is created, and the day is followed by sound sleep,
which means real rest.
Nature also uses Winter to thin out the weaklings, and thus
maintain a better standard of health amongst the Race. In other
words Nature finds out the weak spots in Winter; and unless the
Weakling 1 makes himself a Strongling, a stern hand may be laid
upon his shoulder, and his weakened system go under.
Every Winter makes a heavy claim on those who are run down in
health, and consequently susceptible to pulmonary diseases. If you
are one of the weaklings or if you are in any way down in health at
this time of the year, secure the promptest means at your hands to
fortify yourself against the trials of Winter.
Who is it that is singled out for Rheumatism, Neuralgia, the
ravages of Consumption, the grip of Pneumonia, the wretchedness of
perpetual Chill ? Why, the person who leaves the wide door open for
disease to enter in; in other words the person who sets out on his
Winter Journey unequipped with the Shield of Health. To prepare
yourself you need to tone and brace the system with a good Tonic,
and there is not a better one known than-
CLEMENTS TONIC.
This wonderful Remedy and Restorative tones the nerves and
sets all the bodily functions working in perfect unison. Strength
comes to the muscles, vigour to the body, balance to the brain, rich-
ness to the blood. In, fact the glow of perfect health which keeps out
the cold and keeps up the energy is always followed after a course of
CLEMENTS TONIC.
Would you be forewarned to be forearmed? Then order some
CLEMENTS TONIC! to-day, and commence now to be a Strongling.
You are sure to reap great benefit from its use in all seasons.

Your Bowels
g
Here’s a picture of them. You have
over thirty feet of these in your body.
Just think. When you are constipated
it means that all the waste matter in these
thirty feet can’t get out. That’s why you
feel stuffed. That’s why you don’t have
an appetite. There’s no more room in-
side of you for food. But still you go on
eating, never stopping to think of the
complications which follow as you con-
tinue to force more food into your
stopped-up, overcrowded bowels. Can’t
you see how important it is to keep your
bowels in good working order ?
Nature intended the bowels to digest
your food and in order that they might
do this properly, nature has lined them
with hundreds of little cells which suck
up strength from the food as it passes
along. If your bowels are stopped; if
the food in them does not pass along, it
becomes stagnant, it decays, and instead
of giving up strength and nourishment,
it sends off poison. That’s when you
commence to belch. The decaying mat-
ter is sending off gas the same as any
other putrid matter does.
There is one way of making your bow-
els do their work as nature intended.
That way is Reuter’s Little Pills. They
stimulate and strengthen the bowel mus-
cles, and they do this in an entirely new
and different way from anything else.
Salts, cascara, jalap and the old fashion
pill simply blast out the bowel duct.
This means that the inside of the intes-
tines is wrecked. It means that all the
digestive juices which are so precious
are swept away. It means that the bow-
els are greatly weakened, and that you
will have to continue dosing yourself
oftener, and in greater quantities. Cas-
tor oil only greases the bowel duct. It
lets the matter slide through. It makes
the bowels more lazy and you find your-
self compelled to keep on taking the oil.
Reuter’s Little Pills act like exercise.
They act in a natural way. They build
up and restore the bowels to normal
strength.
This is a personal message to all those
who are afflicted with constipation and
its numerous accompanying troubles.
We want you to try Reuter’s Little Pills.
You can buy them at any chemist’s, and
a trial may mean lifelong happiness.
They are small and the dose can be in-
creased or diminished as occasion may
warrant by simply taking a greater or
lesser number of pills. We are spending
thousands of pounds to let the world
know about Reuter’s Little Pills and if
they were not all that we represent them
to be, we could not afford to spend this
vast amount. Prove it for yourself.

that your body is covered w.th pimples?

Watch or other prizes without any other conditions whatever. The diagram shows nine
squares, with the figure 5 occupying the centre square. The puzzle is to place a number
on each of the eight empty squares, so that they shall add up to 15 in a straight line
in as many ways as possible; no two squares may contain the same figure. There is
only one correct way of solving this puzzle, which, if you discover, entitles you to a
FREE Watch, and for the benefit of those who fail to send in the correct answer we
will publish the same in “ The Bulletin,” when the competition closes. Our decision is
to be final in every instance.

WARNER’S SAFE CURE
Why it cures disorders so seemingly different in character as
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Backache
Sciatica
indigestion
Ancemia
Blood Disorders
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
This is a fairly long list of disorders, but every one of them is caused by defective action of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER.
When the kidneys and liver are working perfectly, it is impossible for anyone to suffer from anj
of the disorders named. In order that this important fact may be realised, the following description
of the work performed by those vital organs is given :
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid,
together with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce.
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proportion
of the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous, and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and Bright’s Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition of
the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it
stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment," or if particles
float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy and no time must be lost
in adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more painful
illness will result.
The Liver.— ln the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood, converts
it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood gradually, as the
latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into urea, which is com-
pletely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived their life and are use-
ful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion, Biliousness,
Aiifemia, Sick Headache, and Blood Disorders.
The health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
the kidneys to be affected and the liver remain healthy, or vice versa.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research directed specially to diseases of the kidneys and
liver was rewarded b.y the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as Warner’s
Safe Cure. It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that it was necessary to find a
curative agent which would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver, these organs being so
immediately associated in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material, and after many dis-
appointments, the medicine which possessed the required action in the fullest degree was at length
discovered. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all diseases of the kidneys and liver, and bv
restoring their activity, these vital organs are enabled to rid the body, through the natural channels
of the urinary and biliary poisons, the presence of which, in the system, are the cause of Rheu-
matism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders, Ansemia, Indigestion
Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, Gravel, Stone, Bladder Troubles’and General Debility’
Warner’s Safe Cure cures all these disorders simply by removing the cause of the disorder. This is the
reason why cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent cures.
A treatise containing full particulars, and accounts of many remarkable cures effected even when
hope of recovery was despaired of, will be sent post free, by H. H. Warner and Co. Limited
