The Exile.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN the sullen summer days,
When the coal mirages quiver on the edges
of the plain,
When the tanks are molten silver, and the starved
earth cries for rain—
Ah ! the empty stretching years,
With their seedtime and their harvest—and the
calling of the sea.
And the haunting ghost of youth, and the love
that’s dead to me !
: Seven dreary years ago,
From my sea-girt home you brought me to the
Western plains of green,
And you made your heart a throne, where you
crowned me as a queen.
Ah ! the murmur of the sea,
And the sapphire of its waters —and the soul
paralysis
That has made your heart forget how once my
lips were sweet to kiss !
And my child whose wondering eyes
Never saw the blue waves play upon the stretch
of golden sand,
Or the white-sailed boats oome inward to the
shelter of the land ?
He is born to bondage too ;
For the red West sets its brand in the dullness of
his eyes.
And the West shall hold him slave till in Western
dust he lies.
But for many hundred years
Have the women of my blood watched the fishing
boats oome home.
{ln my dreams I see the long waves ever whiten-
ing to foam.)
But the red face of the plain,
With the dead trees at its edges, and the quiver-
ing summer haze—
Ah! the calling of the sea through the sullen
dragging days.
N.S.W.
R. BOULTON.
The Commercial and the Pig.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HOGAN eyed the clean-shaven Com-
mercial with evident disfavor, not to say
suspicion ; but that individual bore the
scrutiny with the assurance of his kind. To be
eyed with disfavor was to him neither unusual
nor disconcerting.
' Wherefore he continued to press his point.
“There’s absolutely nothing to stop yer,” he
said, authoritatively. “Put him in a little box
and stick him under the seat. They’ll never
know lie s there. Man alive, it’s done every day.”
Hogan’s face relaxed a trifle.
“It sounds roight enough,” he admitted,
thoughtfully; “ but Oi fill in wanst, an’ Oi’ll be
dahmned iv Oi do ut agin.”
The Commercial drained his glass and set it on
the counter with alarming vigor.
“Please yourself, ” he said brusquely, “but if
you re going to stay in Sydney over Christmas
with the family, you’ll want a sucking-pig, and
what could ye get better than this little chap ?
Tell y’ what,” he announced—then suddenly,
“ When are y’ going ? ”
“We’re dhroivin’ into town a’ Chewsday nixt,”
said Hogan, “ an’ catchin’ the thrain t’ Sydney a’
Winsday.”
The Commerced slapped the counter enthusias-
tically. All hardened Commercials suffer from
chronic enthusiasm.
“That’ll do tip top,” he stated. “I’ll take a
run down on Wednesday meself, and see y’ thro’.
Wanted to see the chief, anyhow.”
That settled it.
* * * a
Wednesday morning saw Hogan installed in a
first-class compartment along with the Samaritan
who was to see him through—also two other
veterans of the bag. The family was stowed away
in a ladies’ compartment; but under the seat be-
neath Hogan was a neat white box, wherein
slumbered, so Hogan said, “as foine a soocker as
y d see in a day’s march ” ; and in these matters
he was a person of much knowledge.
For the first hour or so he was a bit uneasy, but
the pig behaved like a perfect gentleman, and
Hogan felt secure.
At Parramatta, his friend got out and strolled
about the platform, “just to stretch me legs,” he
said ; but Hogan stayed with his charge—he was
taking no chances.
Half-a-moment before the train started the Com-
mercial dropped a remark to the busy station-
master, and then rushed for his compartment.
The stationmaster glanced at the clock.
“Too late now,” he muttered, “I’ll tele-
phone.” And he did.
At Sydney the usual platform crowd awaited
the train, but Hogan had grown bold in the ways
of deception. Yanking his box out from beneath
the seat, he turned to leave the carriage. The
tmket-exarniner, however, had his instructions.
“Let s have a look in that box,” he demanded,
curtly.
Hogan blustered.
mi °.^ e divil,” he began, stormily.
Ilie railway man was impatient.
‘ If y’ don’t let me look at it,” he said, threaten-
ingly. Hogan sighted an approaching helmet,
and handed it over.
The official upended the box, and an agonised
squeal split the air.
“ Righto,” copying the address into his
note-book. oil’ll have to pay for him now,
out you’ll hear from the Department in a day or
so.
It took Hogan a quarter of an hour to arrange
otherwise, and then he was a pound out of pocket.
Commercial was sympathetic.
* Well, anyhow, you’ve got him here now,” he
said ; ‘so you may as well see it through.”
Hogan thought so too.
After he left, the bagmen had sundry drinks.
I hey seemed much amused.
* * * *
Hogan continued to see it through for three
days. J hen the Inspector of Nuisances dropped
:n with an invitation “to be and appear,’’etc., etc.
Hogan was staggered.
He called the Inspector of Nuisances “adir’rty,
low blaggard,” and explained, with much vehem-
ence, that at Skeleton Greek, where he was tha
law, “ a mahn cud kape a bit iv a pig in his bid-
room iv he loiked, an’ th’ divil a worrud would
anny wan say to ’um. Anyhow,” he concluded
loftily, “ Oi’m a magistrate iv this Shtate mesilf,
an’ we’ll see phwat me contimp’ry has t’ say
about ut.”
His “ contimp’ry” said he would require to pay
one pound and costs, or change his residence for
a month. Hogan paid. Further, his contem-
porary advised him to get rid of the nuisance
without delay. Hogan did. He went Lome and
slew it in a scientific manner.
The Commercials attended the hearing; after
which they adjourned with that Good Samaritan,
Hogan’s Commercial, for refreshments. Their
amusement appeared to be intense. When a
letter arrived from Hogan, inviting the Samaritan
to dinner next evening, their amusement was
painful to witness.
* • * •
The nuisance looked perfection.
Hogan surveyed him proudly from the head of
the table.
“ He’s cost me a bit,” he said, confidentially, to
his guest, “but ’tis wurruth uts he is this minnit.
Moichael, go an’ see who’s at the dure.”
Michael went, and returned with a blue
envelope. Hogan opened it—and the surround-
ing air crackled for two and a-half minutes by the
Commercial's watch. It was a summons for
slaughtering hogs in the city.
Then he broke off suddenly.
“ It’s a dahm rum t’ing,” he muttered, half to
himself ; “ th’ poor little divil kep’ quoite enough
on th’ train, an’ only me an’ me fri’nd knew ; but
at the viry lasht minnit they foinds out.” (The
Samaritan was looking distinctly uneasy.) “Oi
brung um home in his box, an’ kep’ um in th’
house so as no wan would know, an’ yet they
foinds out.” (He was watching his guest out of
the corner of his eye.) “ An’ thin,” he went on,
“ whin Oi’m towlt t’ git rid o’ th’ noosinee, widout
delay, Oi comes sth.aight hoame an’ kills um in
th’ kitchen, wid niver a shquale ; but ” (he was
looking straight at the bagman now) —“ they foinds
out art th ’ sa-ame. An’ whoi ?”■
He rose in his chair and grasped the sucker
gracefully by the hind legs. “WHOI?” he re-
peated dramatically, whirling the corpse viciously
round his head.
The Commercial saw it coming—and ducked.
He got it on the top of the head.
Hogan side-stepped round the table, and the
bagman dived beneath it.
That saved him.
Hogan’s kick would have dispersed an army of
bagmen—if they’d stopped it; but the table was
a solid piece of furniture.
The individual who was responsible for the
blue envelope was letting himself out of the front
gate in a leisured manner, when he was propelled
into the gutter by a hurried and agitated Com-
mercial person, who proceeded to break all
records to the tram. Behind him came a lame
man with a sucking-pig.
Hogan turned the last corner in time to see his
quarry board a city-bound tram just as it resumed
its journey. \ Then he commenced to decorate the
atmosphere.
He was still decorating it when someone
gripped his arm.
“ What, in th’ na-ame o’ Hell ” he stormed.
The gripper wheeled him round with startling
energy.
“Come along,” lie commanded. “Charge of
bad language.”
And Hogan went.
SPINIFEX.
The Snake’s Breakfast.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE snake’s breakfast had been a heavy
one, a dejeuner a la fourchettc of seven
courses. He was a very globular and
torpid serpent indeed. It was nice and warm
where he lay amid the bracken—warm and very
quiet. His tongue flickered in and out in sheer
enjoyment—he was tasting that mouse again. He
turned a little to warm the other side, and, as he
turned, far down the valley he heard a great
trampling and many voices. *‘ Man !” he thought.
“I must get out of this.” But his torpor still
held him. The rustling of bracken and snapping
of dry twigs swept up toward him, intermixed
with little squeals of “ Fred, you are a tease. . . .
dim, stop pullin’ my hair.” “Evidently quite a
party,” thought the snake, alarmed. He must
really make an effort. But the breakfast was
strong as the spirit was willing. He torped some
more. J ust as the leading yahoo reached him he
made a feeble wriggle to escape. The yahoo
started back. “Keep orf!”he yelled. “A
snaike! A black ’un ! I near put me foot
on ’im.” The snake heard a cracking of
boughs; the footsteps in the bracken became
cautious; and the girls whispered. “ These asses,
said the snake to his soul, “will now proceed to
kill me because lam a snake'. It does not matter
that lam a carpet snake, and harmless. It docs
not matter that in my sinuous curves (when I’m
not full up of grub) I hold more of the decorative,
and consequently more justification for existence,
than these grotesque persons themselves. I die.
Well, I will die with a full belly—-that’s one
comfort.”
Then they battered him for a space with infant
gumtrees, and broke his back in fourteen places.
They prized his mouth open to show the girls his
“sting.” They did not find it. An eye was
blackened over the vexed question of liis
venomousness. A nose bled freely over his precise
length—the length of a snake was never satis-
factory. This particular snake was declared at
12 feet.
They dragged him 2i miles by his tail, since one
of them desired to take him home and skin him.
Out of the skin would emerge belts, purses, carry-
alls. Then, after having eaten their lunch and
being stung by bull-ants in accordance with the
best traditions of picnics, they dragged him
another miles to the station. This time he
was dragged head-first, by means of a piece of
string round his neck. This treatment mad,e him
nice and supple. The day was hot, and this made
his semi-digested breakfast supple too. Never
was a more supple snake dragged on a string or
hung around a shrieking girl’s neck.
Upon the railway platform, ’Enery, who had the
reputation of much bushcraft (he had once been lost
for 36 hours in the Kanimbla Valley), took charge
of the defunct serpent. Wonder had not yet ceased
over the reptile. The'party stood about it. and
criticised, and lied, and made pointed, subtle japes,
that fell like an elephant’s foot, and made even
the girl from Wolloomooloo blush. ’Enery ob-
served a kerosene tin standing inoffensively upon
the platform. “ Watch me crack ’im like a stock-
whip, ho said. Thev watched. He swung the
snake by the tail thrice round his head, and
cracked it at the tin. As he did so its breakfast
shot out and distributed itself. It took them
quite a while to remove the traces of lizards,
frogs, grasshoppers and beetles from the girls’
white dresses. Then the boys belted ’Enery with
the snake, and the girls leaned over the platform
railing. The kerosene tin stood in dignified
silence. The train came in.
HOWARD ASHTON.
“ Itan : Squatter Keys, Upper Hunter
(IS.S.W.), drafts his cattle into paddocks accord-*
mg 1 to color, and submits them for sale so classified
—whites, or roans, or reds, or brindles. Another
squatter in the same district has reduced the
breeding of black sheep to a science, and has a
mob of 3000 of them. Last they ave-
raged more wool than the whites, and it realised
13d per lb. The lambing ran out at 82 per cent.,
without a piebald or a white. This success dis-
putes the theory that the black jumbuck is a
sport.

The Old Salt.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN over rock and river flat
The tide inrolling ran,
Upon his cottage doorstep sat
The old seafaring man.
His face was tanned by sun and wind,
And wrinkled like the sea
When, with a two-kmot gale behind,
Its waves run furiously.
A grand old mariner he seemed
Of gallant days long dead,
And still like battle-lanterns gleamed
The eyes within his head.
His hands lay idly on his knees—
His great hands brown and stark.
That held the wheel when raging seas
Swept madly o’er his bark.
All over now was that grim strife.
Safe anchored to the shore,
He’d struggle with the storm for life
No more —ah, nevermore !
Yet when the flowing moonlight shone
Upon the flowing tide,
His thoughts were with the brave days gone —
His days of strength and pride.
And oft he’d sigh, the while his hand
Would grimly clenched be :
“ My soul is weary of the land ;
Would I were on the sea ! ”
For ever through his ancient blood,
When winds began to blow.
The currents of the salt sea flood
Went pulsing to and fro.
And he could many wondrous tales
And stories strange relate,
Of raging seas, and roaring gales,
And storms intemperate.
“ I mind,” he said to me one night,
As we sat side by side,
A-smoking in the moonshine white,
And gazing on the tide.
“I mind a voyage, long years back,
Th&wust I ever had ;
The seas was white, the skies was black,
The wind, it blew like mad.
“ Our passengers, they all, good Lord !
With fright was green and blue,
And we had sev’ral pigs aboard,
And cows and ’osses too.
“ But though the breakers, close at hand,
Was roarin’ fierce and hoarse,
Me eye was on the compass, and
I kep’ her to her course.
“ I dug me teeth into me lip—
The mark you yet may spy—
‘ We’ll land upon the blanky slip
In spite of H —l,’ sez I.
“ A parson’s wife close by me sat,
And mighty stiff sez she :
‘ No man should cuss and damn like that,
Espesherly at sea. ’
“ The wind blew off the beam. We ran ”
I stopped him then and there :
“ Why didn’t you, a sailor-man,
Lay-to, or tack, or wear?”
A scornful glance on me he cast,
And said, in tones of ire, •
“ Why wasn’t we a-hangin’ fast
On to the cussed wire ? ”
Then on he went : “I licked the squall,
And landed safe the crew,
The passengers, and pigs, and all,
And cows, and ’osses, too.”
“ Then what were you, who bore at sea
That awful tempest’s brunt ? ”
“ I wus the captain, sir,” said he,
“ Of the Middle Harbor punt.”
N.S.W.
CREEVE ROE.
The Tongans.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 •
student of human nature can find
I much material, of a kind, in Tonga.
If he goes to a “ bolotu” (church service)
at night, when they tell their souls” (testify),
he will see all the women, from those verging on
. ~ m j-j •. , , J
scragginess to the old and decrepit, stand up and
tell in hot Salv’army language how they were the
vilest of sinners ; how they realised their lost
condition; how they. thought themselves far
beyond pardon and worthy only of the most
awful tortures of the “ pit.” The whole meeting
is moved to deep distress. Those who possess
hankies weep copiously. Those who have had
no chance to lift a hanky off a clothes-line,
or who have at home no bundles of white men’s
washing from which .to take one, suppress their
tears, and encourage the speaker with such re-
marks as “Mo one!” (that is so), or “Taka-
fatai!” (God be thanked !). It is almost unneces-
sary to state that the younger damsels do not
“ tell their souls.” They are generally too busy
preparing genuine material for their orations
when the time comes for them to join the has-
beens.
Some years ago there was a celebrated Tongan
beautv named Mele (Mary). “ When Mele told
her soul,” is an episode in the history of the island
to this day. For a long time Mele was the most
popular and sought-after belle in Tonga. But
when the charm of youth began to leave her,
younger rivals eclipsed her, and she quite natur-
ally commenced to think about her spiritual condi-
tion Mele went to a “bolotu” and “told her soul,”
and’great was the telling thereof. About 300
people were present. Mele had resolved to put
up a record. She had a hanky, so she wept freely.
She was not to be satisfied with the usual vague
hints at a sinful, mis-spent life. She used the
customary generalities as a mere prologue. Then
she launched out into full details. “0 Lord, you
know Bulo Oteli,” etc. Bulo, who was present,
was manifestly uncomfortable and embarrassed,
even before some secret foe wailed out, “ Mo one !
mo one!”
“ And, O Lord, you know when Sipo s wife
was away,” etc. “Oeawe!” (alas!) wailed out
other wives who had also been away, while. Sipo
was seen hurrying out into the night. The male
part of the congregation, with wild, expectant
looks on its face, was rapidly but unobtrusively
following Sipo’s example. When Mele had got
half through the task of “telling her soul ” there
was not a male in the room except those forcibly
detained by their wives. When she had finished,
discord reigned in every family circle in the
village, icy coldness sprang up between engaged
couples, the purity of descent of some of the
noblest families was marred, and' almost every
male of mature age in the village was involved in
the awful confession.
The Tongans are a deeply religious people, but
as liars they cannot be equalled. Old Ananias
could not hold a candle to aTongan in good going
order. They are handsomer than the Maoris, but
not so intelligent. The women walk like the
duchesses we read of; they value “good name,’
but nature, climate, &c., are too strong for them,
so much “ telling of soul ” awaits them when they
are so old that sin has left them behind.
Politics are in their infancy in Tonga. Parlia-
ment sits once in three years. Last election the
Opposition secured a fair majority. But their
“big gun” was 9s. in arrears with his taxes, so
he was not allowed to take the oath at swearing-
in time. He rushed off to the king, borrowed the
amount due, ran to the Treasury, paid it in, got a
receipt, streaked back, and found the business of
the House in full swing, and sundry new laws
passed which practically disqualified the Opposi-
tion.
A strong force in the Government party was
Kaho, Minister of Police. If any member of the
Opposition got on dangerous ground in his speech,
Kaho would rise and say, “ Sit down, or I’ll put
you in gaol! ” The Oppositionist sat. Towards
the end of the three weeks’ session, Kaho had only
to look at a member of the Opposition about to
rise, and that member’s eloquence died within
him, and he subsided.
One night, during the session, the King was
entertaining at his palace a party consisting of
the governors of neighboring islands, and some
big chiefs. Liquor was flowing freely. Now,
no Tongan is permitted to. look at the
giddy bowl without a “ tohi,” which is a written
permit to drink spirituous and fermented liquors.
Kaho knew that some of the King’s guests had no
“tohi,” so he assembled his faithful “cops” and
surrounded the palace. He tried the door, but it
was locked. He ordered his “peelers” to climb
the verandah poles, and from the balcony get into
the banquet-hall, which was an upstairs room.
But the cops’ courage oozed out at the thought of
entering his Majesty’s palace in that manner, so
Kaho climbed up alone. The illegal drinkers
scuttled away and hid in all kinds of nooks and
corners, but Kaho ferreted them out and had
them duly fined next day. The King protested a
good deai, but a Tongan King doesn’t amount to
much in the presence of a man like Kaho—embit-
tered, too, by the knowledge that the monarch
had lent 9s. to the Opposition.
The Tongans are the most enthusiastic cricketers
on this globe. When the game was first intro-
duced in Tonga, everybody played from morning
till night. When one village challenged another
to a match, it was not a game between elevens—
every man in both villages batted. At last the
plantations were so much neglected that an Act
was passed, declaring cricket to be illegal except
on Mondays and Thursdays.
Lest tourists, reading these things, rush in un-
due numerousness to Tonga, it must be stated
that it is only a very small land, where a man
like George Reid couldn’t hide his identity for
one hour out of the twenty-four. So let the
frisky tourist still label his portsammy for the
land of the Monkey, and leave poor harmless
Tonga in peace.
WANGA.
The Price of a Soul.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE soul of Smith rapped gently with the
handle of its umbrella on the door marked
“ Manager.”
“Come in,” said a voice.
The soul pushed the door open and went in on
tip-toe.
A little gray man, very shabbily dressed and
wearing spectacles, looked up as it entered, and
pointed at a worn Vienna chair.
“Well, what is it ?” he inquired in an irritable
voice.
The shade of Smith cleared its throat and
shuffled its feet. Then it fumbled with its hat
and umbrella, and said : “I beg your pardon—is
the Devil in ? ”
The worried little man looked keenly over the
top of his spectacles. “Yes,” he answered, “I
think I’m in !”
Smith’s umbrella fell with a crash on the floor.
“ What do you want ? ”
Smith opened his mouth twice and shut it again
without saying a word. At the third attempt he
managed to gasp, “I want ... I want. . . sell
. . . my soul ! ”
The Devil jumped up and fumbled insanely
with his spectacles.
“ D’ye mean it? You’re not joking, are you ?”
“No—no !”
“You really will sell ? ”
“Yes—"-yes!”
“ How much do you want ? Say quickly! ”
Smith clutched his hat and umbrella to his
bosom and shivered.
“No,” he moaned, “ you must make me an
offer ! Make it large—large ! Give me every
penny you can —I’m desperate —must sell ! ”
The Devil, trembling with excitement, seized
a bell and rang it violently until the outer door
was heard to slam. Then he waved his hand at
Smith and muttered, “Sit there ! I’ll look up
your record, and make an estimate. Don’t move!”
Smith shivered and groaned, and the Devil
rushed through a doorway into an inner office.
“ How much ! ” whispered Smith to himself as
he sat huddled on the chair. “ Five million—ten
—twenty ? No! I must have fifty million in gold
and eighty years of renewed life, and—oh ! what
else can I ask ?... Yes ; I’ll stipulate for wisdom,
so as I can beat him in the end ... beat him !...
beat him !... Ah-h-h ! ”
The Devil burst in and slammed something on
the desk.
Smith stared, howled dismally, and leaped for
the door.
The Devil, with a yell of disappointment,
snatched up the half-crown, and flung it after him.
